UDER THE GUMTREE
A Warm Woman.
DOWN at Man-o’-War
Steps the other day a
boat load of clean, trim,
lusty tars had just
landed from one of the British
squadron in Farm Cove, and a
sailor’s lass was walking off
with her man. Each had an
arm round the other’s neck,
and a passing pattern of female
precision paused a moment to
lift a disgusted chin and throw a
withering glance at the amorous
couple. The girl caught it and
swung round—one arm on her
hip, the other still about her
lover’s neck. “Don’t you wish
you had him yourself ? ” she said.
That is a coarse parallel
for the amusing feminine feud
between Gertrude Atherton and Alice Mey-
nell ; but it states the trade jealousy, the
{physical rivalry underlying the feud. Mrs.
Meynell is an English writer of poems and essays
—poems with some value of style and emotion,
essays pruned and polished with a precious
art that kills the life of them. Mrs. Ather-
ton is an American writer of novels about
the elemental woman striving upward the
woman who wants a man with a big arm, a big
heart, and a big brain to match her instincts and
aspirations. She has no style at all, but she pulses
with life and her revelations are capital reading.
And she assails herad versary vi et armis, tongue and
claws—an adversary too dignified to retort, calm
in superiority, yet possibly—one wonders - qualify-
ing her pride with qualms? Is hers the fuller
life, the superior function ?
Gertrude Atherton lives, anyway : she rejoices
in her emotions; denies the ascetic virtues;
admits her physical basis, even glories in it. No
colourless sublimities of art or conduct for her, if
—as can hardly be doubted —her heroines sincerely
represent her. And all a man’s sympathies go
with her. Mrs. Meynell’s literary sculpture
may outlast Mrs. Atherton’s crude literary
paintings; Mrs. Meynell’s repose is more im-
pressive than Mrs. Atherton’s fury of action;
but at any rate this is thrilling and moving, this is
a woman alive. In refusing to segregate her im-
pulses—such fqr myself, such for my family, such
for my books —she betrays her sex’s conventions,
is distinctly ! * unladylike” and femininely un-
literary ; —but she is infinitely more interesting.
Even if, according to the word attributed to one
of her rivals, her avowals make her a common
woman of literature—she is patently a Woman.
And, after all, can a woman be more or better ?
It is settled, of course, that no woman, no
feminine woman, can be a writer of fiction on the
high plane: the apparent exceptions—“ George
Eliot,” “George Sand,’’ Charlotte Bronte and
others—had the physical essence and inind of a
man. Though fiction has not the breadth of history
or the depth of philosophy, at its best it demands
something of both qualities, and the mind of a
typical woman is neither very broad nor very
deep, so she must content herself with being
recorder and suggester rather than creator.
Poetry is another matter. As philosophy, at one
end of the mental gamut, calls chiefly for intellect,
poetry, at the other end, calls chiefly for emotion.
Its essence is passionate emotion, and woman is
characteristically the vessel of passionate emotion.
An overplus of intellect spoils poetry as it spoils a
woman. S > it is that, across the ages, we still
thrill to the passion of Sappho—and the crowd of
novels by modern women are the merest ephe-
merae.
Gertrude Atherton is too much of a woman to
escape from the disabilities of her sex, and her
clever stories are of only momentary interest.
But, their limitations granted, it is possible to
praise highly the results of her talent. She has her-
self the conjunction attributed to her heroines—
elemental passion and a shrewd modern brain; and
her books are among the best being written by
women to-day. “ Patience Sparhawk and Her
Times ” is chief of them, and in it Mrs. Atherton
has probably reached her climax. “ Los Cerritos ”
and “American Wives and English Husbands”
are similar in kind, but not as forceful; and
the shortness of “ His Eortunate Grace ” puts
it out of the comparison. A short story may
yield as intense an impression as a long one of
equal merit; but it cannot give the same weight
of impression. The pebble from David’s sling
slew Goliath as effectually as an avalanche
could have slain him, and maybe more artistic-
ally ; but Goliath, in his last moment of conscious-
ness, would have been much more impressed by
the avalanche.
Mrs. Atherton has been a prolific writer: her
husband is dead : one does not hear of children—
maybe her books take the place of them. She is
a great-grand-niece of old Ben Franklin, and
proud of it. It is not necessary, in order to keep
an impression of her, to have read “ Hermia
Suydam” or “The Doomswoman, ” or other of
her early novels—any more than it is necessary to
plod through her dozen or more books to get an
impression of the local “Ada Cambridge”: she
is all of a piece, she cannot say more or do better
than she says and does in “Patience Sparhawk.”
There is the interest of a new plot, the interest of
pursuing the old ideas through new phases and
expressions, to make “ The Californians ” and
“A Daughter of the Vine ” worth looking at; but
they add nothing essential to the author’s statement
of herself. You can score them for development,
but not for character —the settled character of a
woman who wants emotions, all the emotions of
which she is capable, emotions of sex, sense,
spirit, every attribute of her organism—admitting
and using hereditary accumulations while striving
for the utmost satisfaction of her individual
capacities and potencies. Here’s a full-freighted
galley— vogue lagalerc!
Mrs. Atherton is a fine woman: with a little
,‘nore knowledge and restraint she would be a fine
writer. Gifted with wit, full of fancy and
occasionally flashing phrases, but lacking humour,
and with proper feminine love of extravagance,
craving the too-much, she founders in the quick-
sands of melodrama, frequently splits on the rock
of anti-climax. Her novels have epic force
without the epic form and the olinohing
epio inevitability. _ Patience Sparhawk’s de-
liverance in the nick of time is as jarring
as the theatrical hero’s death in the preoise contr©
of the stage, in the precise centre of the circle of
limelight. The unnecessary people in “Los Cer-
ritos ” are wiped out by a stampede of cattle with
the generous suddenness of a god descending from
a machine. Perhaps “The Californians,” con-
sidered as an episodical novel of character, is
best constructed of Mrs. Atherton’s books ; and
the tragedy of Don Roberto’s end is really tragic.
But his suicide per the American flag is on the
border-line of melodrama : a note of exclamation
would have thrown it over.
To what profit cavils : let us take the good the
goddesses provide.

Bookfellow’s Competitions.
VII.-WANTED—A TRIOLET.
Closed Sept. 9. Several hundred triolets and
other things were received—and not one good.
The triolet only looks' easy. A selection of toler-
able attempts is printed herewith ; and some more
will be given latex-. Many competitors disqualified
themselves by omitting the Australian reference ;
and very few interwove the last lines so as to get
sornething better than repetition. D. O’R.’s
“ Flannel Flower ” might have taken the prize had
it been a triolet: it isn’t. F.M.’s “Modernity and
a Maid ” would have been very seriously con-
sidered if it had local colouring; it hasn’t. So
competition was gradually lessened to “Nil’s”
“Greeting”—which is technically the better ;and
Phil. F.’s “ Bushman ’’—which has more force and
interest; and as force and interest were put first
“ The Bushman ” bears the bell.
THE BUSHMAN.
Here in the out-back wilds of grey,
Gone to the dogs long years ago !
What was the cause 'l Ah ! who can say,
Here in the out-back wilds of grey.
Sighing by night, cursing by day,
Smoking the smart from, years of woe,
Here in the out-back wilds of grey —
Gone to the dogs long years ago !
PHIL. F.
AUSTRALIAN PICTURES.
CATTLE BELLS.
Running, chiming, clinking, tolling,
Jingling, ringing cattle-bells,
Loud in rhythmic cadence knolling—
Running, chiming, clinking, tolling—
From the ranges outward rolling,
How your peal on peal upsweils !
Running, chiming, clinking, tolling,
Jingling, ringing cattle-bells 1
DROVING DAYS.
For the merry droving days
That come in with the rounding year,
Pack the saddle, and then raise—
For the merry droving days
And full mobs on all road-ways—
__ A clinking glass, a rousing cheer !
For the merry droving days
That come in with the rounding year I
THE AUSTRALIAN OWL.
Mournful, forlorn,
Mopoke! Mopokel
All night till morn,
Mournful, forlorn,
Faintly far borne
Through grey she-oak.
Mournful, forlorn,
Mopoke ! Mopoke!
DAWN ON THE DOWNS.
Noiselessly the grey-white sheep
Leave the silent camp,
Though the day is still asleep—
Noiselessly the grey-white sheep
Out across the dim plain creep
Cropping herbage damp.
Noiselessly the grey-white sheep
Leave the silent camp.
CURLEW CALLING.
Wild and eerily from the swamp—
Curlew 1
Weird and piercing near the camp,
Wild and eerily from the swamp,
Wailing up the wind, mist-damp,
Thrilling through and through ;
Wild and eerily from the swamp—
Curlew I
CAMP-FIRE ASHES.
Ashes, silvery-grey !
And burnt-out scattered brands 1
Some traveller betray,
Ashes, silvery-grey.
Far from the beaten way
In far out-back bad lands—
Ashes, silvery-grey,
And burnt-out scattered brands 1
MIRAGE.
Far across the dreary plain
A sea, white-sanded on the marge ;
Dimly shadowy’t will wane
Far across the dreary plain.
Shines the shadow sea again—
The dreamy-distant blue mix-age l
Far across the dreary plain
A sea, white-sanded on the marge.
PHIL. F.
AUSTRALIA.
Though her face is austere,
Yet her heart is of gold !
Lovers fond hasten near,
Though her face is austere;
Haggai’d flats, rivers drear
Hidden treasures enfold . . ,
Though her face is austere,
Yet her heart is of gold !
G. C.
O, NOBLE!
O, noble, sphere-propelling youth
Whose battle-cry, “Advance Australia ! ”
Has flashed around earth’s globe—(the truth
O, noble sphere !) —propelling Youth
To “ go for ” cricket nail and tooth,
And practise bowling inter alia.
O, Noble! sphere-propelling youth,
Whose bat ’ll cry “ Advance Australia! ”
W.B.R.
AUSTRALIA.
Land of the scarce-gliding stream,
Yet dearer than all to me !
Wild lights on thy weird plains gleam,
Land of the scarce-gliding stream !
Half nightmare was Nature’s dream
When she fashioned thee, sand and tree,
Land of the scarce-gliding stream,
Yet dearer than all to me 1
ULOOLA.
KOOKABURRA.
“ Who’s that up above ? ”
Ha, ha, ha ! ho 1 ho !
“ Stop it! there’s a love:
Who ’s that up above ?
I’ll soon make you move,
There now will you go ?
“ Who’s that ? ” (Up above :
Ha, ha, ha! ho! ho!)
AMB. G.
MAGGIE.
Piping crow with fluting note,
Mellowing the merry morn,
Down your glib and greedy throat,
Piping crow with fluting note,
Glide the grubs : your table d'hdte
Fills me (Grub-street bard) with scorn—
Piping crow with fluting note
Mellowing the merry morn !
D.R.
FISH AND FOWL.
A book, a pipe, and a shade of gums,
Beside the creek where the mullet thrive ;
A sand-worm bait and a throw of crumbs:
(A book, a pipe, and a shade of gums).
The day drones on, while the brown bee hums
And the bright bream flash where the black shags
dive —
A book, a pipe, axxd a shade of gums,
Beside the creek where the mullet thrive.
D.R.
WATTLE BOUGHS.
Wattle boughs are gay with gold—
Summer-time is coming!
Winter has his bluey rolled ;
Wattle boughs are gay with gold.
Bee-bushrangers free and bold
Raid the treasure, humming
“ Wattle boughs are gay with gold !
Summer-time is coming ! ”
ZADIG.
A TOAST.
Here’s a health to the anti-Federal fool!
May the devil take him who refuses !
Ere the Dawn disperses the “anti” school
Here’s a health to the anti-Federal fool
In a flagon of verjuice, keen and cool !
Let us drink to the player who loses !
So a health to the anti-Eederal fool—
May the devil take him ! Who refuses ?
NIL.
GREETING!
To greet you, sweet serene-eyed Spring,
O, let me try ! A triolet
Were such a very fitting thing
To greet you, sweet serene-eyed Spring !
While bush and bank and briar bx-ing
The wattle, rose, and violet
To greet you, sweet serene-eyed Spring,
0, let me try a triolet!
NIL.
FLANNEL-FLOWER.
O the mystery
Of the Flannel Flower
You have given me !
—O the mystery
Of the golden hour
When two are one—and three ;
—O the mystery
Of the Flannel Flower !
D.O'R.
WESTWARD LONGING.
West beyond the ranges are our dreamy hearts
enfolden,
Who have heard the gum-leaves singing to the
sleeping stars all day,
For the West winds bring the cadence of a won-
drous lyric olden;
West beyond the ranges are our dreamy hearts en-
folden
Where the buds are on the sandal and the wattle’s
breaking golden,
Brighter in its glory than a poet’s song can say;
West beyond the ranges are our dreamy hearts
enfolden
Who have heard the gum-leaves singing to the
sleeping stars all day.
W.H.O.
MODERNITY AND A MAID.
A kiss is something short and sweet,
Which may be lengthened so discreetly
That love shall mock the prude’s conceit,
“A kiss is something short.” And, Sweet—
(Expericntia docet) —fleet
Is youth ; let’s test the thing completely :
A kiss is something short and sweet
Which may be lengthened— so / . . discreetly

REQUIESCAT.
Sound a cheerful knell--
Linger on each stroke of it!
Anti-Bill is . . . well,
Sound a cheerful knell.
Let glad anthems swell
(He won’t see the joke of it) —
Sound a cheerful knell !
Linger on each stroke of it!

CAULFIELD.
Take a tip for the Cup
(Mind, my money is on !)
When the numbers are up

VIA.
DOUBLE DEALINGS.
Were we going for “ the double ”
With the old horse at Menindie ?
That, you see, was their great trouble—
Were we going for the double ?
It would burst their local bubble . . .
When he won—Lord, what a shindy!
Were we going for the double ! !
With the old horse at Menindie.
Oz.
IN A CLEARING, TASMANIA.
Gum trees, dreary, grim, and gaunt,
Lifeless, ring-barked years ago !
Surely secret sorrows haunt
Gum trees, dreary, grim, and gaunt.
Do you envy trees that flaunt
Leaf and flower, aloft, alow,
Gum trees, dreary, grim, and gaunt,
Lifeless, ring-barked years ago?
R.H.D.
SUNSET.
Those times are past, those days are dead—
We ’ve seen the sun go down ;
Love’s garden grows no roses red,
Those times are past, those days are dead;
No flowerets gleam upon your head,
No wattles in your gown—
Those times are past, those days are dead—
We’ve seen the sun go down.
A.D.
SYDNEY HARBOUR.
O thou big blue wayward tear,
Brimming o’er the eye of Beauty!
Thy capricious moods endear,
O thou big blue wayward tear !
Peerless in the hemisphere,
Object of exultant duty,
0 thou big blue wayward tear
Brimming o’er the eye of Beauty !
W.M.W
Bookfellow’s Comipetitions.
IX.—WANTED -A SPEECH.
Sydney undertakers picnicked the other day ;
and the speeches, sad to say! were all sermons.
Now, an undertaker is obviously a jovial soul, and
if he had imagination lie would make a jovial
speech. The Bookfcllow wants that speech. You
are the chief undertaker at the picnic; you are
naturally bright and humorous; you are jolly
with good living, light hearse-exercise, and the
cheerful atmosphere of tombs ; and the eyes of
all the other undertakers and the mutes are
fixed upon you ;—you call upon them to
charge their glasses, and you fill your
own; they boat upon the table encouragingly
and the caterer’s dog is hished away ; —then you
rise in a hush of joyful expectation broken by a
ripple of anticipatory laughter, and propose
Success to Undertaking !
What do you say ? in not more than 500 words,
packed with wit and sepulchral wisdom—by
Oct. 14.
The best speech earns 10s. fid., plus space rates.
“The Popular Omar!” “Ignotus” is quite
right about Fitzgerald’s alleged translation.
Writing, at I do, with a competent knowledge of
the language and literature of Persia, 1 can say
that the hook has more Fitzgerald than “ Ann-
JE&aiam” in it. (My spelling of the name is the
correct one, according to Hunter’s and Fortv-s’s
system.) An Ismaili mystic of the name lived,
and wrote verses. Every educated Persian writes
verses. The language lends itself more readily
than any other on earth to expression in
metre. It would be easy in Isfahan to
find a couple of hundred Mirzas who would write
quatrains better than the originals of those in
Fitzgerald’s book at sixpence a-piece ; and I will
teach you the trick when you know the language
well enough. Such men are to be found, too, in
Afghanistan—especially in Herat and Kabul,
where most of the population speak Per-
sian—and in many Indian cities. No trans-
lation can really convey the true sense of
the original. It is simply the translator’s inter-
pretation, and its value depends on his capacity
for interpreting another man's utterances. Fitz-
gerald makes his author talk like a European
instead of like the Muslim sectary he was. There
is not a particle of real local colour in the verses.
If there were, they would not be so popular with
British and Australian Philistines who like
Kipling’s “Barrack-room Ballads.”
DAVID G.

WHAT THE DOCTORS SAY
«Woolloston House, Castlemaine, Nov. 30, 1897. My
dear Mr. Malyon,—l have used your ointment Ligmolliene
with distinct success in my own case, but with greater as
an emollient unguent for the face of a young lady suffer-
ing from ‘ Acne,’ very common during puberty, and you
should push it in that direction. Mr. Best, solicitor,
speaks most highly of it in his own bad case of Eczema.
Kindly send me another box of it..—Yours truly, GEO. D.
WOOLLEY, M.D.”
What the Hon. W. H. EMBLING, M.D., M.L.C., says :
“Melbourne, May 27, 1899. Mr. W. H. Malyon.—Dear
Sir,—l have been using your Ligmolliene Ointment for

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Australia.
A lone old maid, amid the solitude
Of dreaming seas you were condemned to wait
Through oountless ages for the gracious mood
Of scornful and inexorable Fate.
Yearning in vain you grew to womanhood,
While barren years passed by, tans love, sans
hate.
In north and east and west the brides were wooed:
To you the white man’s love came late —so late !
Your cheeks are hollow, and your eyes are dim
With pale-blue emptiness of dead despair;
Long summers dried your suppleness of limb ;
Your face is sad, your voice is mute, your hair
Is bleached with age, and life is dull and grim—
Poor faded maid, whose springtime none would
share 1
Q.
VON KOTZE.
Man and Woman.
[According to Maori mythology, the god Tiki created
Man by taking a piece ot clay and moistening it with his
own blood. Woman was the offspring of a sunbeam and
a sylvan echo.]
Thus God made Man to cope with destiny :
Taking the common clay, God moistened it
With His red blood ; and so for ever lit
That sombre grossness with divinity.
So man for ever finds him in the mesh
Of clogging earth ; and though divine hopes thrill
And flush his leaping heart, it faints, for still
His dreams are pinioned in the gyves of flesh.
Yet ever God’s blood in him courses free,
And, penetrated with eternal hope,
Up Evolution’s long, uneven slope
Man lifts him from his sodden ancestry.
And though his eyes the far goal cannot see,
And half the terrors of the dark he knows,
Yet with an inward fire his courage glows;
He bears the torch of immortality.
But Woman from a memory had birth,
Into the forest’s dignity of shade
A sudden sunbeam groped—a soft hand laid
In silent benediction on the earth.
Then filtered through the green a song forlorn
Of some forgotten bird. Lo ! in a mist
Of love the sunbeam and the echo kissed,
And Woman —sunlit memory—was born !
So light and melody to her belong—
The sunlight in the dying echo blurred !
So woman came—a vision and a word
From the Unknown —a sunbeam and a song i
So ever through the forest of the years
Shall Man pursue and still pursue the gleam
That wavers and is gone; and through his dream
The fainting echo of a song he hears.
And when at last his weary feet are led
Into the sacred glade, and she stands there,
He takes her close —all song and sunlit hair ;
The gleam has faded and the song has fled !
And though with blinded eyes he cannot see,
She haunts him like a word that he knows not-
That is not quite remembered, nor forgot—
Some thought that hovers near a memory.
As out from Heaven she leans, on earth there falls
The sunbeam of her hair, golden and fine ;
And drops an echo of a voice divine —
A voice that ever vainly calls and calls !
And though she spill a splendour and a fire
Upon the dark, her glory is unknown ;
Behind the screen of sex she dwells alone ;
She cannot come as close as her desire.
So ever like a pale moon drowned in misto
Her face is vague—a barrier intervenes ;
And ever from her loneliness she leans,
With waiting eyes, all-wistful to be kissed !
N.S.W.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
To Nance at Noon.
Old Pan and all the nymphs are here,
For this is Passion’s flowery way ;
Step easily, and do not fear —
No stupid scruple suits the day.
You must not mind what “people say ” :
People are fools, and youth is fleet ;
Live while you live, though friends gainsay
Love leads us by the hand, my Sweet !
Love is the one thing stable, dear,
While earth grows old and creeds decay;
You’re Cupid’s mistress, Psyche’s peer ;
No stupid scruple suits the day.
Let’s live and love, while live we may,
While violets spring about our feet,
What time, in exquisite array,
Love leads us by the hand, my Sweet 1
Now, ere the roses disappear
And grim Death takes our joys away,
While Paphian music soothes the ear,
No stupid scruple suits the day.
The night draws on. No longer stay
Bemused by ethics of the street.
Come! join the Graces at their play—
Love leads us by the hand, my Sweet!
Nance! Yield your lips. No more delay ;
Nj stupid scruple suits the day.
Soft voices call from Venus’ seat . . . \j
Love leads us by the hand, my Sweet!
v.

At a recent Maoriland Prohibition Con-
vention, a member rushed excitedly into the room
and melodramatically handed an open telegram to
the chairman. The latter read from it an en-
couraging announcement re the Clutha prohibi-
tion district. Then a non-prohibitionist present
asked to see it. It was reluctantly passed to him,
and after a long struggle with the half-obliterated
date, he found it to be only four years old 1

WHAT IS CATARRH ?
/CATARRH is inflammation of the
lining membrane of the nose
and adjoining passages. If this in-
flammation is not arrested it invades
the passages which lead from the
nose to the head, ears, throat
and lungs. It injures the sight and
hearing, destroys the sense of taste
and smell, renders the breath offen-
sive, breaks down the affected
tissues, consumes the nasal car-
tileges, and rots away the small

British Medical Journal.
INVALIDS
AND
THE ACED.
A well-known Australian bishop, \vho
grieves that he never experienced tile blessings of
paternity, recently related his woes to the leading
local medical mam A day or two later the medico
wan uallod i In to Bee the prelate (confined to bed
by bronchial trouble), and, as he entered the room,
burst into laughter that astonished the bishop and
his anxious spouse. The spluttering doctor would
give no answer to enquiries until the old lady had
left the room. Then he pointed to a Bible- text
card hanging on the wall: “ Suffer little ohildren
to come unto Me.”
Premier Turner has been telling a some-
what obscure interviewer about how he was
dragged forward at every stage of his public
career. So far as the measure of his greatness ex-
tends, he embodies the paradox of having achieved
it, and yet of having it thrust upon hnn. This
means that he was more than equal to every posi-
tion into which circumstances forced him. A ears
ago he was simply a hard-working attorney, with
Sam Lyons as his senior partner, and had no
thought of public life in any form. His St. Kilda
residence was about half-way between two railway
stations. It was his custom to take the train at
a certain one of these, but one morning Fate im-
pelled him to take the other. Three of his ac-
quaintances, in comparatively humble life, were
ohatting there, and one said, “ Here’s our man for
Town Councillor.” A vacancy had just occurred.
Turner protested that business forbade,
but was persuaded, and got elected. Two
years later he was Mayor. Then came a new
division of the Parliamentary electorates, cutting
St. Hilda off Prahran. Turner’s friends insisted
that this was built for him, displacing nobody. So
he was elected. The next step was Premier
Munro nominating him for Commissioner of Cus-
toms. Turner jibbed again, pointing out the
superior claims of Peacock and Stuart, who were
in the Cabinet without portfolios. Munro made
him take it. Then Munro went to London as
Agent-General, leaving Shiels at the head of the
Cabinet. Patterson bowled it over. As the weak-
ness of the Patterson team became evident, and
Shiels was sick, adeputation turned up at Lyons and
Turner’s office with a demand for Turner to lead
the Opposition. Turner would have refused but
for the accident of Lyons beingin the office (Lyons
did all the outside work). His hostility to Tur-
ner’s political dabbling seemed implacable, but
when Turner laid his last offer before him, Sam
said : “Take it. It’s the chance of a lifetime.
If you become Premier you’ll be out in three
months.”

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 23, 1899.
Concerning William John Lyne.
Last week Providence, or the agent who
was temporarily keeping the cigar alight till
Providence came back, made William John
Lyne Premier of New South Wales. And
since then New South Wales has been
thoughtfully summing-up the situation, and
it recognises many things. The new Pre-
mier’s front names of William John are
against him. He is a dry, elderly politician,
who has waited long and patiently, and
“ bullocked” long and wearily, before coming
into his kingdom—and that is, perhaps,
against him also. He is supposed to be a
routine person of the old school—though that
is not known with absolute certainty ; lie
may have been merely lying low and hiding
his talent under a pint-pot pending the
arrival of his opportunity. He has never
written bad poetry like Parkes, or execrable
poetry like Reid ; nor has he been known,
so far as the w r riter is aware, to make a single
dazzlingly brilliant observation in all his
life. He has mostly stuck to his principles,
and plodded honourably in the straight track
whether it was the right one or the wrong
one—-so far as The Bulletin remembers it
was often the wrong one. No erratic or
flashy “ Yes-no ” or “ No-yes” has disfigured
his career. He has indulged in no joyful
public calisthenics. He has never really sat
on a rail, though The Bulletin once sus-
pected him of an intention that way, and
accused him of it in a cartoon. For one
thing, no ordinary rail would support him ;
for another, he is a plain, dry man, who has
few artistic tastes, and doesn’t care for the
scenery which is visible from a top rail ;
and, as a third possible reason, his
feet might get hitched in between the rails
coming down. Legend has it that William
John was once a policeman. He never was,
but if he had been The Bulletin is dead
certain he would have been a model police-
man, ever ready to tackle any-sized criminal,
alone or in company, with a plain, wooden
conscientiousness that took no account of
odds. Also, it is sure that his evidence
next morning would have been brief and to
the point. It is a hard thing to say, but if
Lyne were 30 years younger, he would make
an ideal policeman.
* * • *
Just now, however, there is a woeful
dearth of political talent in N.S.W. Every
province passes through these barren stages.
Queensland has been in one ever since
Lilley quitted politics. Maoriland in the
Atkinson and Whitaker days, and Victoria
in the period of Gillies, Munro and Pat-
terson, and S.A. in the Downer and
Colton and jaunty Johnny Bray era, strug-
gled through just the same kind of wilder-
ness. So circumstances have thrown Wil-
liam John Lyne to the surface. And,
despite the patient dreariness of the man,
there is no absolutely certain evidence that
circumstances have not done N.S.W. a good
turn. Lyne has never yet had a great
opportunity ; it remains to be seen whether
he has any use for an opportunity when
it comes. As a subordinate member
of the Dibbs and Jennings Ministries
he had no chance to reveal great con-
structive abilities, if he had them. As
Opposition leader his only show was to be
a brilliant critic, and Lyne’s elocution is like
unto the light and airy fancies of three tons
of bricks. His want of eloquence, however,
has nothing to do with the case. A man
may be a brilliant debater and a giddy mob
orator, like George Reid, -and have no
political honesty, no fixity of purpose, and
no constructive ability ; also, he may label
himself “Democrat” with a huge label sus-
pended on his lower waistcoat, again like
George Reid, and be full of alleged good
intentions, and do practically nothing. If
Lyne will accomplish, in a dull, practical
way, some long-waited-for reforms, instead
of talking about them with bright and airy
persiflage, then he may label himself “Tory,”
or “Reactionary,” or anything he likes.
N.S.W. has had enough gorgeous label
during the last five years to last it for a
century. Five years ago, after Victoria had
decided that the eloquent Shiels, with his
big democratic inscription, was no good, that
province, in a moment of desperation, tried
a painfully-serious, totally un-eloquent,
commonplace-looking little man with ill-
fitting brown clothes, who had never in his
life raised a laugh in a mob meeting or pro-
mised to make anybody Jolly and Fat. And
he has done more for the people, in his
hard, unsympathetic way, than the loudest
alleged Democrat of the crowd. There is no
reason why Lyne should not be the same
kind of a surprise if he wants to.
• * * *
For a Premier of energy, courage and
patient industry (Lyne has all three), of
constructive ability (Lyne may have that
hidden about him, or he may borrow it from
a better-equipped colleague), and of genuine
democratic instincts (Lyne may have been
concealing these so far, and if so his success
in the concealment has been marvellous
beyond description) New South Wales offers
a brilliant field. That province wants so
many things that a Premier needs no great
originality to build up a good programme.
It also wants so many things that one or
other of the adjacent provinces have got that
he doesn’t even need much constructive
ability; a capacity for judiciously selecting
the best features of the legislation of other
States would keep a reasonably adaptable
man going for a dozen years. N.S.W. needs
a thoroughly good Navigation law ; an
Early-Closing Act—which ean be borrowed
to a great extent from Maoriland ; a Local
Governmenc Act, which can be cadged bodily
•from Victoria, that province having about
the most complete Local Government system
in Australasia ; and an Old Age Pension law
after the Maoriland pattern. It wants its
land and income taxes graduated in or about
the Maoriland or South Australian fashion,
according to the size of the income or pro-
perty, with the S.A. absentee tax on top of
all. Its Shops and Factories Act requires to
be improved and extended all over the pro-
vince. It wants a minimum wage ; a State
Land Bank built on the comprehensive lines
of the Maoriland institution but with its
funds raised locally as in Victoria ; a com-
pulsory Conciliation and Arbitration law,
also after the Maoriland pattern ; a State
note issue ; a scheme of land resumption for
closer settlement which will be as nearly as
possible built on the Maoriland principle ; a
State Life Insurance Department; a reformed
Upper House, possibly after the pattern
of the Federal Senate; a law regulating
hours of labor, and it is difficult to say how
many things besides. A man who knows
what to borrow from the legislation of more
advanced countries, and how to borrow it,
and who will turn neither to the right hand
nor the left, and waste no time flying off the
handle however brilliantly, but” will shove
his measures through by sheer dead weight,
has a great chance to do something among
all these urgent necessities.
• " * * *
Above all things there is a large opportun-
ity for an able financier—something that
N.S.W. hasn’t had within the memory of
most-men now living. George Reid left
the deficit just about as large as he found it
—left it the great boss deficit of Australasia.
He used up all the possibilities that were in
the Cash Basis for passing the liabilities
along—his Cash Basis permitted him to
permanently carry forward about £700,000
of unpaid bills, but that stroke of
genius can only be done once. He
seized the Church and School Lands-fund.
He saved one half-year's payment to most of
the sinking-funds by altering the date of the
financial year. He struck the great idea of
charging the interest on the public debt for
the half-year from Ist January to 30th June
(the day the financial year closes) to next
financial year because it isn't absolutely paid
over to the public creditor till Ist July (the
first day of the new financial year), and thus
made his accounts look better on paper. All
these mean little juggles are now exhausted,
therefore Lyne has to face the hard reality
of things. Moreover, Reid cut down the
road-vote so low that his successor must find
more money or see the public highways go to
ruin. He charged a groat mass of ordinary
expenditure to loans, which his successor
can’t do unless he wishes to be the same
object of public scorn that Reid became—
and that also means finding more money.
The late Premier raised the loan expendi-
ture in his last year to £700,000 more
than it was in the last year of the Dibbs-
Lyne Ministry, and cutting down the loan
expenditure again means finding still more
money. And finally, Reid left behind him a
fearful and incredible set of estimates for
the year ending 30th June, 1900, in which
he set forth that if he got £50,000 more
taxation the deficit would be £126,000, and
if he didn’t get it the shortage would be
£176,000.
» * * *
If Lyne is going to cut down loan ex-
penditure to the comparatively reasonable
figure to which the old Dibbs-Lyne Ministry
reduced it, and to cease faking the accounts
by paying for repairs to old buildings and
hundreds of other items of current expendi-
ture out of loans, and to make the finances
balance, he obviously wants about £700,000
a year more revenue. And it is difficult to
see where it can all or nearly all be raised
except by customs revenue. Yet to resort
to new customs taxation now on any large
scale will probably be regarded as a raising
of the fiscal ghost which all parties are
pledged to leave undisturbed till the Federal
Government takes that branch of the busi-
ness over.
• • * •
Reid, the eloquent funny man, and word-
juggler, and figure-juggler, and dancing
dervish of finance, has therefore left a tre-
mendous contract for his successor. His last
estimates, in which he foreshadowed a
shortage of £126,000 for the coming year,
showed that he was close up to the end of
his resources. And the position of the man
who toils to clear up the wreckage which
Reid left will be aggravated by all the
annoyances and all the obstructions which
that comic clown can bring to bear upon
him. Altogether Lyne starts with about the
largest contract which was ever faced by any
N.S.W. Premier. And if, by any weird
chance, he proves to be a strong, silent indi-
vidual, with strength and constructive ability
and plain horse-sense behind his apparently
dull exterior, he has the best chance ever
offered to any N.S.W. Premier to be the
man for the occasion. Sometimes the man
is wasted for want of the occasion ; Lyne
has an occasion as large as a good-sized Alp
if he is only the man. The Bulletin is
getting ready a halo to fit William John if
he astonishes his province by being the big
resourceful statesman that a suffering country
so bitterly requires. And if he proves him-
self only a plantigrade individual with a
yearning to fool away the public money in
this time of great straits by sending a foolish
contingent to shoot at somebody in Africa,
The Bulletin’s smallest boy will use that
halo as a hoop to play with on his next
birthday.
More Contingent.
Over half Australia just now there is a moan-
ing of shoddy majors and drapery colonels
about the advisability of sending troops to the
Transvaal in case of war. Maoriland has got
disease. Queensland has got it badly.
N.S. \V. has got it a good deal. The chief
reason why Victoria is late in catching it is
that Turner is a business man of the most
prosaic type, and all the gauds and feathers
and flags in the world wouldn’t pay him for
having bis finances thrown out of gear. But
let it be noted that the people who want to go
on this loyal and heroic errand don’t propose,
so far as all appearances go, to lose anything
by it themselves. They will probably get
good pay, plenty of provisions, and a great
send-off, and be, for some little time before
their departure, gaudy idols in uniform, and
able to walk out with a different girl every day
of the week. They will have their photos, in
the papers, and have bouquets heaved
at ( them, and have pretty women send-
ing them their portraits. Then they will have
a ( pleasant sea-pienic. When they reach the
Cape they will be treated not quite as the
ordinary Tommy Atkins, who is meant to be
shot at so long as the country requires him, and
then accommodated in the workhouse in his
old age, but rather as a noble and touching
symbol of the solidity of the Empire, and of the
attachment of the colonies to the dear old
mother country. And a Symbol requires a
great deal better food and accommodation than
a plain lommy. They will be taken special
notice of with a view to “ fostering the loyalty
of the colonies,’ and be paraded and inspected
and publicly thanked. It any of them are
killed or maimed by Oom Paul’s burghers
there will, in the natural order of things, be
very handsome provision out of the public
funds for the cripples and for t he dependents of
those who were cut off in the midst of their
glory. And the survivors will come back
brown and glorious, after mother pleasant
trip, to be wildly cheered on arrival,
and be thanked and reviewed and inspected
in the presence of admiring thousands, and
have more flowers thrown at them, and have
more pretty girls looking lovingly into their
eyes, and get their photos, into the papers again,
and be interviewed, and banqueted, and have
oceans of free beer, and he cablegrammed
about, and they will draw their accumulated
pay, and he heroes generally.
* * * m
And it is ten to one that the British military
authorities, having an inward suspicion about
the steadiness of these holiday soldiers, will
put them pretty far back from that part of the
line where Kruger’s grim Afrikanders are
thickest, and all the glory will be secured with
very little damage. And as for the community
in general its business is to work a little
harder, and keep its nose to the grindstone a
little longer per diem, to pay the bill, and also
to wear out its throat cheering when the great
men come back, and to pay for the flowers that
are thrown at them, and shout for them, and
grovel to them, and be pleased when they take
away its best girl, and proud to lose its best
girl in so noble a cause.
• * * *
This proposed patriotic uprising is to help
Great Britain in a war with the 80,000 Boers
of the little State by the Vaal. The population
of Great Britain and Ireland is over 40,000,000
■ say 500 to 1 odds against the Boers. Reckon-
ing Britain and British India together, and
taking no count of the rest of the empire, the
odds against the farmer of the little republic
are about 3000 to 1, without any Australian
interference. The whole Boer population in
the Iransvaal isn’t equal to one-fourth of the
population of Sydney ojc Melbourne. It is less
than the population of Brisbane. It wouldn’t
make a fourth-rate English town. The British
subjects in the adjoining Cape Colony alone
should be able to flatten out Kruger and
Joubert with one hand. The Boers are
vastly outnumbered by the foreigners in their
own country. And three large Australasian
provinces profess to believe that Britain, with
500 to 1 odds in its favor, needs their assist-
ance in this matter, and they propose to work
up a great reputation for valor over it. It seems
a pity that they don’t make arrangements for
hitting some one of their own size. On mere
numbers, Tasmania alone should be a match
for the Boers of the Transvaal twice over.
* * * *
And the reason of the trouble is that a cer-
tain mixed crowd of Americans, Germans, .
Jews, Englishmen, and others, who own the
gold mines of the Rand, have grievances. Their
industry is taxed to about one-fifth the extent
to which the gold industry is taxed in the
adjoining British territory of Rhodesia ; at
about the same rate as in Queensland and Tas-
mania ; and a good deal less than in Maoriland
and (under Bigjohn Forrest’s new laws) in
YVestralia. Also the naturalisation laws are
not liberal enough (that is to say they are just
about as liberal as in Great Britain or N.S. W.)
therefore the alien doesn’t get a fair show to
help at governing the country. The alien is
taxed without representation just as he is in
England and Australia and anywhere else ; and
in addition to everything else the laws are ad-
ministered in a mean and grudging spirit. The
Boer doesn’t show that large-minded and
generous trust in the alien that he ought to do
after the seizure of his country in 1877, and after
the invasion of it in 1895 by a British force in
time of profound peace, and with the admitted
consent and approval of the Premier of an
adjacent British province, supported by the
cheering verses of the British Poet Laureate,
and the wild encouragement of the United
Kingdom in general.
* * * *
Wherefore The Bui.letin rises in its place
to propose these three resolutions :
(1) That Maoriland, N.S.W., and Queensland, if they
must needs go to war, should hit somebody nearer
to their own size instead of wading in against a small
State that, has done them no harm and that is already
facing odds of over 600 to 1. It is low and cowardly and
degrading, when you see a complete stranger with about
two dozen men on top of him, to offer your services as
25th man. The fact that the stranger is a foreigner and
the 24 men are your own countrymen makes it no less
degrading.
(2) That if any section of the Australian people feels so
patriotically fervent that it must go and hit one of the
smallest States in the world, it should pay its own pass-
age, and buy its own gun, and do the whole thing at its
own expense. The individual who is so consumed with ,
patriotic ardour that he must be paid good wages for
letting his feelings loose is a fraud and a hypocrite and a .
mercenary.
(3) But if the ruling classes of Australia are so full of ;
sympathy and ardor that they must tax this, at present, .
poor and depressed country, in order to send assistance ■
gratis to one of the richest crowds of capitalists in the
world, it should at least be explained to the British
Government, that no title or “honor” of any kind must,
be conferred on any Australian politician over the.
wretched business. Because, otherwise, an impression:
may arise that the senders of the contingent are mere
coarse, murderous butchers, who don’t mind who is killed:
or who is burdened with needless extra taxation, so long
as they can wade through blood to a title of the kind
which is bestowed in England on the grocery mayor of ai
second-rate town.
And these three resolutions are hereby carried!
unanimously.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Grief of Certain Eminent Citizens.
An influential meeting was held in Sydney last
week at which Jawbone Neild and Rev. Carr-
Smith and others expressed their just indigna-
tion at the Dreyfus sentence and at Dreyfus’s
judges, and their admiration of Picquart and.
Zola who dared to espouse the cause of the-
oppressed. Also, a committee (George Pile,.
honorary secretary) was appointed to further
express the public detestation. This sort of
tiling sets The Bulletin thinking. The French
authorities, finding that Dreyfus had been,
sentenced on no real evidence, gave him a fresh:
hearing and reduced his sentence to ten years ;;
but when Suffield was sentenced on no real
evidence the N.S.W. authorities refused him
any new hearing at all, although at least one
Minister of Justice admitted almost in so-
many words that there were doubtful circum-
stances about the case. The French authori-
ties tried to prevent Dreyfus getting a re-
hearing because the “ case was closed.”
O’Connor, Reid, Gould and co. and the
N.S.W. Parliament and people did prevent


Suffield getting a re-hearing on the
same ground. Colonel Jouast’s bias against
Dreyfus wasn’t a whit more violent
or more obvious or more shameful than
the unspeakable Windeyer’s bias against Suf-
field, and against every other prisoner who
came before him with a woman for his accuser.
Dreyfus served five years on no evidence worthy
of the name ; Suffield served more than eight.
So The Bulletin appoints itself lion, secretary
of its own little movement to express its utter
contempt and detestation of such cheap heroes
and philanthropists as Pile and Neild ana
Carr-Smith, who are so deeply grieved about
the injustice done to the man 16,000 miles
away, and yet wouldn’t even take the trouble
to be aware of the equally gross injustice done
to the man at their own door. The cheapest,
meanest, shabbiest kind of benevolence is that
which execrates the wrong perpetrated in
Central Asia, or in Patagonia, or on the Congo,
while it won’t see the wrong in thead jacent street
—apparently because seeing the latter would
involve the worry of trying to put it right, and
the worry of trying to put it right might make
the benevolent person tired. The second
Dreyfus trial, bad and disgraceful as it is,
should make Nkild and Carr-Smith and hon.
secretary Pile crawl through a hedge and hide
behind it in shame for themselves and their
own country, instead of leading them to put on
patronising airs. France is, at all events,
civilised enough to have the legal machinery
for revoking an unjust sentence—and it did
revoke Dreyfus’s first sentence. In N.S.W.
there isn’t even the legal mechanism to revoke
the most brutally unjust sentence ever passed ;
if Dreyfus had been wrongly sentenced there
the only tiling that could be done would be to
offer him a pardon. And Neild and Carr-
Smith and hon. secretary Pile never lift up
there voices on that point—presumably because
they don’t care how far their country lags be-
hind. And while France has at least enough
healthy public sentiment to make the Dreyfus
conviction an open scandal, in N.S.W., with its
somnolent Piles and Neilds and Carr-Smiths,
their isn’t sufficient moral sense to make
the public really aware of the existence of an
equally gross outrage on justice in their own
midst. So The Bulletin thinks of calling a
public meeting of its own to express its horror
of the shoddy philanthropy that is so grieved
about the distant wrong that it can’t redress,
and is so callous to the one that it could have
redressed if it had tried —only it wouldn’t try.
And some day, if it hears a great row at night,
and looking out of the window finds Neild and
Carr-Smith and George Pile being grievously
wronged by somebody—well, it will perhaps do
just as they did. Which means that it will sit
down comfortably inside, and hold an indigna-
tion meeting about the injuries of Bulgaria,
and pass resolutions to the effect that thesuffer-
ings of somebody in Poland call for the strong
disapproval of all Christian communities.
The “Crown’s” Licensed Perjurer.
In Victoria the police and detective force has
become a disgrace and a permanent stench, and
the administration of justice, under the foster-
ing care of the Crown Law Office, has become
something a trifle worse. The general rules
now followed appear to be these two :
(1) A Crown witness is licensed to commit the most
glaring perjury with impunity, and however open and
visible is his lying, so long as he lies for the commendable
object of getting a conviction against somebody (inno-
cent or otherwise) his friend and supporter, the Crown
Law Office aforesaid, will not proceed against him.
(2) But the person who gives evidence against the
Crown, and for the prisoner, shall be prosecuted for per-
jury if there is any shred of suspicion against him, or
even with no reasonable suspicion. The nefarious habit
of people giving evidence on behalf of any prisoner must
be put down.
In support of these maxims two more cases
have just cropped up. One was known as the
Reddish licensing case. Two policemen swore
that Mrs. Reddish had committed a certain
breach of the Licensing Act; Mr. and Mrs.
Reddish and two other witnesses swore that
there was no such breach. If the Crown did
not feel inclined to let the matter rest at that
it had two courses open to it; it could prose-
cute the police for perjury, or it could prose-
cute the other crowd. Though the weight of
evideuce was against the police, it proceeded to
prosecute the other crowd, as it always does.
Then some curious things happened in this
order :
(1) The two witnesses were convicted by a jury of per-
jury, on the police evidence, for saying the same thing
that the Rkddishks said.
(2) Reddish was acquitted of perjury by another jury,
though he said the same things that the two convicted
men said.
(3) Mrs. Reddish was acquitted of perjury by still
another jury, though she said the same thing that the
two convicted men said, and in her case the Judge
(Hodges) openly declared that, in his opinion, one police-
man witness was lying.
(4) Therefore two juries held that the perjury was on
the side of the police.
(£•) And when the whole batch of cases were over Chief
Justice Madden sentenced the convicted prisoners to nine
and 12 months respectively for perjury because they made
statements which two juries had decided were true, and
for making which two other people had been dismissed
without a stain on their characters.
(6) And the police whose statements, according to the
decision of two juries, were absolutely false, are not
prosecuted at all, for the Victorian Law Department is
not built that way.
Apropos of which another case is worth quot-
ing. The other day Melbourne Criminal Court
produced a fine sample of the ornamental liar.
He had run away from the Salvarmy farm at
Bayswater, and at Cranbourne (Gippsland),
apparently out of pure cugsedness, laid infor-
mation against a young man on a serious charge.
The police-court proceedings failed to disclose
any discrepancy in his evidence, and it was
only by good luck that the defending lawyer
got bold of a clue at the Sessions’ trial. Then
it all came out after this fashion :
Counsel: “ You swear your right name is Frederick
Victor Smith, yet you deliberately stated on oath, at the
police-court, that it was Alexander Robertson, aged 15;
why did you do that? ” “ I didn’t want to get nicked.”
“ YVhat do you mean by that?” “I ran away from the
home at Bayswater, and did not want to get nicked."
“ You ran away from the Army Reformatory, at Bays-
water ?” “Yes, sir.” “Did you not swear at the
police-court that, you had never been to a reformatory ?”
“Yes, sir." “ Then that was another lie ?” “Yes, sir.”
“ Did you not swear that you came down from Ballarat
with the consent of your parents?” “ Yes, sir.” “That,
too, was a lie?” “Yes, sir.” “ And but for the fact that
your right name was discovered, you would have lied
here as you did in the police-court? " “ Yes, sir.” “ Are
you in the habit of telling lies ? ” “ No, sir.”
Then Judge Hodges ordered the witness t
stand down, and discharged the accused in a
great hurry—lest this lovely Crown witness
should get a further showing-up ? But there is
apparently no redress for the unfortunate man
who was put to heavy expense and frightful
mental torture through the vagaries of this
young scoundrel. And, up to time of writ-
ing, it hasn’t occurred to the law to prose-
cute the perjured witness. In Australia,
a Crown witness is practically exempt from
prosecution for perjury. The short and
simple rule of the Crown Law officers appears
to be that all perjury is justifiable as long as it
is done to aid the Crown in convicting an
innocent person. At least, they act on that
principle. In fact, almost every Crown Law
Department in Australia is the tacit accomplice
of perjurers of the Frederick Smith stamp.
But how the average Crown Law officers boil
over at the perjurer who helps to prtcent the
Crown getting a conviction !

The Nearly-Lost Province.
A straw which shows how the wind may pos-
sibly blow some day :
Adelaide, Wednesday.—ln the Legislative Assembly
to-day Mr. Grainger moved that the finances of the
Northern Territory should demand the careful attention
of the House, and he advised that the Government
should either dispose of it to some company willing to
pay the present debt and relieve them of" all further
responsibilities or obtain the permission of the British
Government to relinquish it. He expressed a doubt as to
whether Federated Australia would care to saddle itself
with such an expensive white elephant.
Plainly Australia hasn’t got within sight of
Federation a day, nor half a day, too soon.
Hardly one Australian in 10,000 knows what a
huge danger has been always waiting at this
country’s door. The Northern Territory is not
Australian property, though it is temporarily
“administered’ by S.A. It was to become
Australian property on certain conditions, the
principal of which was the building of the
transcontinental railway, but S.A., though it
built a huge section of that work, was never
able to finish it. The Crow-eater population is
but small, and the work was very large, and it
had to be suspended. For want of this means
of communication it has been impossible to get
enough population into the Territory to make
it pay. South Australia stands the annual loss
of a huge province which isn’t its own, and
which the British Government may take back
at any time. The Territory, in its present
state, is an appalling burden on a small popu-
lation which has to police it, and supply it
with postal facilities, and put its inhabitants in
gaol when they get drunk, and look after its
wharves and telegraphs, and keep its one rail-
way going ; and if nothing happened to make
it pay better than it does, and no Federal Gov-
ernment came along to take it over, the almost
certain result would be that it would some day
go back to British ownership. And then it
would probably be sold to a semi military
British co. which would flood it with Chinese
and nigger cheap labor —and said cheap labor
would constantly slop over into wdiite
Australia, and make it piebald and poor and
leprous. And the co. would probably keep
a military force of its own, which would be to
white Australia the same menace that the
Rhodes and Jamieson crowd has been to the
Transvaal ; and this country, after a period of
complete self government, w ould partly relapse
to the old, degraded status of a crown colony.
The Federal Government can avert this danger.
It can secure the definite surrender of the Ter-
ritory as Federal property if it is only in dead
earnest about the matter. S.A. wants to get
rid of its white elephant, and Britain will
hardly refuse if all Australia demands it. And
though the Territory has been a dead loss to
S.A., it should be a gloriously profitable asset
to the Commonwealth, and worth £2, 000,000 a
year by-and-bye in land-revenue alone. This
rich and well-watered country has been a
failure in the past simply because of its
utter inaccessibility, and because S.A.,
whose whole population is less than that of
Sydney, couldn’t raise the money necessary to
open it up. The Commonwealth Government
can raise the money to open it up—which
makes all the difference. But considering how
very near this great asset lias been on several
occasions to lapsing back to Britain, to be
thereafter jobbed away to some nigger and
Chow importing syndicate, it is evident that
Federation hasn’t come any too soon.
The “ Star’s ” Advice to Lyne.
The disastrous Australian Star started
bright and early last week to give N.S.W.
Premier Lyne advice of the kind that is cal-
culated to cut his Premiership very short
indeed. Of all the Sydney dailies the Star is
the dullest and most wooden-headed ; and the
pity of the thing as regards Lyne is that it is
supposed to be the special organ of the Lyne
party, aud has a big lien on the special job of
advising Lyne as to how he may most speedily
walk over a precipice and be made quite dead.
The Borrowing Policy was the subject on
which the Star first proceeded to breathe its
ancient and fish-like counsel into Lyne’s ear.
It didn’t advise him to leave off borrowing as
any sane paper would have done, but with its
usual dull fatuousness it implored Lyne to
leave off local borrowing—to swear off the only
good feature in the dreadfully bad business—
and raise his loans in London in the good old
way, so that the interest would go out of the
country and be a perpetual drain on its
resources. According to the Star :
It was an unnatural thing, on the face of it, that.large
amounts of local capital in a young country should be
locked up in Govt, securities, instead of flowing through
its proper channels in the shape of private enterprise.
That local capital should be employed in local industry
seemed not only an incontestable maxim in itself, but an
indispensable condition to prosperity. It could not be
supposed that money circulating through Govt, agency,
always with an eye to political necessities, could
afford as much employment to labor, or produce
as much national wealth, as if it were in the
hands of private employers, stimulated by pri-
vate enterprise. The local loans that were regarded
with so much caution by financial authorities before Mr.
Reid assumed otlice afterwards became an established
feature Of his financial system. The risks and dangers
attaching to them accurately predicted then have sinoe
made themselves conspicuous in the actual condition of
the country. In just proportion to the rate at which the
Govt, absorbed local capital private enterprise has
dwindled away, local production has decreased, and the
labor-market has become more and more hopelessly
stagnant. That these things stand in the relation of
cause and effect no unprejudiced observer can deny.
The proof, if any be needed, is abundant and palpable.
The more the market has been flooded with 4 per cent.
Treasury bills, and the more the Trust Funds have been
drafrued in to the rescue, so much the more have these
disastrous effects made themselves apparent from day to
day. If the ten or twelve millions that have been bor-
rowed from local capitalists in this manner had not
been withdrawn from private enterprise the money, or
most of it, at any rate, would certainly have found an out-
let in local undertakings of one kind or another. By that
means, and to that extent, private employers would have
been placed in a position to employ more labor, the volume
of local production would have been enlarged, and the late
Govt, would not have been driven to the humiliating'
necessity of appointing an Advisory Board to help them
out of the unemployed difficulty. When that point in
the downward path was reached it was surely high time
to call a halt. The longer the present system is con-
tinued the worse will the situation become. Common
prudence suggests that a limit must sooner or later be
placed to the absorption of local capital, and with such
evidence as we have before us the conclusion is irresistible
that the sooner it is done the better.
The Star, in short, is grieved to its inmost
soul that N.S. YV. should have begun to show
some small speck of self-reliance, and it im-
plores Lyne to leave it off at once. But the
whole story about the “ withdrawal of capital
from private enterprise” is pitiable bunkum.
If N.S. W. borrows £2,000,000 or £20,000,000
in the country for public works, and spends it
in the country on public works, then the
money never leaves the country at all. It
isn’t “ withdrawn.” The country has the rail-
ways and other works, and so far it is a gainer,
but there isn’t a penny less available to “flow
through its proper channels in the shape of
private enterprise.” Both principal and interest
remain in the country. But if N.S.W.
borrows £2,000,000 in London at 3§ per
cent, for public works, at the end of 30
years it has sent away £2,100,000 in inter-
est, and in 60 years it has sent away
<£4,200.000 in interest, and so on ad infinitum.
It is foreign—not local—borrowing that takes
away money that should be available for pri-
vate enterprise. Owing to foreign borrowing
over £2,000,000 that should be available for
private enterprise is going out of N.S.YV.
every year, and it is this huge outflow which
makes N.S.W. so poor. The theory of Pre-
mier Lyne’s fatal adviser is that N.S.W. will
be ruined unless it has to send away plenty of
money every year as interest, and if that in-
terest money remains in the country the re-
sults will be disastrous. In fact, it holds that
the province is in desperate straits for money
through not having to pay so much money to
England as it should do ; and that if it had to
send away more money then it would naturally
have more money left. If Premier Lyne wants
to do the greatest and noblest thing in N.S.W.
history he will swear off borrowing altogether.
If he can’t rise to that he will at least do the
second best thing if he utterly swears off
foreign borrowing. And if lie knows when to
come in out of the wet he will swear off the
Australian Star, as a disastrous counsellor
whose capacity for being wrong is utterly
past belief.
The Sweating* of a Surplus.
Premier Turner’s conscience must at times,
when it catches him revelling in the beauti-
ful proportions of his surplus, give him a
bad half-hour. Also, the same conscience has
opportunity to make sundry sarcastic observa-
tions to him and his brother Minister, Peacock,
when these two ponder over the provisions of
the Amending Factories’ Act. For that part of
the Treasurer’s surplus which is not accounted
for by the natural increase in the province’s
prosperity appears to be chiefly due to unre-
stricted sweating, for which the Treasurer and
Chief-Sec. aforesaid are largely responsible.
In the bad times following the boom the
pruning-knife was applied with great vigor, and
the Victorian public would have promptly
sacked any Treasurer who attempted to raise
the salaries of any class of public servants.
But now that things are so far improved that it
is seriously proposed to relieve the wealthier
income - receivers of some of their alleged
burdens, it is the business of Parliament
to compel the State-housekeeper to pay his
servants decent wages before blewing his
income in the reckless fashion he intends.
There are three classes of employes in Vic.
Public Service to-day who are as outrageously
sweated as any worker for an undercutting
slop-clothier or boot-factory. Between ’B9 and
’9O some 200 men were taken on by the Post
Office as messengers, at a maximum salary of
£6O, less insurance and sundry other small
deductions. They were given to understand
that they w r ould ultimately be transferred to
the clerical and letter-carrying branches.
These men, many of whom now vary in age
from 24 to 29 and have wives and families, are
at present employed in operating and clerical
work. They do the same work as is done by
others alongside them at from £lOO to £240
per annum, yet they are still called messengers
and still receive their £6O per year. One has
full charge of a fair-sized post-and-telegraph
office at about £1 a week. There are also 1553
pupil teachers employed by the Education Dept.
Many of these, having remained stationary,
in the matter of grading, during the post-
boom years, are now men and women of from
25 to 35. They have passed three stiff exams. ;
they are qualified teachers ; they do work for
which others working alongside them are paid
at from £l5 to £25 per month, and they are
paid at a maximum rate—less insurance and
other deductions —of, men £5O, women £4O per
annum. The third case is that of the lunatic-
asylum warders and wardresses. The maximum
pay here is £54 and £6O per year, which in
itself is a paltry remuneration for any man or
woman in any kind of work. But the sinful-
ness of their treatment lies chiefly in the fact
that the Govt, demands their atttendance at
the asylums for no less than 98 hours per
week 1 1 Writer has inspected the attendance-
books of both male and female attendants at
Kew Asylum, in addition to having separate
statements from the persons themselves. Their
sweating employer actually works them 77 hours
per week and compels them to remain in their
rooms at the asylum—where they can be called
at any moment—for a further 21 hours
per week. These men and women, as
public servants, are refused the right of
laying their case before the public through
the press. They are also refused access
to members of Parliament individually, though
they may petition that august assemblage ; and
when a private member does altempt to obtain
something like decent treatment for those of
them who are his constituents lie is told that
“ the Premier has no sympathy with public
service complaints, and that there are plenty
of hard up unemployed outside that service.”
Also Minister of Mines Foster met one com-
plaint with the brutal irrelevancy, “ Are there
any public servants starving?” Well, if a
man has to keep a wife and family on £1 a
week, or a woman of 35 has to maintain her-
self, pay her insurance, buy her necessary
books, &c., on 15s. a week—if that isn’t
starvation it comes as near to it as makes no
differ ence.
A CURIOUS commentary on the “value’ of
human testimony is furnished by the wide dis-
crepancies between the impressions produced
upon different observers (the newspaper cor-
respondents) of the same Dreyfus at the same
time :
(1) A man 3.) ; hair white as silver, and at the back
of the crown bald ; face bolh burned and pale—a larg-e-
-featured face with a pointed chin . . . face suffering a
misery hardly to be borne.
(2) He walked with light elastic step ; his brow un-
clouded by recent suffering, and his joyousness at the
sight of the vast concourse of his fellow-men “ sunned ”
the spectators.
(3) Ninety-nine persons out of 100 would set down as
an old man this individual with the grey, clos ly-cut
hair, somewhat large ears, and stooped shoulders.
(4) He steps on the platform with a firm swing, salutes,
aces his judges for an instant, and takes seat.
(5) The figure of a student and of a military man com-
bined ; straight, and yet shoulders rounded like those of
an aged clerk. The back of his head projects beyond the
curve of the shoulders in an ugly way.
(6) His closelv-oropped hair is white, but the face clean-
shaven, except for the well-trimmed black moustache, is
ruddy with health. The eyes are steadfast, and the
whole attitude bespeaks strength and determination.
(7) A cool, calculating man, ever on his guard. Steps
cautiously, puts his chair exactly as he wants it; and
seats himself with precision.
(8) A face foxy in its cunning—nothing to denote the
Jew, except something in the sharp, blue eyes.
Yet all these people were trying to be accurate.
“We find what we expect to find ! ” Think of
it, ye jurors, when holding in your hands the
balance of life—or death.

Brisbane Telegraph still plods away at its
uninteresting old semi-perjury about how Bris-
bane and South and Central Queensland “gave
a majority against the Bill.” Recently the
figures were : Centre, federal majority, 1832 ;
South, federal majority, 16; Brisbane, anti-
federal majority, 4091. And, by lumping the
three together, you can make a lie with some
semblance of truth about it to the effect that
the three districts gave an anti-federal majority
of 2243. That is how the Brisbane parson-
ridden paper does it. It is the same as saying
that Brisbane and Victoria together gave a
majority of 120,000 for Federation ; or that
Queensland and China put together give a
majority of 998 followers of Confucius to one
Christian ; or that in Toowoomba and Central
Africa put together 95 per cent, of the people
are black, and nearly naked, and can’t read,
and worship idols. Also, it is the same as say-
ing that Brisbane and bell put together show
such a vast majority of fiends as compaied with
decent people that Sodom and Gomorrah weren’t
a patch on them for wickedness ; and that, if
you take the editor of Brisbane Telegraph' and
Nero and Caligula together it is two to one
the party would as soon murder you as look at
you. Also, if you take Thargomindah and the
North Pole together the average temperature
of the two places is about zero. Of course, the
statement is true—yet, all the same, you
couldn’t find a snowball in Thargoniindah to
save your life. And Brisbane Telegraph’s
half-true lie is built on just these principles.

The Maffra (Vic.) Beet Sugar Industry, born
of fatuous ignorance and nourished on supine
lassitude, having temporalily collapsed, the
Vic. Govt., which advanced some £62,000
towards its establishment, has taken charge of
the factory with a view to keeping the
machinery and plant in order, and have sent
an expert up to teach the local farmer how to
capture the i-ugar-beet in its native lair. It
proposes to pay the expert a bonus on every
ton grown until such time as the person-on-the-
land shall have found a way of growing beets
at a profit. Whereupon Melb. Argus rises to
remark that the chief cause of the trouble is
the State’s intermeddling with private enter-
prise, carefully ignoring the fact that the State
in this case didn’t intermeddle with private
enterprise, for there wasn’t any. In giving
evidence before Melbourne’s Want of Employ-
ment Commission, Mr. Catani, Roads and
Bridges Engineer, put his finger on the
sore spot in this matter, and incident-
ally threw out a valuable suggestion. Said
Mr. Catani: “If you put 500 poor people
on the rich land at Boisdale (Maffra), and get
them to thin the land and grow tire beet, you
will produce sugar. The fault is that the people
there now are too rich, and will not take the
trouble.” Which is frozen truth ! And seeing
that there are idle hands in Melbourne, and
suitable land and a thoroughly-equipped fac-
tory at Maffra, there is here a grand opportu-
nity for a go-ahead Govt, to see what it can do
working “on its own ” —as a producer. Also,
it might prove a satisfactory method of coping,
to a moderate extent, with the “ unemployed
difficulty.” In any case, it couldn’t make a
worse exhibition of itself than utterly incom-
petent private enterprise has done in this case.
An important and debatable question : Would
it nob be better to bring an accuser before the
suspected person and, for identification pur-
poses, ask him bluntly, “Is this the man ? ”
rather than, as at present, putting the suspect in
a group and leaving the accuser to pick and
choose ? It is plain that the accuser’s sense of
responsibility would be greater in the former
case; it is also plain that if that way were
adopted the police, who have had a verbal
description of the guilty man, and who, having
found a man to answer the description (more
or less), place on either hand men wholly un-
like, would be shorn of a power frequently
abused. “Is that the man ? ” concentrates the
accuser’s mind on a single object and euables
him to notice points of likeness and unlikeness
liable to escape in the comparison of one indi-
vidual with several. Ou the other hand, of

The little six-nights’ seison of “ Pygmalion
and Galatea,” at Her Majesty’s, is so very short
and sudden that it will he almost over by the
time this issue catches the public eye. The fact
that calls for loudest mention, therefore, is that
“The Christian” follows on Saturday, for the
first time in Australia, and the tale of Glory and
John Storm will be on till further notice. The
present show is a satisfactory performance, after
a certain unobtrusive fashion. Thomas Kingston
makes a good Pygmalion, though his rendering of
the part isn’t equal to his Northern Spy in “Secret
Service.” Miss Henrietta Watson does Galatea
pleasantly and gracefully, though not quite so
cleverly nor quite so gracefully as din Essie
Jenyns of yore. And Hawtrey’s Chrysos is re-
sectable, though it doesn’t come within 25i miles
of Charles Fabert’s fat, unctuous Greek in ye olden
time. Fabert, who never did anything brilliant
otherwise, built up a fat and overwhelming Chrysos
who is the record Australian Chrysos to date ;
his aggressive corpulence, his bcw-leggedness, his
enquiring leer, his oily |>onip, his vulgarity, his
greasily paternal aspect, and his general semblance
of being a Greek alderman of the remote 8.C., made
that Cnrysos a remembei-somo patron of the arts.
Hawtrey isn’t fat enough, nor clumsy enough, to
lend point to the statue-girl’s remark about his
uncoutliness ; nor does he sown sufficiently alder-
manic, nor sufficiently like an old Greek cheese-
monger, to be inkeepingwith the patron who bought
statuary by the half-ton. Yet though nobody in
the co. towers very high, nobody falls with an
obtrusive thud into utter contempt, and the
statue-show is a good level one throughout. In
the case of George Majeroni’s Leucine, it is fully
as level as a billiard table. Miss Etliel Appleton s
Cynisca, and the Myrine of Miss Rose Musgrove,
however, help materially to prevent things being a
lot worse than they would be if they weren’t nearly
so good as they are.

“A White Stocking,” in one act, which
precedes the principal production, awoke glad an-
ticipations in a city which recently saw Mrs.
Brough publicly pulling up her leg-gloves. It is
a pretty little play, but the public was grieved on
oi>ening night to find that it was a horse, and not
a lady, that wore the pallid sock in question.
Cecil Ward rides the animal with the pale gar-
ment on its feet, and goes forth, for a giddy wager,
to plunder a nobleman on the highway, but it
being dark, he plunders his best girl’s father in-
stead. And when he calls on his best gill an
hour later, the old man recognises the horse
with the clean clothes on its leg, and takes
things seriously, and wants his money bock, and
as Cecil Ward has lost the money ana can’t give
it back, he gets talked nearly to death by the
terrible old parent. Hawtrey, Cecil Ward, Lis-
sant, and Miss Musgrove do this trifle among
them, and do it well—Lissant especially.

The last complete week of Brough’s season
at Sydney Royal is occupied with “A Village
Priest.” After that there will be three short one-
night barks —“Mrs. Tanqueray ” on Saturday;
“Dandy Dick” on Monday, and “Niobe”on
Tuesday, and on Wednesday there will be empti-
ness, and shadows, and dust, and rats, and a feel-
ing that some valued friends have departed. The
“ Village Priest ” hasn’t been revived in Sydney
for a long time, and, judging by its reception, the
revival fills a want that is larger than the ordinary
backyard tank. It is the only modern play with
a miracle in it —that leaf of the Bible that slowly
rises like a condemned man’s hair or the tail of tho
pleased cat at breakfast time, and bristles up for
a moment in the limelight, and then turns over,
leaving the abbe staring at the text wherein lies
an answer to the prayer lie has prayed for 19
years. It isn’t a very definite text, though he
gathers from it that he must break the seal
of confession, and extract the holy cork of
the church, and unbottle the gruesome tale
about how the Judge who sentenced Jean
Torquenie for murder was himself the assassin;
but if the text is rather indefinite, the miracle is
smart and effective. It says much for Brough’s
stage-management, that all the'time he has played
the “Priest,” that pallid leaf has risen impres-
sively in the east and set in the west without a
hitch. Joseph Game’s abbe is a satisfying character
in every respect, and if there were more priests
like him, and fewer like the hurrying, hot, flushed
stout father that one meets in everyday life, the
writer would —but, on second thoughts, he pro-
bably wouldn’t. Brough fills his old place as
Jean Torquenie for all it is worth. There are
no conspicuous female parts in the “Priest,” but
Miss Bessie Thompson makes a loveable old blind
lady with that artistic finish that she puts into
all her characters. Mrs. Brough fills a small,
sad part very cleverly, and Miss Noble does
her little streak of girlish prettiness in a way
that satisfies everybody. One remnant of the
original cast that is much missed is Miss
Bessie Major as the abbe’s housekeeper. The
housekeeper is a very small but Miss
Major made a great deal out of her. That lady,
by the way, was rather a mvstery. In the old
days she used to transpire on the stage about once
in six months or so, and though she transpired
very well, that fact seemingly didn’t encourage
her to transpire any more frequently. And then
at last, a long time ago, she left off transpiring
altogether, and the abbe got a new housekeeper.
