On Saturday night between 5000 and 0000
people gathered together at Red fern (Sydney)
in the largest tent this writer ever saw to watch
the opening of the Flying Jordans’ circus and
hippodrome. Many things happened that even-
ing. There were three events going on simultane-
ously, high up in tho air, when suddenly a pink
figure that swung and swayed between the rings
seemed to shoot straight out, and the audience
gasped as it whirled downwards and struck the
ground with a “wough.” The lady that fell
never seemed to utter a sound. When they
picked her up she smiled and waved her hands to
the audience. Then they bore her away in a
chair, the bell rang, and the cloud people came
down to earth again. A cloud of clowns trotted
on ; a small boy built himself a tower of chairs
and contorted on top of the trembling edifice,
while on the stage a huge frog gibbered at
the people and tied his legs in knots. Then
what seemed a tiny dot of a girl came out
and climbed up on to a little platform about the
size of a chair and opening a Japanese umbrella
stepped out boldly into empty space. The dot
was a wire-walker of no ordinary kind, but when
she threw herself down (into a net) the public hair
stood up for a moment —the public hadn’t got its
nerves in order again and wanted something with
a stick in it. The Farley trio gave their balancing
feat and some weird mephistophelian figures in
red did indescribable things with their legs
—and just about then the electric-light
gave a flash and went out, The tent was in the
deep twilight of four flickering gasolene lights.
More flickers were brought, but the crowd on the
northern side, seeing two sparring exhibitions and
a woman juggling on the platform, crowded for-
ward and packing themselves round the performers
blotted out the whole show. A mighty wind came
up and shook the tent, and timid people be-
came alarmed and left. Suddenly there was
great shouting to clear the course, and the crowd
stCK>d on chairs and craned its collective neck in
wild excitement, as a dim ghostly outline of a
human head and body glided through the gloom,
while behind rushed a dark figure on horseback.
It looked like a human soul fleeing from the sins
of its youth ; in reality, it was Olaf Schrader on
his bike, racing Pawnee Bill on horseback. They
fled round and round, till at last Bill overtook and
rode right over the cyclist and trod on him amidst
thunders of applause. One man evidently forgot
the age he lived in. and held out his hand with
the thumb turned down. Two Roman chariots
came out and started to gallop through the gloom ;
the one with cream horses winning by a length.
Then a tall human apparition with a fearsome
headdress fled round the ring, pursued by the
dark Bill who hail already claimed one victim.
The apparition ran like a lost soul before a fiend
with a toasting-fork, hut the horse broke into a
gallop and passed him. About this time the
gloom became too thick, and the show left off.
On Monday night it opened again with a repaired
electric-light, and everything went smoothly.

Tho rather indefinite career of tho Asiatic
insurgent leaves off shortly at the Criterion. On
Saturday Rignold reappears as Paolo Macari in
“ Called Back,” and if he is anything like the old
Macari the show should pay rent and gas and
other burdens and liabilities for a week or two.
There have been sundry changes in the cast of the
“Mutiny.” Miss Wheeler is absent and Miss
Helene Burdette plays the colored heroine in the
long, gay clothes, and whirls round the big gun
which is fired atNebu Singh’s horde. She fills the
part very well, but on her first night the big gun
sent in its resignation and refused to go off, though
the horde stood iu front of it very patiently. The
curtain came down disgustedly, and the gun was
remonstrated with. Then the curtain went up
again, and the gun went off with a sheet of flame
that appeared to set the horde on fire in eight
places. Brown Augustus has also given up being
chief insurgent, anil Johnson Weir fills his place,
while a rather unsatisfactory kind of person runs
for assistance iu Johnson’s old character.

The loud trumpet-blare of “ Tommy At-
kins, ” which al ways turns up somewhere when there
is talk of war, is blowing loudly and hard this
week at Sydney Lyceum. War oil the stage isn’t
a means of settling international differences ; it is
merely a device for killing off the dramatist’s ac-
cumulated stock of wicked captains, so as to make
room for tho new season’s shipments. If it wasn’t
for war the dramatist’s wicked captains would
have to die of old age and ingrowing toe-nail, or
be run over by an escaped cask of beer bounding
downhill. “Tommy Atkins ”is a drama wherein
the captain has two wives—one of whom he shoots,
while lie shows tho other a nice, quiet ferny place
in the river, where she may drown herself and in-
fant comfortably. Then he goes away as a polished
gentleman should when a lady is thinking of
drowning herself. He knows that she is poor, and
has torn stockings and broken boots, because ho
never buys her any new ones; and he feels that
she won’t like him to lute these details when she is
inverted in the air on I .or way over the bridge. It
is otherwise roinarhable on account of a rich
Christian who, when his prodigal daughter comes
home, expresses the hope that she will die miser-
ably of cold feet under a tree somewhere, with a
snowball for a pillow; and a curate, who on see-
ing his inhospitable conduct, takes off the crow-
like garb of his sacred calling, and kicks it into
next week, and enlists- in disgust. After which
events the captain goes on being wicked to the
end ; and after trying to marry a third wife, and
being forcibly removed from the altar where he
is defiling the sacred edifice with language,
and after endeavouring to sell his country, and
doing all the other cardinal sins, ho dies in the
wilderness, using shocking expressions in his last
moments. The old Cliristian gentleman remains
bitter to the end, and never forgives his lost
daughter. The curate stays out of the Church
all his days, and retains a low opinion of it to the
end. And the good young man called Ehenezer,
who takes to drink, remains drinking—in fact,
the writer makes no laborious effort to clean up
his characters, and lets most of them go their own
way, which is probably the best he could do with
them. The Bulletin has a vague idea that it
rather likes “Tommy Atkins,” but it has no re-
liable information on this point.

.Rickards’ burnt-out company has now
settled down at Sydney Palace, and lias partially-
replaced the large and expensive wardrobes which
were reduced to ashes at the old establishment.
Pathetic tales are going about as to the immense
losses which xvere sustained when the former
trousers and vests took fire, and the previous bell-
toppers were singed. The average actor, when
one meets him on a steamer, appears to have as
small a carpet-bag as any other citizen, but when
he is burnt-out ho creates as much ash in the
advertisement as if he were a four-storey clothing
warehouse. Anyhow, it is pleasing to note that
the 00. is as well-dressed as ever.
The Bulletin’s occasional contradictions of
the story re Amy Castles and J. C. Williamson’s
proposal to take her voice on a Ion" lease, were
seemingly not quite true in fact, although they
expressed the real meaning of the wily manager’s
negotiations. The original tale spread by little
Miss Amy’s boomsters was offered as evidence of
the damsel’s glorious future. It was said that
Mr. Williamson proposed to pack her off to Mar-
chesi per next boat, pay for her education, and
subsidise her family for three years, at the end of
which long period she would be required to re-
turn to Australia on a two years’ engagement.
He was to “risk ”£3OOO, said the tarradiddle, on
the chance of getting it all back with enormous
profits in 1903-4. This would have been a splen-
did confession of faith in the young lady’s voice,
and the question now arises—Did the boomsters
in charge of the “ Future Melba ” misrepresent the
case, or did the Melb. newspaper paragraphists
carefully suppress the principal clause in the pro-
posal ? The best offer that manager Williamson
made was designed for his own immediate benefit
—a sound business offer, with no sort of risk at-
taching to it. He was to give 12 Castles concerts
in Australia, and scoop the whole pool previous to
sending Miss Castles away to be educated at his
expense. In short, J. C. W. was prepared to buy
up the boom on the chance of making a
profit of two or three thousand pounds within
three months. He reckoned on getting about
£5090 from the 12 concerts, to meet an obligation
of about half that amount. And if there ever was
a great future in Amy’s voice; it is a pity
for her that her parents didn’t sign J. C. W.’s
bond. He would have kept her top note on the
chain (when she wasn’t singing as per contract),
and sent her ’ome in good vocal condition.

Touching the Bijou programme, there is
nothing to say this week except Cinquevalli, and
touching Cinquevalli, The Bulletin will merely
remark, “Wonderful!” in the same tone of voice
as before. The famous juggler has now arrived
at his very last nights. On Wednesday of next
week he will give his farewell performance in
Melbourne, and Melbourne may be expected to
give him the biggest and most intelligent burst of
applause it has ever hurled at a star-performer
since Sarah Bernhardt took her leave. The city
has slowly absorbed the fact that Cinquevalli—
strong man, graceful man, acrobat, juggler and
balancer —is positively incomparable. And the
incomparable person, when found, is a reasonable
excuse for indulging in a wild, healthful whoop on
the occasion when one is never going to look upon
his like again.
Two concert seasons will be concluded at
Melb. Town Hall on Saturday. In the afternoon
Scherek gives the sixteenth and last of his admir-
able Orchestral Concerts, and in the evening
Mdlle. Trebolli is to give her positively last Song
Recital. This is the time o’ year when Melbourne
turns off its musical gas at the meter, and ••‘•sorts
to savage out door life.
One notable feature of the Dreyfus case
is that a French officer’s commission is his
property, of which lie can only be deprived by
process of law. An English officer’s services may
be “dispensed with” at tho pleasure of the
Crown, and he has no appeal. That is why
Dreyfus, the first sentence against him having been
quashed, appeared at Rennes in uniform. The
socond sentence of 10 years in a fortress does not
seem to carry with it iiis dismissal from the ser-
vice—at least the cable doesn’t say so.
Henry Malcolm Deakin, erstwhile a well-
known and popular Sydney citizen, who, like
many others, came to financial grief at- the end of
the boom, and went to Westralia to repair his for-
tunes, died in Mr. Fitzgerald’s private hospital,
Melbourne, on Sept. 10, aged 44 years. He had
just made a large fortune by leasing the Collie
(W. A.) collieries and selling the output thereof for
years ahead to a big Adelaide firm, when he was
overtaken by a cerebral trouble which firstly
necessitated the removal of an eye and then the
desperate expedient of trephining, to which
latter operation he succumbed.
One of the brilliant lights of the Vic. House
of Rich Landlords was in rare form at the Melb.
Dreyfus meeting, last week, and, when it was
proposed to cable the sympathetic message to
Fiuauo, he put in his humble negative. “I don’t
believe in sending the message to this Figger-rovv,
or whatever he’s called,” he said ; “I've got no
faith in these French officials. I think the message
ouglit to be sent to one of the newspapers.’’ The
aitchless M.L.C. was evidently under the impres-
sion that Fioako was some sort of a potentate,
like the Rajah or the Mikado. Another eminent
M.L.C. aired his wisdom and his statistics in the
Legislature, last week, on population. “ The
deaths during 1898,” he said, “ were nought hun-
dred and noughty nought, including those of
children, while the births during the same period
were 1000 less— also includinu those of children.”
Nobody laughed. “And these be thy gods, 0
Israel.”
“A Soldier and a Man ” was constructed on the
plan
Of a dozen melo-dramas where the British Flag
conies in,
And we knew the other night, when we saw the
hero “tight,”
He would have to fight for Hingland, and be
cleansed of his sin
By the blood of many supers
Who presume to face the troopers
Of the only blessed army that can ever hope to
win—
Of the blessed British Army that is ever bound to
win.
A Soldier and a Man, when the narrative began,
Was accused of crooked dealings in a quiet game
of Nap.,
Which made Lis credit shrink, till he drowned his
grief in drink,
And his wife said “ Don’t be selfish, let me liquor
too, old chap,”
For the tempting glass was handy :
But he flung away the brandy,
And a girl reuruitiug-sorgeant pinned some ribbon
to his cap—
Pinned some temperance blue ribbon to the
brandy-waster’s cap.
To South Africa he went, whore a wicked mining
gent
Tried to get him into trouble whilst ho battled for
the Queen;
But the villain always fails in these illustratod
tales—
O, the Truth prevails o’er him in the good old
final scene.
And this hero’s faithful missis
Came and smothered him with kisses,
His innocence was proven, and we left ’em both
serene :
You can bet your bottom dollar that we left ’em
all serene.
’Tis a wcll-appointod show, so we think it ought
to go
With the Melbourne Royal public, who are fond
of Love and War ;
The heroine, wo guess, scored a pretty big success;
She oan quite express tier feelings, and sue fairly
holds tho floor.
Likewise hero John D. Saunders
Is a man who seldom maunders,
And he shmethas a shoddor of tho lurking Zulus’
gore—
He’s a free-and-easy shodder of the luckless
heathen’s gore.
Shall the truth bo bluntly told, re tho pauper-
mother old,
Who suddenly deceases when she’s seated on a
chair ?
We prefer to droop our head in the presence of
the dead,
And forget her stagey accent and hor artificial
liair;
And the sergeant-girl-recruiter !
Not at present will we shoot her
With our popgun of displeasure—for we haven’t
time to spare,
We could paragraph her badly—but we haven’t
time to spare.
Our life is but a span, and “A Soldier and a
Man ”
Provides a brief distraction for our weary, worldly
mind,
We have sampled better plays in the bygone
Saturdays,
But a piece that pays the management has virtue
of a kind.
Brief our life of joy and sorrow,
Here to-day, and gone to-morrow ;
To meet what next may happen we are patiently
resigned,
And the more we meet this drama, well, the more
we’ll be resigned.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Soddon Govt, has started to truckle a little
harder to that easy • going public sentiment which
demands railways and other public works, and doesn't
want to pay for them, so a £1,000,000 Loan Hill has gone
through Maoriland Parliament with no opposition worth
mentioning. In this matter the Seddon Govt, is foolishly
throwing away a splendid opportunity. There are
£2.700,000 of gold lying idle, and earning nothing, and
doing no possible good to anybody, in the M. 1,. banks.
And, if Maoriland bar! courage enough to adopt the Cana-
dian law, which has been proved workable by many
years’ service, it would enact that the hanks must keep 40
percent, of their reserves in legal-tender State notes, and
thereby it would get Jtl.loo.ono of this money as a per-
petual loan, without any interest at all. and without
doing the least damage to anybody. And within the
next few years, as population and the stock of money
increased, it wotihi probably get another million on the
same terms. The money Maoriland wants to complete its
three big trunk railways is lying at its door waiting to
be picked up gratis—and the Seddon crowd hasn't the
courage or the energy to pick it up.

The Modern Permanent P.uilding Society (Mel-
bourne) got to the end of its financial year on .list July,
1809, with a loss of 1.15,469. The last eight years of this
rather disastrous concern have been as follows :
July, 1892—Profit.. £16,752 July, 1896—Loss .. £11,431
„ 1893 „ .. 51 JS: 17— „ .. 7.511
„ 1894-Loss .. 13.800 „ IS9S „ .. 10.343
„ 1895 „ .. 2,030 „ IS99 „ .. 15.4C9
This unhappy episode finally extinguishes the reserve
fund and leaves a debit of £033 against capital. Vet,
despite the melancholy outward aspect of things, the
position appears to have improved a good deal during the
past year. The loss incurred bv sales of property below
its cost to the society was £15,750; so apart from this
item the institution paid its way and had a profit of £231.
During the last three years the profits and losses, apart
from losses on properties sold below book-value, have
been as follows :
July, 1897 ... Loss £1193 I July, 1599 ... Profit £231
„ 1898 ... „ 1671 I
Taking all things into consideration, it is doubt-
ful if the Modern Permanent is doing well to force its
properties on the market on such disastrous terms just
now. It is set forth in the balance-sheet that rents are
going up in Melbourne, and property values are increas-
ing, and the fact that the institution is now earning
enough to pay interest on its deposits and all working-
expenses strongly corrobotafes the statement. The
returns for 1899 are fully £1950 better than those for
1898. There is no obligation to pay more than 2 k per
cent, to depositors until 1903, unless it is earned—and it
needn't- he earned ; surplus profits can he used to write off
depreciation without selling the property. Also, there is
no obligation to start repaying the principle of the de-
posits till 1902 or 1903. Under these conditions, it looks
as if the policy of selling at heavy loss just now, instead
of nursing the assets for a year or so is ill-advised. The
Modern owed £438,172 of deposits and debentures in
1597, and £420,522 in 1898, and now it owes £399,773, so it
hast) t made much of a reduction so far despite the big
sacrifice caused by dumping its assets on a debilitated
market.

* * * ,
The wholesale beer-fiends of Australia certainly
do “ go it strong.” In a case recently heard in the Sydney
courts Tooth and Co. had advanced £2309 on mortgage
of a hotel at Alexandria with the usual condition that the
borrower should buy from them only. A dispute having
arisen, they wanted the court to decide that the house
was “ tied ” for ever and ever in terms of the deed—that
even when the loan was ancient history the borrower and
all his successors, so long as the hotel stood (for hundreds
of years if the hotel stood so long), were hound to buy
Tooth's beer only ! And it was quite a shock to the tieci-
honse industry when the law decided that if the borrower
paid off the loan and interest lie would thereafter cease
to be an enslaved heer-seller, and could buy his beer
wherever lie pleased.

Apropos the outsiders’ (mostly Britishers) howl
about the iniquity of the Boers’ div.-tax, &o. A popular
contention is that, the fact that the mines were left to
foreigners to work proves the Boers unfit to own the
country. The Straits Settlements are British ; but out-
siders own nearly all of the tin-mines. Vet there is no
proposal that Britain should clear out. (The position in
the Northern Territory, by the way, is much the same).
Also, the Govt, in the Straits levies a duty of 13 per cent,
on mineral exports. The Master Miners’ Association’,
mainly Chinese, has been vigorously objecting to that for
months past; hut there lias been no cable about it. Quite
likely, however, there have been crushing remarks in the
Chinese papers ; and who knows but that some Ctiinese
volunteer majahs haven't been offering their services in
the matter?

Several of the Southern moneyed crowd are
already standing on velvet over the recovery of land-
prices. Three or four years bock city and suburban pro-
perty fell to prices as ridiculously low as they were high
in boom-times and those who were not panic-stricken
took advantage of the opportunity. One of these snapped
a block offered to the indigent writer for £5 10s. a foot
(it went for £4O in ’88), and last week lie got out at £l2
10s.—a profit of over 120 per cent, in three years.

The success of the (mostly German) Australian
Met.al Co. in handling the 15.11. zinc “ tailings” by mag-
netic concentration, with tlie primary object of reducing
the bulk prior to export, has turned all 15. H. managers’
heads that way for ihe moment. The managements of
the Central (Sulphide Corporation), Block 14, South,
North and Junction North are already represented in
patent applications ; and an ex-manager of the Pinnacles
(Odling) is also an applicant—he has been experimenting
on these lines for seven or eivht years, and may thus lie
regarded as the pioneer of the idea as applied to 8.11.
sulphides. Apparently there is going to be a big fight
over the patent applications, in which the 8.11. Prop,
will take a hand—as an objector, it is understood.

Broken Hill mine employes have long com-
plained that, they were repulsed when they sought to
submit ideas for improving ore-treating methods. Lately,
however, probably in view of the very modified success of
all processes so far tried, more encouragement has been
given to the rank and file ; and the comparatively new
general manager of the 15.1 f. Prop, lias now capped this
by offering a substantial reward for any information or
suggestion which may lead to improved methods either
of treating Ihe ores or working the mine. As many old
Cornish miners do know a little about ore-dressing, this
may lead to something.

“Traveller”: Here’s an abuse for the new
N.S. W. Mines Minister Began to put his foot down on. A
few weeks ago I secured a dredging-lease on a southern
river, and paid thp Government survey fee, £6. This
week I am waited upon by the Govt, surveyor for said
district who kindly informs me i must pay him ‘ 25 addi-
tional fee hefore he will start to work. I remonstrate,
and ask him what penally non-payment of this demand
entails. “Oh ! just waiting.” he replies. “ How long?”
1 ask. “ Years—perhaps for ever,” is his reassuring
reply.

The Turon "River dredging business is the latest
mining outrage on N.S. Wales. The weekly tip-
ster kept the game of boom up to the very last.
A gentleman had been up to see the dredge said
everything was very satisfactory, and people were
advised to go in as a solid and permanent investment.
Well here’s the result:—“ Gold taken off the mats during
the opening-out operations to 22nd July, 930 z. Four
weeks from 24th July to 19th August, from mats, 1520 z.,
or 3Soz. per week. Cleaned-up bo!tom of paddock,
dredged between 24th July and 19th August, Ooz.
Total gold won, 2510 z., worth about £3 18s. per
oz.” Suppose the co. has been at work for
10 weeks, and it is more than that, the return
represents 250 z. a week. This from a show whose
market - value has been varying from £75,000 to
£100,000! S.M. Herald says, in a skim-milk fashion;
“ Tlie result was received on 'Change with some expres-
sions of disappointment.” The D.T. says the return is
disappointing as regards the bnt'om of the.paddock, hut
the return from the gravelly na-h shows a good margin
of profit, Great Scott!—less than £lOOO worth of gold.
Why, IOOOoz. would have been a disappointment after all
tlie months of newspaper-booming this dredging-scheme
has received. However, good may come from evil. This
Turon racket, may act as a wholesome tonic on those dis-
posed to go dredging-mad.

From Collar :—Occidental talks of increasing its
crushing power shortly. The new portions of the cyanide
plant have been put to work, so that the Occidental
should win twice as much gold as formerly.
Mt. Drysdale has made a 3d. call, to pay for its
cyanide plant. .It has yet to be proved that cyanidation
will be a success there. The Mount Drysdale tank does
not hold enough water to keep the plant going for a
couple of months, so that its only hope is to use the mine
water, and mine water is not always suitable for such
purposes.
¥ ¥ * *
The Bulletin office often has a lot of ore in
sight, and though the practice of sending it in has never
been encouraged, this paper receives too many requests
for gratuitous assays and opinions as to value and treat-
ment, of samples submitted. Arrangements have now
been made for testing, by assay and otherwise, samples of
ore, provided the sum of £1 is sent with each to cover
cost. Assays alone do not serve the miner’s purpose,
hence certificates supplied to Bulletin correspondents
will not, only give a statement of metallic contents, but
owners also will be advised by fin experienced, practical
metallurgist as to the best methods of treating the ore in
bulk, whether by wet or dry crushing and amalgamation,
smelting, c.vaniding, chlorinating, etc. Parcels should
not lie less than lib. nor more than 101 b.

Dear Bulletin.—Your correspondent “Gnome”
has not exaggerated in the least the condition of affairs
at Webb’s Mine, Gmmavilie. There is one aspect of the
case which he has not touched upon. Some 18 months
ago a number of Emmaville residents, being dissatisfied
with the old Medical Association (the one to which
“ Gnome ” refers) started a new fund. In spite of every
obstacle, the new association has thrived, and it even now
threatens to swamp its older rival. At Webb’s, as
elsewhere in the district, the new fund claims a
goodly number of supporters (or rather would-be sup-
porters), hut none dare join, as the ukase is plain enough
—“ Bay into the old fund or quit! ” Don’t you think it is
as great a piece of tyranny to compel men to support a
certain doctor when they prefer another as to enforce
their attendance on Sunday at a particu'ar religious
establishment alien to their own beliefs? —Pluto.
“Malle”: Rc Bulletin’s remarks (2/9,/’99) on
Woodlark Island. Writer has no interest therein, but
knows something of it. Lode is 15ft. wide, partly de-
composed, and by simply breaking it down and running
it through sluice-boxes the discoverers have won some
thousands of pounds' worth of gold during past two
years. The parcel spoken of as “ 14cwt. of concentrates
which returned lloz.,” were not concentrates at all, but
headings thrown from boxes. Also, 1 ton 18cwt. ore re-
cently sent to Aldershot, Q., returned OOoz. gold. Lode
is traceable for a considerable distance, and a Sydney co.
has secured 30 acres joining the prospector’s ; but as the
sensational returns come from the latter’s ground, the
Sydney people’s block may or may not be worthless. A
curiosity in this find is that coral comes right up to the
footwall of lode. Apropos, there are rumors current in
Sydney which it may he worth while for shareholders to
investigate concerning an alleged serious dispute over the
flotation of the Woodlark show.
The Anaconda, the world’s greatest copper-
mine, is said to be weakening ; it is so gigantic an affair
that the opening of many new rich mines would not com-
pensate for its falling-off. For some years its production
(350 tons of refined copper per day) represented one-fifth
of the world’s output.
• • • •
Another steadier for the copper - boomsters is
aluminium, which American electric-power people are in-
troducing in big quantities. One Chicago co. has given
orders for 150,0001 b. of feeder-wire ; and that would go as
far as three times the quantity of copper. Also, one
reason why Straits Settlements tin-producers are hanging
on to their stocks is the statement, widely published
locally, that a process is incubating for displacing copper
by tin in certain electrical appliances. How that is to
work, though, with tin at present prices isn't explained.

“ A Shareholder,” writing to Sydney D.T., falls
into the error of thinking' that Mt. Garnet Freehold Co.,
is trying to get ri hfs from Q. Parliament to build a rail-
way from the Chillagoe line to Mt. Garnet. It is the
Chillagoe co. which is pursuing the proposed concession.
But otherwise “ Shareholder” is all right when he says
that “ the building of the 38 to 40 miles of line from Mt.
Garnet to Wades’ will certainly provide a large additional
traffic for the Chillagoe line, hut it will provide that traffic
at the expense of the Mt. Garnet shareholders who will be
forced to pay freight on 30 additional and unnecessary
miles.” The route from Mareeha is the route, and as
Govt, proposes to construct the line to Atherton, the pro-
posal of the Chillagoe co. to build a roundabout branch
from their line is simply a very large job. Syndicate
railways are just the things for a country nnt to have, but
if a country must have syndicates two syndicates are
better than one. And the Chillagoe Railway and Mines
co. has bitten off all it can chew, ahvhow.

Mt. Garnet Freehold’s first half-yearly report
shows a much more solid financial position than most
Chillagoe companies occupy. Cash at bankers on June 30
totalled £32,990; and £4904 of the fourth call was then
outstanding. Since that date “ nearly the whole of the
fourth call has been collected,” so that bank balance*
have been little affected by subsequent work. Though
the reports are copious there is still little by, which one
may judge the permanent value of tbe co.’s numerous
“shows.” No mine can be really tested until shafts or
winzes have been sunk ; tunnels and drives therefrom
are of much less value. The assay variations in the two
winze* sunk are therefore the most important part per-
haps of the report—the scores of other assays may be
passed. In No. 1 winze, at. 30ft. (at which point the first
samples were taken by the co.) the return was 7.8 per
cent, copper, and 6oz. 12dwt. silver. There was a steady
fall to 2.2 per cent, copper and 4oz. lfidwt. silver at 46ft. ;
but in the last fathom the return went up to 2.6 per cent,
copper, and 19oz. 12dwt. silver. In No. 2 winze the first
return was 7 per cent, copper and Boz. fidwt. silver; at
the bottom, 33ft., the stone {rives 5.1 per cent, copper and
13oz. sdwt. silver. In the latter winze, however, the
quality is exceedingly variable. Mine-manager Potter,
while admitting' that they “ cannot estimate with accu-
racy the quantity of ore available,” does not think he is
assuming too much in setting down 200,000 tons as the
approximate quantity.
Vic. can still provide some alluring specimen-
stone ; a couple of mates at Mansfield, in the Goulburn
Valley district, have just crushed 55 tons for 5330 z.

They are humorists in Westralia. A deputa-
tion of people with low-grade mines lately asked Premier
Forrest to reduce the railway freights on poor ore,
alleging that some of it couldn’t stand the existing
charges. Forrest said that if lie did he would have to
raise the freights on rich ore to make up the loss, and the
deputation (which had no rich ore of its own) cheerfully
assented. Then the Government thought the matter out
and raised the freights on rich ore, but didn’t reduce
those on the other kind. And it says that the owners of
poor ore should be satisfied with the new arrangement,
because their product now goes so much more cheaply
than the rich stone.

The penny ’bus has come to Sydney, and
the owner of one loose brown can now ride all the
way from Redfern Station to Circular Quay.
From the tired person’s point of view, that is an
excellent innovation, but look at it from the other
fellow’s point <Jf view. The average ’bus carries
23 people inside and out. It is supposed to take
23 minutes to travel from Circular Quay to Red-
fern ; as a matter of fact, it takes longer on
account of the many stoppages and blocks in the
narrow, crowded thoroughfares, and it generally
occupies quite the full hour on the round trip.
Bet ween 8 and 9 a.m. and 5 and 6 p.m. the ’busses
do good business, but for the rest of the day they
seldom get anywhere near a full load. If they
had a full load every time they would take in
round figures 4s. per hour. But they don’t take
2s. And out of that is supposed to come driver’s
wages, feed, and wear and tear erf at least four
horses (even the hardest walloping can’t make the
same two skeletons run with the huge ’bus for 15
hours out of each 24), wear and tear of ’bus, in-
terest on cost, stable rent, groom’s wages, and
sundries. The horses are run off their legs, the
drivers work hideous hours for next to nothing a
week, and the whole business is a cheap penny
infamy which should be suppressed by law.
• » * •
There is a strong spice of British hypo-
crisy in the Dreyfus indignation. “Such a thing
could not possibly occur in England,’’ say the
papers. But within the present generation the
Bradlaugh persecution was very much akin to it.
Bradlaugh for six years was harassed, brutally ill-
used, slandered, kept out of his legal rights,
financially ruined, and his strong frame shattered
till he died in his prime, just and only because his
beliefs were antagonistic to pious England. To
all intents and purposes Bradlaugh was perse-
cuted to death. And, oddly, it was from France
in those days that Bradlaugh received most sym-
pathy ; leading Frenchmen like Clemenceau and
Yves Guyot were among his staunchest friends.

A few years ago a spiritualistic leaflet was
published by a Melb. medium giving a description
of Heaven as dictated by a literary angel. In the
course of its narrative the ghostly one mentioned
that a relative who on earth had been named
Godfrey was known upon the golden shore as
yFrey,” the syllable “god” being considered
irreverent in such close proximity to the Supreme
Being It would therefore appear that English,
besides being the prospective language of all the
earth, is already the vernacular of the Better
Laud, and that to all intents and purposes the
Kingdom of Heaven is also under this muoh-
talked-of “British suzerainty.”

Screamingly funny was the despatch from
Yea (Vic.) of a cable to Paris Figaro stating that
the public of the former mighty centre of popula-
tion “deplores” the verdict in the Dreyfus case.
Yea—consisting, as it does, mostly of two stores,
a pub., and an army of goats—evidently con-
siders that its yea shall be yea, and its nay, nay.

Melbourne Argus, referring to the Ther-
mopylae wreck, specifies 19 saloon-passengers by
name, then casually observes that “ there were
also 30 passengers in the steerage.” Further on
it gives at length the pedigrees and histories of
the two racehorses that were on board, sympa-
thetically relating that “ Chesney’s backers were
disappointed when he was withdrawn from his
er !Pf a gcmentb,” but it relieves public apprehension
with the information that “the horses were housed
in very comfortable boxes ”; and places upon
record the important historical fact that “the
horses were in charge of J. Bloomfield, who took
Patron, the Melbourne Cup winner, home.” The
account finishes-up with such car«o-details as
“ 2030 carcases mutton. ” And it’s quite surpris-
ing that “ also 30 steerage-passengers ” were given
precedence over “two valuable racehorses,” and
that the 30 unspecified live humans were men-
tioned before the 2030 defunct muttons.

A bright blue light was let in on the
“ housekeeper ” industry by the recent Adelaide
case in which a veteran of 78 was sued for breach
of promise to marry his housekeeper. Plaintiff
•admitted in cross-exam, that she had been married
twice already, that defendant had arranged to
marry her after only 4 days’acquaintance, and that
he stood in bodily fear of both her and his previous
housekeeper, who had turned up and was threat-
ening to make things lively all round. The poor
dear young thing (of 57) was non-suited.
‘ Did Lorimer know his dog was danger-
ous ? asked Justice a’Beekett of the jury in the
Melbourne dog-biting case of Chase v. Lorimer.
Little wonder his Honor bad afterwards to regard
the jury’s verdict as unsatisfactory. If a dog
bites a sheep tiie sheep-owner can claim and will
obtain damages without having to prove previous
bad character against the dog; but the dog law
practically allows the animal his first mouthful of
human flesh free—one of the absurdest laws of a
frequently absurd code.
A bit of bad luck lately happened to a
nice, elderly gentleman prominent in political
movements for the Encouragement of
Morality. Another elderly gentleman
conspicuous in the same' circles tells
the story against him, naught extenu-
ating. The latter, whose name is
not Brown, answered a late knock at
the door of his suburban villa and
found the former, whose name is not
Jones, standing on the mat. Jones
greeted Brown with a glare of sur-
prise, but stepped into the hall unin-
vited and began to divest himself of
nis overcoat. All he said was:
“Great Scott I Jones, who’d ever
have thought of meeting you here ! ”
Jones assumed the attitude of a
stern moralist and answered: “Is
there anything remarkable in that,
Mr. Brown? I live here—shifted
in yesterday morning.” Brown left
hurriedly, and Jones at onco insti.
tuted inquiries into the characters of
the former occupants of his house.
And—well, there you are.

Tasmania’s licensing law-provision that no
female shall work in a bar after 10 p.m., had long
been a dead letter until recently the police were
taken with a moral spasm and got a lot of hotel-
keepers fined over the matter. Petitions are now
being signed asking Parliament to amend this
part of the Act, and it was thought that a petition
from the barmaids themselves (to be presented by
Gilmore, the ladies’ man of Tas. Parliament)
would carry much weight. So a meeting of the
profession was called in Launceston, but only one
solitary damsel attended. But why should any
barmaid wish to work after 10 p.m. ?

“ A Struggler ” to The Bulletin :
Nobody seems to have pointed-out what a serious evil
these charitable bazaars and fancy-fairs are to local busi-
ness people. Money and goods are continually being
appealed for by customers whom it is difficult to refuse,
and, in addition, our business suffers while the bazaar
booms. In this Melbourne suburb (admittedly not a pros-
perous one), we have just had the third dance within a
few weeks in aid of a Church-bazaar, the promoters of
which are making it a point to “cadge” everything in
connection therewith. The charity of religious bodies
appear to me to stop severely at home.
In Perth (W.A.), the W.C.T.U. runs what
it calls the Leisure Hour Club, into which a
billiard-table was some time back introduced.
But, as it hasn’t been paying, the Union
has all at once discovered that billiards is a
demoralising game, anyhow, and that the com-
mittee deserves severe censure for the innovation.
Woman is a “ hard citizen ” where the bawbees
are concerned.
* * * *
At recent Randwick Spring Meeting an
edifying spectacle was a country J. P., in deafen-
ing raiment and with full name on betting-bag—
bawling the odds with the best of them. When
this potentate returns to his district, with what
front will he adjudicate upon some miserable
3-card spieler, bowled-out at the local races ! It
would indeed be the unkindest cut of all to any of
the take-down fraternity to be “ sent-un ” by a
bookie.
* * • •
M.L. Chief Justice Stout has not made a
particularly bright start as a criminal Judge.
After declaring that he viewed offences against
the person as more heinous than those against
property, he sentenced a man convicted of a
murderous assault to 18 months’ gaol, while
another for stealing goods worth about .£2O got
four years’ quod. The latter, of course, was an
old offender; but a free-thinking Radical like
Stout should know better than punish a man for
his former bad character.

I am not a believer in this preaching: to men only or to

audience of men that I would not care to say to mj' wife,
my mother, or any other woman.
Which is rough on the ever-increasing number of
his brethren who, to attract an audience, have to
fall back on sexual suggestiveness !

The other night the big Sydney firm of
Wood—it trades in wooden overcoats enter-
tained its employes at a smoke concert. The
affair was characterised by the overflowing animal
spirits and rollicking hilarity which ever dis-
tinguishes that light-hearted butterfly-in-black,
the undertaker, from his fellow mortal. A feature
of the evening’s entertainment was “a comio
singing competition among employes—the first
prize a whip.” A hearse-whip, no doubt —the
feverish hurry of the age having at length reached
that erstwhile easy-going humorist, the hearse-
driver. Some Bulletin verses for the next coffin-
makers’ spree :
At Melb. City Court, the other day, an all-
out blind old man (he claimed to be 66 and looked
it) was charged with begging. He said that he
had not long left the Benevolent Asylum, that he
lived by playing a concertina, and that the 265.
found on him represented six months’ savings.
The police-inspector’s suggestion that the ancient
should get another “show” made his Worship
(Mayor M‘Eacharn) snort. “ I don’t see why he
should get a show,” that awful person said.
" hy should he have gone to the asylum while
lie had money?” The old man pleaded that the
little fund was for providing an occasional butter
accompaniment to the regulation dry bread, and
craved for a little sympathy—which the well-fed
M'Eacharn promptly extended to him in the
shape of a month’s gaol. And The Bulletin
isn t sure that he didn’t almost deserve it for
expecting “sympathy” from a smug and
paunchy cit.
» * * •
Fine feathers don’t always “fetch” woman.
The evidence in a recent Melb. divorce-case set
forth that petitioner’s husband, after a continued
course of blacking her eyes and dragging her
around by the hair, had vanished one morning
and remained untraced until information reached
petitioner that the missing one was living with
another woman in a distant town, and had airily
inquired of petitioner’s sister “how Julia” (the
petitioner) “ was getting on ? ” He “ rose ” upon
even this, however, later on, when—the provi-
sional spouse having died—he promptly proposed
to the original Lady of the Blows that'she should
come along and resume relations, setting out as
an inducement that the other woman had left
abundance of fine garments “just to your taste.”
The lady was not taking any, however, and sue-
ceeded in getting her divorce.
* * * *
An Oamaru (M.L.) beak has shed a ray of
joy on the. path of the “prohibited” drunk by
deciding that a railuayrefresliment-room is not
a licensed house “within the meaning of the
Act.” So another law goes to pieces through
the inveterate legal habit of deciding that
wherever there is room lor doubt it shall
always be assumed that the framers of the
law meant the most unlikely thing possible.
And pending repairs to the statute, that
customary official notification that is hung
up all over the average Maoriland pub.,
“ You are warned not to supply any drink
to John Smith,” will be of 'little effect
wherever a railway refreshment-room is
available. For the prohibited Smith can
drink himself blind there, and then get
comfortably run over by the express till fur-
ther notice.

• * * *
The recent flogging of two prisoners in
Bathurst gaol for alleged insubordination was
surrounded with such secresy that only a few
even of the officials knew when it took place.
Such childish mystery-mongering would be absurd
if it were not a part of the iniquitous system by
which a man already in gaol for one offence can
be secretly arraigned fer another, and brutally
punished upon the order of an irresponsible
official. No community of such barbarous methods
has any business with the term “civilised.”
* * « *
A prisoner transferred from Bathurst
gaol to Sydney gaol last week opened his shirt on
Bathurst railway platform and, with vituperative
comments, exhibited to the crowd the surgical
bandages placed around his body after the aforesaid
flagging for an alleged infraction of discipline in
gaol. The prisoner exclaimed against the mode of
trial which adjudged his flogging. Offences
against gaol discipline are tried by what is really
a secret tribunal, and secret tribunals for the
punishment of offences in gaol or out are among
the curses of civilisation.

The Bulletin s exposure of the conspiracy
of silence in barrister Gregory’s case seems to have
shamed Melb. Age and Argus into mentioning
last week “an application by an inmate of Kevv
Lunatic Asylum for his release from confinement.”
Neither of these “ respectable ” journals brea'. lied
a whisper as to the name, legal position, or particu-
lar delusions of the lunatic, but they were careful
to explain that Judge Hodges had been convinced
of his insanity. Had his Honor’s decision been
favorable to poor Gregory it would probably have
been kept as dark as the previous proceedings in
this case. All the Vic. Supreme Court Judges
have now considered Gregory in their official
capacities (in their private capacities they were
always well acquainted with his history) and none
of them can find him other than insane. Gregory
talks Law with more than ordinary cleverness
and soundness of reasoning, but he needs very
.ittle inducement to betray his monomania

Chief-Justice Darley, endeavoring to quash
a case in which a R.C. plaintiff applied for
damages against his R.C. parish-priest, for slander
and assault, said from the bench that a law-court
was not the place for trying such charges. His
Honor meant, of course, a secular law-court. In
that dictum, he is quite in accord with R.C.
canonical law. Time was when no such action
dare go before a lay tribunal. If it did, plaintiff,
•Tudges, and all concerned would fall under heavy
penalties.

During that recent London wife-desertion
hearing against one M’Gregor, complainant’s
lawyer said that—
The representative of the Ajjent-General of Victoria in
Loudon had informed him that while it was true that a
Bill was introduced into the Vic. Parliament annulling
the marriages solemnised by the Rev. Nathanial Kins-
man, yet for the sake of public policy such marriages
were looked upon as valid.
It would be interesting to know what “represen-
tative” of the Vic. A.gent-G- meral made that
absurd statement. Whatever may be thought
otherwise of Kinsman’s marriages, they are un-
questionably valid. The trouble in the case was
that plaintiff had no proper proof of her marriage.
* * • «
Education-Minister Butler got up in S.A.
Assembly the other day to give some information
about wine-growing. Somehow or other he got
his figures mixed, and what ought to have been
gallons became acres, and a confused House was
soon listening to statistics showing that S.A. had
grown =£48,000 gallons 2s. Gd. worth of vines in
383 years at a cost of three acres per gal. The
abstemious Premier Kingston then started to
audit his colleague’s figures, but got mixed up be-
tween the light and dark wines, and the only con-
clusion possible was that S.A. has been growing
vines for 40 years when soda and brandy would
have been more profitable. By the time the dis-
cussion had finished the House’s stock inebriate,
who has been going square for over a month, had
become paralytic on ginger ale and had to be
hung out to dry.
* * • •
In a recent Melbourne wife-desertion case,
the lady (19 years of age) took everything “in her
stride ’’ in the following hair-raising manner :
“You admit having committed adultery with
Blank?” “Yes, sir.” “ Have you misconducted
yourself with a man named Dash ? ” “ Yes,
sir.” “Did your husband catch a man in the
house with you one night after the alleged deser-
tion, and give him a severe battering? ” “ Yes,
sir.” The best thing this candid young person
can do is to get a new husband and drop the
subject.

The struggle for a crust. “ Holdar M.” :
Re Sydney boarding-houses. I lately received a
hatful of replies to an advt. for bachelor accommo-
dation—some of ’em distinctly inviting. One
obviously illiterate lady wrote to the effect that
she was a young, blithesome widow as per en-
closed photo (in evening dress) and would be glad
if advertiser would call and confer. Had in the
house only her one child and an elderly servant,
so that advertiser could have her undivided
attention. Another replier said she kept an
accomplished lady-help who would gratuitously
teach the gentleman musio and singing. Others
offered tuition in French, the violin, dancing,
shorthand (and in one instance in manicurin')) as
incentives to share their homes at an average rate
of £1 per week.

* • * •
The hard-headed Aberdeen court took a
calm, reasonable view of the Cemetery Desecra-
tion case, in which a chief sexton was found
guilty of “ exhuming corpses on the night after
burial, and huddling them with gross indignity
into pits dug in the walks of the cemetery, or
burning them in the tool-house.” Allowing for
the fact that a sexton’s business destroys all his
sentimental respect for corpses, and that what
seems “ horrible ” to human vanity is not horrible
to a cemetery official, the Scotch tribunal let this
shocking materialist off with three months’ im-
prisonment, After all, he was a public benefactor,
only the public don’t know it. He has brought
graveyard-mourning into ridicule, and suggested
the sanitary advantages of cremation in a tool-
house.
* * * *
After this, it is time to let the hairy-man
discussion slide. Writes “ Ben Sun ”: —
“Puyakka" (8., 9 9’99) asserts (1) that hair isn’t an
indication of strength ; and (2) that the hail-appreciating
female is a retrogression. As for the first, the present
writer “ wallops out a crust ” amongst, those with whom
strength is absolutely necessary—not the cultivated
strength of the athlete, but the staying power of the
navvy, the wood-splitter, or the quarryman, and he
would back the stamina of the hirsute fellow every time,
even if the great preponderance of such in those callings
did not go far to establish his physical superiority.
As to the second point, if, as the exponents of
evolution assert, its trend is mainly forwards, and if
“Puyakka’s” argument is logical the “relative fre-
quency” of the hirsute homo is entirely inexplicable.
Again—“ The average healthy woman possesses a rooted
dislike to the sight of abnormally hairy limbs or
chest.” Dogmatic—but fallacious in crediting woman
with an artistic delicacy of taste which she doesn’t
possess, and because it doesn’t take into account the
demonstrated attraction of opposites. As for the racial
theory, its value depends on the extent of its possible
application. The African pigmy’s intellect is as yet a
practically unknown quantity, and his skin-growth is, in
any case, more wool than hair. Moreover, the absurdity
of saying that the Montenegrin is “ below the Northern
Chinaman in intellect and physique” is probably due to
the fact that in the Levant people only meet with the
Montenegrin debased to the level of his rascally sur
roundings.

A bush teacher to The Bulletin :
Your remarks (2/9/'99) re “ franking •• M.P.’s letters
lead me to ask you to state the case of N.S. W. bush peda-
gogues. We get two packets (total 50) of O.S. envelopes
per annum, and our 62 regular yearly, returns take (at the
least) 51, since envelopes may be double-banked in some
cases. Thus we have a quantity of “minus” to serve
for all the other official correspondence, and have not
only ourselves to bu.v the requisite large envelopes, but
also to stamp them like private correspondence. In the
Railway and Postal Departments an ample supply is
given. Why the difference ?

Angora-goat flesh—masquerading as mut-

When right before some dark'ned door
Our sable team doth stay,
From Toil and Care some party there ’
It softly bears away.
Then let the glass go gaily round,
And toast the Party homeward-bound !
The house to which both poor and rich
We bear is quite secure—
No bailiffs dare to enter there
To seize the furniture.
There comes unto its quiet door—
One judgment summons, and no more !
Then toast the Mute with white cravat,
So jaunty and so spry—
And saucy weepers round his hat,
And moist and mellow eye.
And drink their health in one more round,
Our kindly patrons underground.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, September 19, 1899.
My Dear Mtee, —
Blue tickets and white tickets again at Lord
Beauchamp’s second ball. But people had grown
used to the seidlitz-powder entertainments, and
the fizz was much more subdued. About 450
guests assembled. This ball differed from the
first in several ways. In the first place, a list of
those who were not there would not be quite so
interesting as a similar list about the first hop, for
everybody seemed to be present. There were few
glaring omissions noticeable to anyone there,
whatever people who stayed away might have ob-
served. After seeing the garden-party and the
two balls, it seems that the invitations were not as
frightfully far-fetched as i>eople said, but the
usual Govt. House men were very few and far
between at both balls.
Certainly, things are not as they used to be at
Govt. House. Never before in its chequered
career (not for a long, long, quite-forgotten time,
anyway) has Sydney Society been watched and
shadowed, and asked to stand back, and not go
there, and not to enter by that door, and—in a
whisper this last—not to take two turns at the
Cattles cn Cronstadt.
The wounds of one well-known citizen are un-
speakably painful. He took in to supper a lady
who asked him presently to go and find her
daughter, a debutante, and bring her in also. He
went out of the supper-room, found the debutante,
and was returning with her on his arm when an
Aide, stationed outside the supper-room, swooped
down on him. “Pardon me, you have been in
before ! ”
If you are mathematician enough to add that to
Birthstains, you will find the result is almost im-
possible.
Society was horribly surprised and upset
and insulted and yet what happened at
the swagger polo-tea that very afternoon ?
The guests swarmed the tea-tables, rushed
the strawljerrios and cream, demolished ices,
cakes, and tea, and reduced the tables to
desolation. The hostesses, Mrs. W. B. Mitchell
and Mrs. Lassetter, hadn't thought it necessary to
keep guard over the food, and were watching to
receive His Ex. and party. At 4 o’clock arrived on
the scene the Gov. and the Admiral. They were led
into the tea-room where the vulgah crowd was just
completing its work of demolition and—well,
luckily they could not get near the tables. His
Ex.’s eye has been cultivated for ruins, but it was
a sickly moment for the hostesses. On the even-
ing of that same auspicious day, Govt. House is
charged with keeping watch over its feed—and no
wonder. If all these things are known in Heaven,
and made manifest among the angels, there isn’t
much inducement to be good and get there when
you die.
Gove. House ball-room has become a thing of
beauty, with its testhetic music balcony and soft,
yellow pairing. Under the balcony, along the
wall, runs a divan where Potts Point tries hard to
take its seat. The aides were very busy here,
asking ]>eople to move aside, and stand out of
somebody’s superior light. It seems a pretentious
and unnecessary thing to do, but if there’s a pre-
cedent for it at the Court of Her Gracious, let us
bow and be thankful we are being taught some-
thing that would never have occurred to our
natural understanding.
People are saying oracularly “Something will
happen.” Nobody knows definitely what the
nature of that something will be. Possibly, the
ruffles will smooth themselves out, but at present
everyone is busy writing a letter to His Ex. telling
him straight. And so Society continues to heave,
but His Ex. continues to go blandly on in his own
way. His theory of inviting people is sweetly
erratic, quite in keeping with a “ little Billee.” It
is to gather into Govt. House people who do some-
thing for others. Knowing this, it is easy to see
how the invitation lists have become so disorgan-
ised. How can a new Gov. make accurate picks
at the people who do things for others? He can-
not have an intimate knowledge of the thousands
of names in his visiting-book. He goes by what
he sees—by the'obvious. The result is that H.E.
invites broadcast the noble army of people who do
things for other people in a public and obvious
and unmistakable way, while Charity that begins
at home stays there.
The second ball was less exclusive than the
first, and more gorgeously frocked. The Some-
bodies and the Nobodies—these are the people
who need not trouble to be socially dressed.
But the In Betweens have to depend on their
outer garments, and where the In Betweens are,
there are the gowns that glitter. Lady Mary’s
ball-dress was built up of string-colored lace over
orange silk. Its quietness was its particular
feature. Lady Brassey was in grey silk trimmed
with steel embroidery, a cerise waistbeltancl heaps
of diamonds. Mrs. Brough, who arrived after
“Algy,” was in her favorite black, with pink
roses round the corsage. Mrs. Burdekin wore
white satin and diamonds. Mrs. Willie Mitchell
aired a glittering blue-grey net smothered in (lull
silver sequins, and carried a huge bouquet of pink
roses. Mrs. Tom Hughes was in a lovely white
and silver net, with touches of turquoise blue
about it. Miss Wade Brown sorted a simple,
fetching little cream breton net dress with elbow
sleeves. Miss V'iolet Somerset kept up the house-
party’s reputation for quiet frocking in a simple
black satin with white chiffon sleeves.
And, speaking of Biddies, what do you think of
the coming V.R. ball ? Tradespeople and domes-
tics. The cruellest cut that ever fell on Potts
Point’s ’aughty shoulders is that the tickets for the

Rumored—but it can’t, it really can’t, lie true—
that a very large, portentous, swagger, waxed and
waisteoated gentleman from a very fashionable
suburb, whose name is identical with a place where
they sell crockery, has been graciously invited to

For my part, I refuse to believe in anything so
rankly blasphemous. But one can’t help feeling
with the wounded Darling Pointers who have not

Saturday’s Randvvick was at the mercy of dust
and warm winds, and of a badly-managed tea-
room, where the meaning of tips they did not, or
would not, understand. Men complained that
their coin was taken and their wants ignored in
the most startling way. The supply of waitresses
was absurdly inadequate, and infuriated husbands
and fathers rushed the counters, and seized what-
ever they could get. Hopes have been held out
for a long time of new arrangements, when all the
sandwiches would be buttered, and everybody’s
sandwich would contain more than one lonely
clump of fat, but the hope is only a hope still.
The only alteration I saw was that the teacups
have been reduced in thickness from an inch to
three-fourths of an inch.
A whirl of drapery met the eye on the lawn.
Everything was light, in color and in texture.
Parasols were masses of chiffon frills, or of plain
turquoise or pink silk. These plain parasols are
much sought after,- and are often employed to
strike the one note of color. Tulle was on all the
hats. The little hats were made of tulle, the big
hats had fluttering tulle strings. There was
plenty of opportunity to observe that thesestrings
didn t suit everybody. A large face looks doubly
large with the strings to outline it. A thin face
becomes longer and thinner, and drabber, and
more like a face in a tablespoon if you tie it round
with tulle. The face for strings is a shortish,
roundish, pink and white face that is not over-
burdened with chin, and if there’s a dimple,
so much the better for the strings and everybody.
A terrible vision is a long, bony chin outside an
airy tulle bow. It is also trying to look on
diaphanous strings winding their way between
the fourth and fifth chins of a woman of forty.
In styles of frocks, polonaises led by a neck ; the
lace polonaise over silk frills, or—as in Mrs.
Lassetter’s case—the silk polonaise over a guipure
underskirt. Black and white was a good deal
worn, but chiefly in gauzy things, like black
mousseline-de-soie and black val lace. Mrs. Harry
Leyien and Mrs. Tom Watson both aired filmy
white and black muslins. Nothing looked better
than the flowered muslins with heaps of lace,
made apart from the linings, so that their airy
texture could be preserved. The bride, Miss
Henrietta Watson, who was accompanied by her
husband, was in one of these muslin gowns, a
simple little mauve and white, with a violet waist-
band, and a floppy black leghorn hat with feathers.
You have heard, of course, that Miss Watson
isn’t Miss Watson any longer. She was married
very quietly in Sydney, recently, to Mr. Rad-
cliffe, a wealthy young Englishman. Rose Mus-
grove was the only one in the secret. Mr. Rad cliff e
accompanied his bride to the races on Saturday,
and was seen to be a tall, dark young man with
a black moustache and eyeglasses.
Mrs. Brough was there, simply dressed in a red
hat and black coat and a skirt with white revers.
It is one of Mrs. Brough’s charms that she never
parades the theatrical in her out-door dressing.
Mrs. W. B. Mitchell wore her pink-rose toque
again with a white lace frock, transparent at the
neck and sleeves. Lady Brassey was in a cigar-
brown costume, with a black-and-white toque
brightened with red roses. Lady Mary wore a
simple apple-green muslin trimmed with much
lace. The Melbourne visitors were less conspicu-
ous in their dressing than on Derby-day. Mrs.
Pakenham was in a pink silk flounced with real
lace. Mrs. George Chirnside, in grey voile. Miss
Carter, in a white serge Eton costume, braided in
black, and a large black hat trimmed with red
roses. A tall, pretty Sydney girl wore a mauve
frock covered all over with coarse lace, and a
little hat made of wheat-ears. Miss Slattery
aired a light tan voile and a blue tulle toque with
long white feathers. Another pretty voile frock
was worn by Miss Dinah Cooper—grey, topped
with a little white tulle head-gear, and sweeping
pale-blue feathers.
People on the look-out for omens took their tip
from the band for the last race. Scotch airs.
“Auld Lang Syne” looked the Scottiest thing
going; and after that, “Caledonia.” Backers
rushed for both of these. Neither of them got in ;
but the omen might have been an omen all the
same. The winning horse was Glenroy.
The Mayoral “At Home’’given to H.E. and
the Admiral drew a large crowd to the Town Hall
on Monday. The Mayoress is a cheery, kind-
hearted being, who can beam on fifteen hundred
people with absolute naturalness, and if the Mayor
doesn’t find it as easy to cast off smiles nobody
expects a Mayor to be always dimpling. The
party went off crisply. The hostess received in
black net, spangled in turquoise blue, and the
host wore black and ermine. The hall was beauti-
fully fixed up with big palms and long chains of
greenery. These latter enclosed a space in the
middle of the hall, where the distinguished guests
were kept on view for a little. By the way,
why were 1500 people asked to meet the
Admiral, whom we always have with us? In
order of land precedence, the benign Chief
Justice, who doesn’t look quite like his 0!)
years, ought to have been next on the list after
11.10. But people didn't mind whom they were
asked to meet, and nobody met them who hadn’t
done it before, so the matter isn’t a burning one.
Quite a relief to touch on a topic nowadays that
isn’tscorchful. Lady Mary wore laceover anorange
robe, and Mrs. Admiral was in grey. Mr. and
Mrs. Jack Want were much focussed ; Mrs. Want
clothed her tall, dashing figure in black satin,
carried a pink bouquet, and the best coiffure seen
formany a day on any female head related to
things political. Mrs. George Reid was in white
chiffon and silk, with a wreath of pink roses in her
golden hair. One of the new Ministry caused
faint amusement by his carefully-considered way
of being always everywhere within view, and the
wife of another was mysteriously unable to recog-
nise her dear friend the greengrocer's lady. On
the whole, the “ At Home ” was the most success-
ful Mayoral function to date.
Mrs. Stoddard has been working hard at her
water-color portrait of Lady Mary. In order to
give her every opportunity of making a good
thing of it, Mrs. Stoddard was invited up to the
Sutton Forest residence where she put in a couple
of quiet weeks with the V.R. party. The portrait
will be finished just in time. Lady Mary, Lady
Bertha, Dowager Raglan, and Miss Somerset ail
depart next Saturday. His Kx. wisely keeps
dark his intentions as to how his future parties
will be hostessed, but the Chief Justicess will
probably be called in to fill the gap.
Sad to think that the Broughs’ last nights are
drawing nigh, and the time is coming when there
will be no Liars, no Algies, none of these nice,
well-groomed people saying smart things in pretty
drawing-rooms that have been livening up the life
of the play-goer all the past winter. Society paid
its biggest tribute to the popular co. last Satur-
day at “The Village Priest.” Every box was
turned into a side - show of bare arms and
glittering raiment. Thecircle wasabrilliant massof
jewelsand frocks and flowers. Thedress-sialls flowed
far back over the ordinary unreserved seats. Mel-
bourne and Sydney mingled their pathetic expres-
sions together—Chirnsides, Maddens, Osbornes,
Carters, Burflekins, Mitchells, Levys. Rumor
said that several of these were glad of an author-
ised opportunity to look sad after their adventures
with the “bookies,” and the faithlessness of
“ Bobby ” and “ Gladdy.”
The Society of Artists held its conversazione
on Saturday night at its show-rooms, and a
crowd of principally youthful people turned up in
evening dress and otherwise. There is not much
difference between one conversazione and another,
and this one was as pleasantly like any other as
the other one. _ The tall young lady artist with
white nasturtium decorations is generally re-
garded as lucky in having sold her two exhibited
pictures, and been blessed with orders for two
more. Perhaps the feature of the evening was
the number of pretty girls present in the flesh and
evening dress, whose portraits hung about tho
walls and made the real things look three shades
better.

Writes my Melbourne corresi>ondent: —
“Sir Charles Holled-Smith, the Vic. military
commandant, is not at all inclined to return to
England at the end of the term of his present en-
gagement, a few months hence. He has been very
popular with the military folks, and he and Ins
buxom wife have been great social successes.
Therefore. Premier Turner is being pressed to re-
new tho engagement. But he is dead against
doing so in view of Federation. If Holled-Smith
returns to England, lie will probably be at once
put on half-pay as a retired Major-General, and
have to dawdle out the rest of liis days at some
semi-fashionable watering-]dace, unless (and it is
not likely) he could secure the Australian Federal
command. It was he—as Steevens points out—
who rendered possible Kitchener’s work in the
Soudan, and lie looks, as well as is, a perfect
soldier. But Turner wants to save the salary.
“When the first Baronet Clarke died, ho left
the huge barrack-like family mansion in E. Melb.
to his willow, while the country-seat and estates
went to his son. It is now stated in social circles
likely to be well-informed that widow Clarke is
going to sell the place, and that it will be obtained
by a syndicate; and turned into a big fashionable
hotel. The on dil is the more probable in that the
Clarkes raised no opposition to the Collmgwood
railway, though a station is to be raised imme-
diately opposite the mansion. This would spoil
the place as a private residence, partly blocking
out the view across Richmond reserve 'to the
Yarra and Botanical Gardens, but would add
immensely to its value as a hotel site. Moreover,
the palace would need few alterations for the pur-
pose. There is immense bedroom accommodation
upstairs, and the downstairs rooms are large and
fine. The Cliveden Hotel will probably be the
great swagger Melbourne hotel of the future.

“ Presbyterian parson Dixon, of Warmambool
(Vic.), against whom a writ has been issued for
breach-o f - prom i se, is as unlike ono’s conception of
a gay deceiver as Mr. Pickwick himself. A
fastidious, scholarly, middle-aged bachelor, always
under the wing of his mother and sisters till lately,
when the former died and the latter took to
matrimony. Mr. Dixon, who has lived in China,
tells a story of tho modest Koreans. It was hot
weather, and tho sailors on an English yacht went
in for a swim alongside, whereiqion the whole
Korean village put up its shutters in horror, and
when the culprits had ceased their atrocities the
leading ofiieials came off in a boat, with their eyes
handmjed , to make a formal complaint.
“ Father Isaac Moore, the head of the Jesuits
in Melbourne, died last week in surgeon
Fitzgerald's private hospital. Tho first edition of
the A nous contained a paragraph stating that he
was so much better that lie would shortly leave
the hospital, but a later edition contained news of
his death. lie was 70 years of age, but still up-
held his reputation as one of the foremost
preachers in Melbourne, though he had aged
much during the last two years. Gentle and
tolerant as he was on many subjects, ho grew per-
fectly rampant on the subject of women’s tongues,
and would rail at his congregation in no measured
terms on the ‘unruly member’ which is ‘full of
deadly poison.’ But, all the same, the women
respected and obeyed him, even while they feared,
lie was a clever, well-read, and thoroughly able
man, and will be much missed in Melbourne.
“ Amy Castles and her aunt, so they say, pant
for Paris and quiet study. When it comes to the
point, poor M iss Bendigo, dried into a small brown
shrimp with travel and excitement, will probably
jib at steady grind after recent delirium. At the
Castles’ concerts the best part of the show was a
peep behind the scenes. After each song, Amy
wept on the bosoms of the queerest crowd of
Paddies extant. When she took her presentation
gewgaw to his Reverence to be blessed, the good
man did his duty with the air of a “ heavy father, ’’
and then indulgently beckoned forward a broth
of a bhoy to clasp the sanctified bracelet on the
bridling owner’s wrist. Those “cruel” press
accounts of the farewell Castles’ sing-song set
the criticised colleen and her confidantes “keen-
ing” like so many banshees, while their gintlemen-
friends twirled their shticks and trod on the tails
of imaginary journalistic coats. Another disap-
pointment for the clan was Melbourne's cold re-
ception of little Dolly. Earlier in the boom Amy
told people that she herself was anxious to get
away to Europe, so as to leave the coast clear for
her sister.
“‘Geisha’ Florence Perry is back from her
wanderings a shade tougher and more travel-worn
than she went, having annexed a trifle more
assurance by the way, in exchange for a layer of
her old demure daintiness. Dorothy Vane, .as the
masquerading British Miss, has altered her auction
plumage. She is now knocked-down cheap with
so much scarlet in her haberdashery and crimp in
her hair that the artless little English girl
makes the Jappies brought up to the tea-
house trade look callow and coy by com
parison. Nothing unlikely in this ! We all know
the real-life saint ‘whose strength is as the
strength of ten because her heart is pure’ and who
plays-up in consequence to an extent denied the
alleged sinner. Picture Mrs. Grundy’s fury, if
the Tottie class publicly ventured upon kitchen
lancers, skipping matches, cherry-picking capers—
those innocent diversions of the local naicest !
And poor Flossie’s knuckles would be rapped even
harder if, taking her social cue from the ‘Salt of
the Earth,’ she kissed in the open air like Sunday-
school picnickers, or ostentatiously paired off with
John Chinaman, after the manner of mission-
seraphim !

“ Mdllc. Trebelli grows into the good graces of
hearers. Her voice, not of sensational volume,
has tones suggestive of elfin horns, silver bells, an-
gelic carols, and other sounds peculiar to dream-
land. lake her singing, her appearance is gradual
in its charm. At the first glance one thinks “She
looks older and staider to-night. Dress too sub-
dued ! ” Then, by degrees, the pale yellow bro-
cade with its lace chemisette, treble chain of big
cool pearls across the bodice-front, and sprinkle
of diamonds, gets to seem an ideal toilette for a
warm evening.
“ When barrister J. B. Gregory (now of Kew
Asylum) lectured to the law-students on Law and
Property he indulged in a yearly joke. ‘Gentle-
men, needless to - remark, there are no manors in
Australia.’ Probably much learning hath made
Gregory mad. Euclid, for instance, he studied in
the original Greek, disdaining the ordinary trans-
lated version.

In a man’s club, a person who inserted such an
adyt. reflecting on the whole institution, would
be ignominiousiy expelled.
“That adyt. recalls the notification posted by
a member in a certain fashionable English men’s
club:— ‘ Iho nobleman who stole my pipe will
kindly return it to the steward, to save
trouble.’ The club arose in indignation, and the
offending member was haled beforo the committee.
‘Why had he imputed theft to some noble mem-
ber?’ ‘Well,’he explained, ‘the rules say that
this club is for noblemen and gentlemen. The
man who stole that pipe must be a nobleman —he
is no gentleman.’

of age on Sept. 18. He spent most of his early

schools.

Chief-Justice Way (S.A.) puts any little
cash he can spare into sheep. The Chief-Justice
of another Australian province uses his spare cash
in buying-up house property—including city and
suburban pubs.
Herr Borchgrevink’s great expectations in
Antarctica are that he will find there eight million
square miles of land—about twice the size of
Europe—and a new huipan race, somewhat akin
to the Greenlanders.
L. A. Wells, who led the unfortunate
“Calvert ” exploring party in N.W. Westralia, is
now working for the S.A. Lands Dept, in the Far
North valuing pastoral improvements and report-
ing on new gold-finds.
The story that the new N.S.W. Premier
was once a policeman has no foundation, except
that when he was a Tas. municipal official he
more or less controlled the local police. Mr.
Lyne was never in the police-force.
The N.S.W. portfolio of Justice has again
oscillated to a layman, W. H. Wood. Gould
(lawyer), Lee (layman), Hughes (lawyer), Wood
(layman). But Wood, it is circulated, studied law
a little, and is at present reading for the Bar.
The diploma of Dr. Edward Boot, who
lately died at Moruya (N.S.W.), is dated 1837.
The doctor was 86 years old, and had practised at
Moruya for 50 years. The diploma of Dr. Crooke,
of Darlinghurst, aged 86 and still in active prac-
tice, is dated 1838.

Bushranger Frank Gardiner’s father still
lives in Switzerland. The belief current at the
time the amiable highwayman was sentenced was
that Gardiner was not his real name. His release,
by hocus-pocus process—a release which caused
the downfall of the then Ministry—was also attri-
buted to the exercise of the influence of an influen-
tial relative of the bailer-up. The person desig-
nated as Franks father is a pensioned British
military officer.

A S.A. C. of E. parson, C. E. Doudney,
has about made a record—been to England and
back, married a wife (of the family of ex-Gov.
Gawler, by the way), and scored a “possible” at
the annual rifle matches in Adelaide, all inside
five months. Mr. Doudney was one of the S.A.
representatives at last National rifle meeting in
Sydney, but shot without luck.
There are aspects of Maoriland Premier
Seddon that suggest Haroun Al Raschid. Com-
plaint having been made in Parliament that un-
married men had been refused employment on
Govt, co-operative works, Richard Bun Tuck
announced that every bachelor over 25 who would
promise to marry within six months of getting
work would be given a steady job by the State.
A sprig of English nobility, who has seen
hard times in Australia, relates, in a meditative
way, that he only went bailiffing once. “It
was for rent, and when I got to the house,
the old woman told me to sit down, as the
old man wouldn’t be in for a few minutes.
I sat down. When the old man came along
I told him my business, but
he only smiled, and asked me
if I had lunched yet, don-
oherknow. Told him, yes.
Then I helped him to take
out his horse and put the
cart in the shed, and he
kept smiling all the time.
• Could I turn a grind-
stone ? ’ ‘ Oh, yes !’and I
turned it, and he held the
axe to it. Said I turned
splendid, and still the same
smile. After about ten
minutes he stopped, and
asked me to feel the edge
of the axe. It was like
a razor. ‘Going wood-
chopping ? ’ I asked. ‘ No,’
he said, smiling worse’n
ever, and flourishing the
axe round his head. ‘ I’m
goin’ bailiff-choppin’ !’—
and he makes a rush at
me. I was over that fence
quick, and found out after-
wards that the man spent
more of his time inside a
lunatic asylum than out-
side. I reckon that was a
tight place, because when
the police went for him
next day he had his wife
up the chimney and
was lighting a fire to
smoke her out.”
“P.”: Knew something
of the Maoriland “ poet ” Wills (a
bootmaker, by the way), lately men-
tioned in Bulletin. An edition de
luxe of his verse was published 15
years ago by Henry Brett, of Auck-
land —a collection of easy rhymes
possessing very little merit. He
was an omnivorous reader, and a very Parkes
for aitchlessness, but a merry companion. In
Hori Grey he had a sympathetic patron.
He acted as Otahuhu correspondent for
Auckland Star, and on one occasion
referred to the “farming gentry” as
attending a meeting of the Pakuranga
hounds. Sturdy old John Blackman,
alluding to this, said: “Wills, if
you love me, never use ‘gentry’
again. I came 16,000 miles to get
out of reach of the sound of that
abominable word.”
Reid and Wise go to no trouble or expense
in hiding their mutual animosity. When the new
N.S.W. Attorney-General took over his depart-
ment he had perforce to introduce himself, stray-
ing in for all the world like a casual caller. All
the other Ministers paid their successors the
courtesy of introducing them to the under-secret-
aries and the higher officials of their new depart-
ments and formally handing over the office. But
when Mr. Wise dropped in to assume the duties
of Attorney-General the late Premier “wasunfo:
tunately engaged, elsewhere.”

Lord Elibank, an old-time warrior and
modern globe-trotter who will soon be trotting
around Australia, carries a snap-shot camera with
him. He will be able to get studies of one or two
Australian Governors in a splendid assortment
of unrehearsed attitudes.
