income is estimated at .£150,000 per annum. The
other day he bought 300 tons of pipes for irriga-
tion purposes—the outcome, probably, of the
decision given last year whereby he had to lower
the by-washes of his dams.
Recently died at Caulfield (Vic.) Jones,
the man who started the first train in that pro-
vince. He was Flinders-street station-master
from the early 50’s until a few years ago, having
been taken over with the old Hobson’s Bay line
when Government bought it in the 70’s.
King O’Malley, the Yankee Jabberwock,
has finally shaken the dust of S.A. off his feet,
and is going to settle in Tasmania. “ I think the
climate will suit me,” he says. If O’Malley takes
a hand in the politics of the Land of Nod, there
will certainly be a change in the climate.
The last official act of N.S.W. Attorney-
General Reid was to direct a discontinuance of
the proseoution of Gilbert Probyn Smith, the
Nortonian pamphleteer. “ Jack ” Want estab-
lished a precedent for no re-trial in a criminal-
libel case, when he, strangely enough, refused to
have John Norton re-tried for the “open letter”
to colleague Carruthers. The question is: Is
Norton displeased or delighted ?
Henry Clarke, M.L.A., the omission of
whose name from the list of new N.S.W. Ministers
excited animadversion in some quarters, voted
with the Opposition on the censure motion. He
has been 32 years a member of Assembly, missing
only one Parliament in all that time. But Clark
himself did not inspire or supplement this good
feeling of his friends, for at his age, nearing 80,
additional responsibility would be onerous. *
F. W. Holder, who has made the annual
Budget speech in S.A. Assembly every year since
*94, has probably succeeded so well as Treasurer
in these lean years because he has always had to
pay attention to the pence. He began life in the
ill-paid S.A. public-school service, became
proprietor and editor of one of the smallest of
country sheets, took a hand in local municipal
work, and stepped into Parliament, and almost at
once into office. He is the thinnest man in Aus-
tralian politics, and one of the readiest ; also
about the most confirmed Methodist local-
preacher.
Gov. Tennyson has the bookish man’s
aspect of short-sightedness. When he is talking
to you, he looks at you as though you were small
type and rather hard to read. Also, he has a
queer little way of saying quaint things out of the
corner of his mouth —so that he may smile with
the other side. Beyond that, he is the typical
English country gentleman with a Cambridge
training, not (after his father’s ideal) “ mouthing
out his hollow oes and aes, deep-chested music,”
but speaking with the regulation University
accent. Professor Morris (Melbourne’s official
literary mentor) and Tennyson might easily pass
for brothers in aspect, manner of speaking, and
style of looking.
The other day that patient magistrate and
ex-Mayor, Joel Eade, one of the oldest beaks and
identities now left on -Collingwood Flat, was
knocked up at 3 a.m. in cold, wet weather to
bail a drunk out of the lock-up. Joel has the
ill-luck to live nearly opposite the police-
station, and, being the handiest J.P.,
there’s a great run on him all round the
clock. He was wanted as usual by the
mother-in-law, or aunt, or some other
relation, of a drunk, and he went,
i But nary a stamp the people had for
the bail-bond. Then a messenger was
sent round the neighborhood to get ss.
worth of stamps, while old-man Eade
toasted his shins at the watch-house fire.
In the course of an hour or two the mes-
senger returned with 120 halfpenny
stamps ! The watch house-keeper and the
beak and the messenger and the drunk
(now pretty sober, and choking for a long
beer) combined to lick the stamps to the
bond, and then Joel had to cancel the
accursed things by writing his initials
and the date 120 times !

ous when otherwise rooted out, lately fell

civility ! ” Excellent word-painting.
W. P. Crick agreed to accept the dolt’s post,
the Postmaster-Generalship, “until the advent of
Federation —if we last so long.” To take any
other portfolio would have involved too great a
sacrifice of legal business. Crick, the only real
N.S.W. legal “amalgam,” probably makes J 22500
a year by court practice. He is regularly briefed
by other solicitors to appear for them, and gets
briefs marked with figures which many a leading
barrister would envy.

Vic. M.L.C. Ham is about the most harm-
less politician extant; but once he got off a wheeze
which makes his fellow Fatmen groan with Scotch-
byterian horror even unto this day. Cuthbert, the
only Minister in the Upper House, was submitting
a tolerably democratic measure, and, while object-
ing to some modifications suggested by Nat. Levi,
M. had accepted more important changes
from Campbell, M.L.C. Whereupon, remarked
Ham, “I am sorry to see the Solicitor-General
following this course. He strains at a Nat., but
has nevertheless swallowed a Campbell.”
Chief-J ustice Darley celebrated his 69th
birthday on September 18. In Nov. next he wil|
be 13 years C.J. All the Judges on N.S.W.
Supreme Court Bench at date of his appointment
are dead. When Darley was barrister, J. 11.
Want, R. E. O’Connor, and C. E. Pilcher (all
distinguished to-day) read law under him. He
was called to the Irish bar in 1853. He happened
accidentally to meet the late Alfred Stephen,
N. Chief Justice, touring the old country,
and Stephen, owing to the congested state of law
business there, invited him to Sydney. He came,
and in time took Alfred Stephen’s seat as N.S.W.
Chief-Justice.
The great-grandson and namesake of
Joseph Holt, the Wicklow farmer who more than
any other individual incited the Irish Rebellion of
1798, was married at North Sydney on September
6. That great-grandfather spent over 14 years in
N.S.W., a sentence of death recorded against
him being commuted to transportation. A private
feud first drove hint to the ranks of the rebels.
When there his abilities as an organiser and his
turn for military tactics gave him a leading posi-
tion and won him the title of “General.” He
was betrayed and captured. In the little Botany
Bay rebellion of 1804 Holt got embroiled. His
own asseveration was that, so far from aiding the
disaffected convicts, he had exerted all his in-
fluence to repress disorder, but he was banished
to Norfolk Island notwithstanding. Sixteen
years after his conviction in Ireland he received a
free pardon, and returned to Dublin where the
balance of his life passed tranquilly.

Aboriginalities.
Local-preacher Muggins got bushed in the
Jingeras, and had to put up for the night at
Muldoon’s. Hadn’t long turned in when rain
started, and began to irrigate him from a crack in
the roof. He got a light and looked round for a
remedy, and seeing a carpet-bag in a corner opened
it and hung it on a nail in the rafter, so as to inter-
cept the dropping. But the rain came faster, and
long before morning the bag filled —and fell. It fell
on Muggins and blackened his eye in a dreadful
fashion. He had to go on his way to toil in the
vineyard with the discredited optic, and naturally
everybody asked him how it came about. And
Muggins, being a good Christian, told the exact
truth that was stranger than fiction—that a port-
manteau of water bad fallen on his eye in the
night. Muggins never went to that part of the
vineyard again, but the discredit he and his eye
and his explanation brought on the church was a
blow from which the good cause hasn’t recovered
even yet.

“Old Shikari”; Re “C.F.’s” sambur-
python yarn. Waterton considered phenomenal
a 30ft. boa, of which he preserved the skin, and
Col. Pollok, 30 years a Public Works official, in
the jungles of Burma and Assam, and knowing
more than anyone else about the “shikar” of
those countries, never saw a python over 20ft. He
and I once saw a wild-boar-and-python fight on
the Monaso, and the pig killed the snake. Col.
P. had a sainbur’s head spreading sft. 9in. in the
antlers, the brow-antlers being each 20£in. long.
Here you have facts and figures. It would be
well if “ C.F.” would give ditto.

Cause and effect:—
Croydon, August 21.
A number of publicans to-day were fined for keeping
liquor mixed with ingredients having a deleterious effect,
the evidence showing there had been adulteration by
aniline and sulphuric acid. The prosecutions were laid
at the instance of Inspector Gabriel, who has been testing
liquors here lately.
Croydon, August 30.
Four deaths have occurred from liquor here during
the last fortnight
Melbourne Table Talk tells the most
gruesome cat yarn of the decade: —
Westralia fears the rabbit pest which, coming from
S.A., is already within her borders but has yet a con-
siderable stretch of desert country to overcome before it
can reach the more hospitable regions of the agricultural
country. The exploded idea of encountering the invad-
ing bunnies with an army of hungry cats was revived ;
and the “Groper” intellect being persuaded that
the failure of the experiment elsewhere was
merely owing to the want of a proper under-
standing of the means by which the full measure
of feline ferocity may be awakened, a cargo
of cats was procured at Albany and shipped on
board a schooner, without food or water, for the
voyage to Eucla. Never was the law- of “ survival of the
fittest” in more active operation than during the voyage
of that lone schooner across the Bight, and never were
such fearsome sounds of woe heard from anything afloat
since the Phantom Ship with its crew of lost souls was
last encountered. Not a single cat escaped from that
ship, yet only 40 per cent, of the original complement
were landed; and a few scraps of fur were all that was left
to indicate the sad fate of the missing majority. At Es-
perance, where the schooner touched en route, a resource-
ful “ dead-beat ” had a little shipment of cats—all that
town contained—to offer on his own account, and for
these he netted the munificent price of £1 each. But his
villainy was discovered before the schooner sailed, and a
heart-rending scene ensued. Anxious householders,
peering into the hold, could distinguish their
own plump Toms and Tabbies fighting for their
lives against a horde of famine-stricken spectres,
but were powerless to save them. The police were
appealed to in vain, and there was no rescuer brave
enough to venture into the hold. At Eucla, when the
schooner came alongside the jetty, the crew scrambled
ashore, leaving one hero to open the hatches ; '• and what
remained of the oat cargo landed itself in exactly 3$
seconds. They have since only once been heard of—when
they beseiged the camp of a lone prospector who only
saved his life by three hours’ hard work with a toma-
hawk, opening and throwing to the horde of famishing
pussies his six months’ store of tinned meats.
Sounds beautifully incredible, and yet strangely
’Groperesque things do happen in ’Groper Land.

Dear Bulletin. —Any reader had experi-
ence similar to mine in being blind for nearly a
day after sleeping with my face exposed to bright
moonlight? Old bush-hands generally, when
moon is bright, sleep with a fold of blanket over
eyes. I was rabbiting at the time.—Lunby.

The other day a Brighton (Vic.) lad, sus-
pecting a certain fox-terrier of slaying his parents’
poultry, inveigled said dog into the garden and
securely moored him close to some bee-hives, with
result that the poor tyke was stung to death.
People who are incapable of appreciating original
ideas have been expressing the opinion that this
ingenious youth will finish up on the gallows.

“ Pelican ”: Re off-colored birds. In
a locality on Goondiwindi-road there used to
be arosella entirely blue, though otherwise
it was identical with its companions in size,
shape, and call. It was not a D.T. bird. I
tried several times to shoot it, but only suc-
ceeded in knocking a couple of azure feathers
off. The Moreton Bay rosellas have some
blue about the body, but aforesaid was not of
their variety.

“Hairpin”: Re carpet snakes. A
woman once had a pet snake, 16ft. long. It
was very hind of milk, and one day the woman
left her infant with its sucking-bottle in the
cradle, while she performed her household
duties. Returning to the bedroom not long
afterwards, she found that baby and sucking-
bottle had disappeared down the snake’s
throat, one of the child’s feet protruding from
the snake’s mouth. Husband was called,
siiake ripped open in short order, and child
rescued. That child was/. Has the M'Cooey,
in all his vast snake-experience, met a more re-
markable case?

malice. By the way, how does he live ? Has

tried to dig him out and investigate, but failed.

“ F.li.A.” : The “hoo-hoo-hoo ! ” inquired
about by “ J.C.G.” (8., 26/8/’99) is the cry of the
porcupine ant-eater, and the difficulty of locating
it is due to the fact that the animal is either close
to or right in the ground when it calls. Have at
times vainly tried for hours to find the creature,
but at other times have succeeded first try. Al-
though the echidna is of nocturnal habits, it may,
in localities where seldom disturbed, be heard in
the day-time.

“ Rhaumac” : Re whale-catching in nets at
Whangamunui, M.L. (B. 5/8/’99), there is no
“ Captain ” Cook in it. Years ago an old Peter-
head (Scotland) skipper told “Bertie” Cook how
a whale had been entangled in an ordinary fishing-
net on the Scottish coast. Together they dis-
cussed the feasibility of making a large-mesh
strong net, and, as a result, the present mode of
whale-catching near Cape Brett speaks for itself.
Last time writer was at the whaling station at
Whangamunui the nets were in sections 36ft. sq.,
the meshes being 6ft. sq. The sections are depended
from a strong wire hawser which is either run out
from shore to rock, having deepwater channel
between, or run from a point on the rocky coast
out seaward and moored at outer end. Each sec-
tion of net is made fast to wire hawser by light
lashings and there is a buoy at each end of seotion.
Whale strikes a section, hawser-fastenings snap,
and whale’s head becomes enveloped in net.
Whale never goes backward, cannot do so, but
keeps boring ahead, gets tired out, then lance and
harpoon finish him. Cook is now trying steel-wire
hawsers, which answer very well. This season’s
returns are very good, but there has been much
loss of net seotions owing to stormy weather.

“8.5.”: Anybody ever noted swine’s
inability to locate sound ? A train which passes
here is locally known as the “ pig-killer ” from
the havoc it makes amongst the grunters. If
“ Dinnis ” sees train first, he gets clear; otherwise
he looks solemnly in some other direction till
train hits him. When a boy I was once jogging
along with a basket of butter on a stiff-legged
“ crock.” Pig appeared in the dust, and, hearing
the horse’s tread, grunted enquiringly at every
compass-point but the right one; and brought
down “crock” and rider. Then he calmly
devoured the cow-product, while I bemoaned a
crushed ankle.

A recently-pulled big crop of turnips in
the M.L. South Island averaged 281bs. each. Tur-
nips grow so big in Maoriland that the sheep,
The Bulletin is assured, on the very worst
authority, often eat out the inside and .orawl in
for warmth in frosty weather.
This “ tyle of love” in the very vulgar
tongue, sent by a Parramatta man, is not much,
considered as a poem. But, for all that, it has
point about it: —
Queensland bush has of late been fertile
in the horrible. About a month ago a settler in a
Southern agricultural district found the body of a
man impaled on a stump. A fallen tree lay
beside the stump. The luckless man had been
felling timber, and this particular tree had
knocked him backwards and pinned his body on a
sharp stump behind him. Four days elapsed
before the discovery was made.
* * * *
“ F.” : Re jibbing horses and cures.
Recently saw a nag in a cart stick-up his driver
on the tram-line in Elizabeth-street, Sydney.
Nothing would make him shift until a tram came
along behind and the driver puffed up to the cart
and steadily pushed it along 20 or 30yds. The
old horse hung back valiantly, but he had to go,
and he eventually walked away with his head
hung in shame and a pained expression in his eye.

An angel—it was very hot—
Called at a shanty placid
Up Croydon way ; but all he got
Was pure sulphuric acid.
To Heaven's Gate the message went
Like lightning, only fleeter:
“ They’ve formed a branch establishment
Of Hell in Croydon, Peter ! ”
And no one who arrives and knocks
From date, need be annoyed on
Receiving the cut orthodox,
If he should hail from Croydon.
'Twas in the sweet and merry month of Mye,
I took a trip to Manly one fine dye,
Where I met a pretty myde,
Half inclined to be afr.vde,
But her fears I 9oon allyde—in this wye.
I rysed my hat pilitely, and with gryee,
And swore I’d never seen so sweet a fyce ;
Introduced myself by nyme,
Begged she’d not my freedom blyme,
Love at first sight—heart in flyme—’twas a kyse 1
At first the lydy simulyted ryge,
And then, from my own politeness, tore a pyge,
For she, half-ash.vmed, did sye,
I could have with her my wye,
To love, honor and obye—she’d engyge.
Then mutual vows were fervently exchyngcd,
Was courtship ’ere so speedily arrynged ?
All was settled thus in hyste,
And my arm I fondly plyced
Firmly round her maiden wyste—things were chyng^v
So all my vows of single life were vyne !
To-day I am enslyved, yet don’t complyne I
How I, once, had been enryged,
If my best friend had presyged
That to-dye I’d be engyged—and yet be syne.
My single joys will terminyte, they sye,
Upon my near-approaching marriage dye,
When my bride, with blushing fyce,
And her usual winning gryce,
Will be smothered up in lyce—and then awye 1


One of the most wonderful inventions of
the age is Willson’s discovery of crystalline carbide
of calcium. There is little doubt that acetylene
gas is about to revolutionise artificial lighting.
As with all successful patents, a host of pirates
have started up to try and rob Willson of his
rights. However, the recent decision of the High
Courts at Leipsic, Germany, will somewhat damp
the ardour of Willson’s opponents. It has been
decided that Willson is undoubtedly the discoverer
of crystalline carbide of calcium. Following up
this success, the English Acetylene Company nave
issued a batch of writs against infringers, and the
Acetylene Gas Co. of Australasia Ltd., of 36 Pitt-
street, Sydney—who own the Willson patents for
Australasia—are getting ready to give the Aus-
tralian pirates a surprise. This co. have now fitted
up some hundreds of installations around Sydney,
and are prepared to give estimates for any kind of
acetylene installations. Write direct to 36 Pitt-
street, and beware of infringers.*

“C.S.G.” in answer to “Pelican”
(B. 15/7/’99)
Eggsof both varieties of butcher-bird arealike—greenish
with red or brown spots. Have found one exception—
eggs pale blue, with black spots at big end. These eggs
(got at King's school, Parramatta, in ’B4 and ’B5) were of
the black-and-white bird. Have also noticed at least
three varieties of magpie’s eggs, with apparently no dif-
ference in the birds. Also—with again no apparent dif-
ference in the birds—two varieties of peewit’s eggs. One,
longish, pale pink, with small purple spots (usually in ring
round big end). Other, salmon-pink, with brown
blotches. This egg is short, and tapers quickly to a
point. I might mention that in ordinary seasons all
native birds will continue to build and lay until they
fledge two broods. A pair of coots, on Parramatta
River, were persistently robbed of their eggs from Aug.
to Dec., yet they hatched out a brood in Jan., and a
second brood at beginning of April. Started laying again
in Aug.

“ An Australian Woman,” to Bulletin :
Re Australians marrying Chinamen and other colored
aliens, personally lam so jealous for my country’s good
that I would not even have these aliens enter Australia,
or, having them here, I think there ought to be a law
prohibiting them intermarrying. All the same, however,
I do not wonder that these aliens are often so favored.
Have seen and heard of some that Australians would do
well to copy. Kindness to animals, and especially to
horses, appeals powerfully to an Australian woman, and
influences her strongly in the choice of a husband. Dur-
ing six years’ station life I have had chance to notice that
the horses of alien hawkers are always in fine condition,
and that there is always plenty of feed in the vans. These
men also were careful to rug their horses on chilly nights.
Noticed, on other hand, that an alleged white hawker on
6ame track with two vans always had his horses in
pitiable condition. Never carried chaff, even when the
track was as bare as a baking-board, and often had a
horse die on the road. Knew a station manager to refuse
to sell him a horse on account of his callous brutality.
Chinamen are specially kind to horses, and I have known
them displaj - thoughtful kindness otherwise, as in case of
one here, who, after marrying a white woman, discovered
that she was both immoral and drunken, whereupon he
made her a comfortable allowance and left her. Perhaps
Australians grow callous from being so accustomed to the
spectacle of drought-perishing animals dying by inches
and tortured by crows, flies, and ants. Have often won-
dered how men could pass a creature in this condition,
when a knife-thrust, or a revolver bullet would end its
agony.

“A.E.G.” : Once knew a miner, “ Geor-
die,” who liked his meat raw. Have often seen
him buy a lb. of steak, and “scoff ” it right there
in the shop with apparent satisfaction. He never
used tea, coffee, or beer, and always wore three
suits of woollen clothes, which he changed every
night, the innermost suit becoming in turn the
outermost. Also, he never used a bed, but slept
in a large chair. Snuffed out at 84. No tuber-
culosis about him although he gave it every
chance.
LADY MARY LYGON, Governor Beauchamp’s sister, who is
leaving for England.

M. Raymond Dubois, the Victorian wine-ex*
pert, gave a capital little lecture at the
Working Men’s College, Melb., the other week.
Some of his facts and figures are well worth
remembering. “In 1801 a scientist employed
by Napoleon reported that not one of Great
Britain’s colonies cultivated the vine for wine-
making purposes, and that fact holds good to
almost as great an extent at the present time.
. . . Australia now produces about three
million gallons of wine from 49,000 acres of
land. . . Paris alone could consume in 12
days the whole annual production of Victorian
wine. ... It is apparent that if Australia
were an out-and-out wine country there would
be no need for an export trade just now.
. . . Australia has ten million acres of
vine-growing land, and seeing that a good
vineyard is worth £3O "per acre, this
means a possible capital value of three
hundred millions sterling, or even with only
one-tenth of that area under cultivation, thirty
millions of money ; while the industry would
then give employment to thousands of men who
at present roam about the large cities, seeking
work but securing none.” Having pointed out
the sound temperance principles of wine as
compared with spirits and beer, and having
assured the audience that Australia is already
producing a chablis that can’t be beaten any-
where, the lecturer slung a stone at the
Philistines who decry all the products of their
own country, and will never say half a good
word for Australian wines. And these, by the
way, are mostly wine-purchasers and land-
owners who would be advancing their own
interests if they were to abandon their
prejudices and start to drink the local article
for the benefit of Australia.
*.
“ Royal Artillery ” to The Bulletin :
_ The Peace Conference kicked up a dust about “ explo-
sive bullets and asphyxiating shells,” but these concern
nobody but the actual fighting men, who can take care
of themselves, and suffer less than others from the real
horror of battle. Civilians suffer by their crops being trod-
den down, their towns and villages being held as fortified
posts, taken and retaken, and aboveall by bombardments.
The Germans in France, in 1870, constantly resorted to
this barbarous measure, hoping that the governors of
strong places would surrender to save the inhabitants.
They carried their point at Strasburg, but failed at Bel-
fort. The garrison of a fortress always have plenty of
bomb-proof cover for themselves and their stores. There
they are safe from the shells which are shattering and
burning the civil buildings. Whole towns have been
laid in ruins, and thousands of their people killed without
the fortifications being injured, or more than a dozen or
two of the garrison hurt. Strasburg was bullied, Paris
and Metz were starved into surrender, and the bombard-
ment of Paris was a piece of gratuitous brutality wld-h
did not hasten the surrender by a day.
GOLD TRESSES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You stand at my hnee, Gold Tresses,
Most true of all lovers of mine,
With lips that are fashioned for hisses
And fingers that thrill where they twine.
And bitter at heart, thus early,
And weary of life am I,
And you are so happy, girlie,
—The sun and the birds say why!
Your heart is so pure, Gold Tresses :
You know of no life like mine
That is hot to the brow with kisses
And red to the lips with wine
The hate in her courtly greetings,
The scorn of her soft replies,
The shame of her stolen meetings,
The grief of her wild good-byes.
Oh! Fashion your wreaths in the sunlight
Untouched of the mist and the ram,
For youth is the rose-light, the one light
That never will gird us again;
And ours is the load you must borrow,
And ours is the path you must fare,
Who have passed by the archways of Sorrow
And knoclted at the gates of Despair.
You nestle with soft arms around me;
Your face is upturned to my own ;
The chaim that have crippled and bound me
Lie heavy and cold as a stone ;
And out to the sunligh t there surges
A passionate longing and wild—
Ah! These are the whips and the scourges,
The lips and the hands of a child.
WILL H. OGILVI[?]

“ All the world’s a stage, and all the men
and women merely players.” This applies to the
present “sassiety ” world with particular aptness.
Now, considering that the success of the players
depends upon the manner in which they dress
their parts, and that it is necessary for lovely
woman to look beautiful in all her moods, the
P.D. corset may be said to be indispensable to
her. Graced by a pair of P.JD’s., lovely woman
may confidently take the centre of the stage at
any social gathering.*
Country readers would do well to try the
“Albatross Lamp Wick.” “It requires no trimming,"
saves oil, has no smell whatever, and it is almost impos-
sible to have an explosion of a lamp in which they are
used. Messrs. Chambers and Seymour, of Collins-street,
have one window entirely devoted to the Co.’s goods.*
My Melbourne correspondent continues :
“The history of the £2OO piano at Melbourne
Government House forms a queer chapter in
‘ nearness ’ in high places. Two years ago
Gov. Brassey hired the piano for use at
Government House functions, but he did not
pay the hire. The piano remained, and the hire
accumulated, but the old man disregarded the
bills. Finally he made the extraordinary claim
that Govt, ought to pay for the hire of the piano
for his (H.E.’s) entertainments. It would be
as reasonable to say that Govt, should have paid
his wine account or his pastry-cook’s bill. When
the hire had reached the amount of £OO the matter
came under Premier Turner’s notice, and, to save
further haggling, he said, ‘We will buy the in-
strument right out 1 ’ So he passed a cheque for
£2OO, and Brassey was left chuckling over the
fact that he had wriggled out of paying the £OO
hire. The official papers show that these are the
facts. But why not get the whole of the papers
in print ?
“Melbourne highflyers, at present sampling
Sydney gaieties, worry the girls they left behind
them herealxmts with written rhapsodies over the
increased smartness discernable in daughters of
the Harbour city. Maybe their bachelor Governor
and all that hangs thereby acts as a pick-me-up to
the fascinations of fair Northerners who even be-
fore said stimulus promised sooner or later to give
Viotoriennes a run for the championship. The
belle of ’9B Cup was a Sydney matron, and the
frankest, friskiest toilettes at subsequent shoulder-
shows were worn by same charmer and her com-
patriotes. _ Also Melbourne’s solitary genius, pro-
fessed cynic and misogynist that he is, seemingly
joys more or less in the sympathetic society of a
charming Sydney-sider, now the wife of a local
professional man.
“It is becoming tony in Melbourne to entertain
your friends at a hotel or cafe instead of by your
ain fireside. Accordingly two or three of the best
pubs, are toeing the mark and showing great style.
One hostelry has imported a chef who transforms
plain mutton and ‘ wild rabby ’ into high-sounding
1 ’renchy dishes; the menu is so very up-to-date
that if you were to ask for tripe-and-onions or
Irish stew, the chef would throw up his billet in
disgust. At dinner-time you chew to the music of
a string band, and on all first-nights it is correct
to dine-your theatre-party there first and after the
play run them off somewhere else for supper.
'“lsn’t it time it was made a penal offence to
quote that battered old pome, ‘Oh woman in our
hours of case ’? Of course it was trotted out at
the Woman’s Suffrage struggle, though it has
about as much bearing on the question as Pea-
cock’s laugh has on Federation. It is so untrue
on the surface of it. The average woman spends
most of her hours of ease in yarding up the uncer-
tain, coy masculine, and when she has married
him he seems to spend his life in patting himself
on the back for his own magnanimity in giving her
free board and lodging, and making all sheol stir
if the chops are burnt.
“ Miss Goldstein has thrown aside the tennis-
racquet which brought her some suburban glory,
and has taken up the political racket. With
brother-in-law Champion directing her tactics, the
dark-eyed kitten of the Cat Cause made a bid for
notoriety. She lay in wait to catch the cautious
legislators night after night in the lobby of the
House with the result that when the Bill came
on they all had something sweet and oily to say of
the pretty persecutrix. Perhaps they threw out
the Bill harder than usual in the hope that Miss
G. will come and worry them again next time.
“The gallant lieutenant who was hitherto the
only local military johnnie to support a solitary
eyeglass, has been robbed of his individuality by a
visitor from Madras, who wears tight pants,
waxed moustache, and a window-pane, with an
air of active service thrown in.
You must no longer roll your veil in a
ball under the chin. Hitch it a little higher
and, let it rest calmly on the chin’s edge. I
don’t think women will take affectionately to the
new style. What a watch-chain is to a man when
words fail him was that little twist of veil to a
woman in any sort of mental quandary. She could
always depend on it to help her out. If she
couldn’t make herself understood, she unrolled the
knot —knob —it’s a mysterious thing, I hardly
know what to call it—twitched her veil, and rolled
it up again feeling considerably better. To give
this up will be a sort of ave atque vale, I should
imagine.
“Why doesn’t the A.N.A. popularise Austra-
lian literature by prescribing selections therefrom,
for its annual elocutionary competitions ? ‘Den-
ver’s Dream ’ (Silver King) is epidemic just now,
thanks to the Natives. And detached from the
context, it’s such a gratuitously gruesome passage.
At mild suburban at-homes, it’s horrid for un-
comprehending non-theatre-going hearers when a
leather-lunged reciter swells the veins on his fore-
head, bites his lips till they bleed, and bellows—
He stood up in his winding-sheet. And the face was
Geoffrey Ware's ! I took him by the throat—throttled
him—rammed him into his coffin, etc.
Or —
The rope was round my neck ; the hangman drew the
bolt! I dropped—dropped—dropped !
“A curious mistake occurred at a recent Melb.
suburban wedding. The contracting parties were
young persons of some provincial consequence,
and the assembled guests were large and varie-
gated. The fittings and the decorations were of
the kind that paralyse the sassiety reportress, and
the breakfast promised to be a perfect dream. The
wedding was due on the premises, and when the
hour struck people stood round awaiting events.
The bridegroom dropped in to keep his appoint-
ment, and the young lady was waiting, and no-
body could discover what was lacking, although
there was a general awkwardness, and a sort of
stiff feeling arising from a presentiment that all
was not well. Suddenly, amidst a painful silence,
the bride’s papa ‘fetched a yell,’and cried deso-
lately, ‘ Dear, dear ! I know what it is—l quite
forgot the parson ! ’ That was the trouble—no
parson had been ordered, and none was laid on to
solemnise the ceremony. Two guests, the best
man, and the bride’s father arose in heat, and tore
off in several directions in quest of a rapid parson,
and all returned in the course of twenty minutes
or so, each with a spiritual master in tow. Then
the wedding progressed in an atmosphere of deep
gloom occasioned by the awful hauteur and in-
jured spirituality of the three shepherds who did
not get the billet, but lagged superfluous on the
scene.
“ Strange that so many prominent men have
nothing but daughters to inherit their abilities, or
else rejoice with trembling in a too-precious only
son. Under the latter category come Gov.
Brassey, Judge Madden, Premier Turner, Dr.
Maudsley, and parson E lgar, while surgeon Fitz-
gerald, Ministers Isaacs and Best, Trenwith,
M.L.A., and various ’Varsity profs, have girls
only. Bishop Goe and Rabbi Abrahams are
childless.
“An appalling story to the effect that a Scottisli
Volunteer kilt hangs m its pride alone, with ne’er
an undergarment to keep it in countenance turns
out to be a wicked fable. Somebody has learned
from a military tailor that the wind is tempered to
the shorn sham Highlander in a quite respectable
manner, so one may now criticise a parade of the
new regiment without blushing at the thought of
what revelations might ensue if the bare-kneed
heroes were to march on a piece of orange-peel or
get surprised by a hurricane. It appears, though,
that the Melbourne canard was founded on fact.
Some years ago a real Scottish regiment, serving
in China at the time, adapted itself to the
warm weather as regards the mysteries of its
toilet. All went cool and pleasant with the reck-
less creatures until a storm came and disclosed the
bare rationalism of their costume, so to speak.
Whereupon the majah, or colonel, in command
gave them a good dressing-down and told them to
‘ have ’em all on ’ for the future, weather or no.

her foot. The profanity of it! ‘ Dave, ’ indeed!
“ Two fair society leaders are at daggers drawn
and their enmity to each other is a joke that goes
on for ever. At the races particularly, it is the
best fun in the world to watch their respective
circles of satellites —the one matron being crooned
over mainly by relatives ; the other by the more
swaggah herd. It is a four-year-old story. One
of the sistern in her spinsterhood beamed tenderly
upon her present brother-in-law who was secured
after a desperate struggle (in which a barmaid
figured) by the other fair girl. And now the
figuring Hebe—but that is another story.

for the Turner Govt., and advocates Women’s
Suffrage.

S MITH: “Saved a cove's life to-day, Jones. "
JONES “ No; how did you do it? ”
S MITH: “ Well, he came along and ashed me for threepence to get a feed, an’ I ses to him, ' What’d
you do if I was ter give yer two bob?’ ‘My God!' he ses, ‘ I'd fall down dead with
joy.' An’ I didn’t give it to him — an’ so I saved his life."
Business, Bobbery, etc.
Vic. Customs department acknowledged the
receipt of 15s. “conscience money." tire other day.
The most recent—and most
promising Maoriland industry is
bacon-curing. Eight years after the
dairy-factory system fairly started the
“ cockies ” began to realise that skim-
milk is the' ideal food for pigs.
Thousands of calves have been knocked
on the head to make room for more
profitable stock. Bacon-factories are
springing up like mushrooms. One in
Stratford, the centre of the dairy dis-
trict, cost £lO,OOO to erect and furnish.
3d. to 3id. per lb. is at present the ruling
price for hog-meat. Meanwhile, the drovers
are not displeased ; the abnormal slaughter of
calves means high prices for dairy cows in the
near future.
An investigation into the “ fines ” imposed by
big city draperies would reveal much pitiable hardship.
Some firms have as many as 40 offences on the list, carry-
ing fines ranging from 3d. up to 6s. Many of the offences
look as if put there as a means of taking back a portion of
the wages given. An eye should be given to this in the
next Factory legislation. Here, for instance, is an item
from a long list: “If any shall say or do an vthing
contrary to the interests of this establishment— 2s. 6d.’
And the firm is accuser, jury, and judge.

A candid admission (which litigants will please

The way of virtue is sometimes made hard. A
woman entered a M.L. lawyer’s olttee, a few weeks ago
and explained that sometime hack she had obtained
judgment in a case against a neighbour. lie was then a
drunkard and “ doing a perish”; now he had “turned
teetotaller and was earning good money,” so that a judg-
ment summons might profitably issue.
• * * •
Several Viet. Govt, servants are commissioners
for affidavits and are allowed to put the fee of Is. 6d. in
their own pockets- This is a pernioious practice which in
some quarters has developed somethin? like blackmail-
In a number of cases the affidavit has to be sworn in con-
nection with some document that passes through the
hands of an officer who holds the commission, and it is
peculiar to note how soon a matter wherein the
affidavit has been sworn before that particular officer gets
through as compared with a matter wherein the affidavit
has been sworn before someone else. In one department
there is an officer who is reputed to make between £5 and
£6 a week out of affidavits, in addition to his salary,
which is £375 a year. It is said that out of the fees of
affidavits this official has built a mansion for himself worth
at least £5OOO.

“ Business Man ” : A matter of greater public
interest than appears at first glance is that of inducing
N.S.W. Telephone Dept, to issue its “ List of Subscribers ”
book in a stouter and more serviceable cover than it
appears to consider necessary. The book is nearly always
out of cover within a week of issue, thus rendering un-
available the information printed on the rotten paper of
the cover. My protests have hitherto resulted only in
the stereotyped intimation that my “communication has
been received.”

Re the common and inferentially-
insulting notification—“ This business is now under en-
tirely new management." In a Sydney suburb, the other
day, I came across a miserable huckster’s shop (containing
about a crown’s worth of bilious-looking lollies) boldly
displaying the legend, “ This business has recently
changed hands for the better.”
• * * •
“ Malle ” : A Sydney shop-keeper recently ad-
vertised for clerks, who would hp ve to pass an examina-
tion prior to engagement. Huge numbers rolled up and
passed, and the few lucky enough to be selected were
employed for two days at five shillings a day. Truly
Sydney is a beautiful place to hustle for an existence in at
present.
* • • *
The average mining-man is indeed a poor-spirited
creature. He will submit to being robbed in the most
barefaced manner without protests—robbed, not by clever
manipulation or cute swindling, but by forgery, embezzle-
ment, and plain ordinary larceny. The cool effrontery of
many legal managers pulls them through meetings of
their shareholders, after they have been shown to have
unaccountable shortages in the cash ; and the strange part
of it is that the same men who fear to prosecute would not
hesitate a moment to run in any miserable wretch
caught stealing their fowls. One legal manager, bowled
out months ago, gave three distinct and different ex-
planations of a deficiency, and though all were proved
lies, no shareholder has yet got out a warrant.
* • * •
An auctioneer had hard luck t’other day. He
had run up a piano—with several keys that only went
“woof’’when you touched them —to about £2O. When
the bidder was found and was just giving his name, two
policemen rushed in r.nd took him away. He had just
escaped from an adjacent lunatic asylum. Of course, the
auctioneer couldn’t get at the next-best bid, and when he
put the wheezy instrument up again it was passed in at
30s.
* * * •
Lawyers sometimes come off second-best. In
Oollingwood (Vic.) Police Court once a marine-store
dealer was fined £5 for some breach of the Act
under which he held a license. His solicitor gave
notice of appeal. Ten minutes afterwards defendant
appeared in Court and handed £5 to the clerk. The
P.M., noticing this, enquired why he was paying the fine,
as his lawyer was appealing. Defendant, with a cunning
leer : “ Oh, yes, I know ; he wants to get more money out
of me. Fust loss, best.”

“E.K.”: Money is being spent in trying to
catch the tote and bookie. The other day a couple of
Vic. officers found trouble in indulging in the time-
honored palm-grease—out of which Robert has built so
many rows of houses. What sort of grease must the
lottery-Chow use, and where is it applied? John will sell
openly to police or parsons, no questions asked—only pay
your money for a chance, and a very poor one, in a game
which no one rightly understands ; in fact, it is doubtful
if you get a chance at all, for you never see the drawing.
Yet the banks are open—wide open—all hours for seven
days a week. Once in every months somebody (not
the parson, for the boss Chow is generally a Clistian and
goes to church) cries out to the Chief of Police, and a
couple of summonses are the result. Boss John’s
“ cousin ” appears. The police know it is not boss
John (case in Melb. a few months back). Cousin
pleads guilty—asks time to pay fine. Ultimately
takes it out in gaol at a small weekly wage,
and boss John, in his diamond rings, smiles and goes
on selling tickets. But it’s the deadest of cer-
tainties that if a European started a lottery he wouldn’t
be let run it a week. The totes and the books have got
to learn the secret of John’s effectual palm-grease. There
has been in Melbourne for months past a lottery-boom—
men, women, and children play it openly.

Mon Think nf Women.
What men minis OI women.
Beauty is only skin deep. The prettiest woman
is not always the most happy or the most sought
after. Men, lords of creation as thoy are, have
learned that a pretty face is not always a criterion
of amiability or a good constitution. Now-a-days
men are very careful to choose a life companion
from among the robust and healthy women rather
than one who is simply fair of face. Good
health is absolutely essential to happiness,
and many a man has found marriage a failure
through marrying a woman who is constantly
ailing, whose temper is irritable and who through
her own weakness, though without intent, makes
life a burden to all around her.
The internal organism of the female system is
so delicately constituted that it needs much care
and attention. Hence it is that women are not
able to enter into the rougher occupations of life.
If every natural physical function is not absolutely
regular in taking place, the woman’s life will be
more or less one of continual misery. There being
so many delicate female organs, it is but natural
that if one becomes in any way clogged or ham-
pered in its working by refuse matter the others
will be affected to a greater or less extent. The
circulation of the blood, too, if sluggish, will
weaken the action of the various organs. The
symptoms manifested by this condition of affairs
are numerous, among them may be mentioned
backaches, dizziness, fainting fits, pains in the
abdomen, loss of appetite and a general run-down
feeling.
Now, having explained what is necessary to
good health in woman, kindly let us impress upon
you the easiest and surest way to attain this end.
The system must be kept perfectly clear of refuse
matter, the circulation must be animated, and the
whole system toned up and kept in regular work-
ing order. All this can be accomplished by Bile
Beans for Biliousness. They purge the digestive
organs, increase the circulation, and invigorate
the liver. Once tried they will be found beneficial,
d their use continued the entire system will be
luned up and general health restored. A healthy
woman is always interesting, she is happy, and
her spirits are contagious. Good health and good
nature are inseparable ; secure the first by using
Bile Beans, and the latter will take care of itself.
Spring Time Cleaning.
Spring has arrived, and now the careful house-
wife goes through the annual spring house-clean-
ing. The summer will soon be upon us, and at
this time of the year we should take care to cleanse
the whole human system and fortify it to with-
stand the enervating effects of the Australian
summer. The increasing hot weather of this
period has an extremely debilitating effect
on the system if it is not in perfect
working order all through. It will be thus
seen that a, medicine which will regulate and
cleanse the interior mechanism of man, is especi-
ally valuable at this time of the year. Such a
medicine is Bile Beans, and no better preparation
can be found for toning up the digestive organs,
cleansing the blood, and invigorating the liver,
Spring generally brings with it an assortment of
ailments peculiar to this particular time of the
year ; such as spring fever, tired feeling, loss of
appetite, &c. For these take Bile Beans, which
are also a specific for biliousness, indigestion, con-
stipation, and debility. which are also very preva-
le nt attl “ a season. As a purgative, Bile Beans
are acknowledged without an equal in Australia.

Very Excited.
In Mark Twain's new book, “Following the
Equator,” there is a description of a fox-nunt,
which closes with: “There was a man ahead of
the rest, and be came spurring straight at me.
He was fiercely excited. It was fine to see him
ride ;he was a master horseman. He came like a
storm till he was within seven feet of me, where I
was leaning on a wall; then he stood his horse
straight up in the air on his hind toenails and
shouted like a demon : * Which way’d the fox
go? ’ I didn’t much like the tone, but I didn’t let
on, for he was excited, you know. But I was
calm, so I said softly and without acrimony,
‘Which fox?’ It seemed to anger him —I don’t
know why—and he thundered out, ‘Which fox?
Why, the fox 1 Which way did the fox
go?’ I said, with great gentleness, even
argumentatively, *lf you could only be a little
more definite—a little less vague—because I am a
stranger, and there are many foxes, as you know
even better than I, and unless I know which one,
it is that you desire to identify, and ‘ You’re
certainly the biggest idiot that has escaped in a
thousand years 1 ’ and he snatched his great horse
around as easily as I would snatch a cat, and was
away like a hurricane. A very excitable man.”
Such excitement certainly Mark Twain was not
used to, and had the great author followed up the
horseman’s rapidly disappearing form with : “Say,
young fellow, you need a few Bile Beans for Bilious-
ness ; your liver needs stirring up ” ; it would have
been about what would be expected. Verily, ex-
citement wasteth the form and maketh the eyes
hollow and the face white. The three B.’s are the
antidote of exoitability. If you doubt, make a
trial. Mark Twain is certainly a humorist of the
first water, and such remarks as the above roll
from his lips as if he had planned them all for
weeks.
A Matter of Spelling.
An interesting little paper, called Akmy
News, tells the following amusing yarn of an ap-
plication for leave put in by an old Indian colonel,
the reason stated being an attack of gout. The
brigade-major, through whom the application
went, happened to meet the colonel the same
evening, and thought to politely condole with him
on his ailment. “ Sorry to hear, colonel, that you
are suffering from gout,” he remarked. “Gout,
sir,” roared the colonel, who was rather a peppery
old salt, “ what the devil do you mean? Never
had gout in my life. Gout, indeed!” “Well, I
didn’t mean to offend you,” said the brigade-
major, “ and, anyhow, you can’t deny that you
have applied for leave on account of gout.” “Oh,”
said the colonel, “ I remember now that I did say
gout. But, then, who can be expected to spell
such an infernal word as rheumatism ? ”
There would be no need of spelling rheumatism,
the word would soon become obsolete if the people
would only use Bile Beans for Biliousness. Try
them.

When Wise turns in his bed and sees
Fegan’a head on his pillow, he will realise that
politics makes a man acquainted with strange bed-
fellows.
B. R. Wise is the only member of the
Lyne Ministry who sits for a Sydney or Sydney
suburbs constituency. The Country Party has
arrived at last.
Vosper, of the Hair, finds it advisable to
trim his sails, and he lately wired to his Kalgoorlie
constituents that he was “entirely with the
Federal League in its demand for a referendum.”
The Lyne Ministry was born from a ques-
tion of purity of government —the Ministry of the
Immaculate Conception. And all the more so
because half its members don’t know who their
political fathers are.
Ex-Premier Reid’s groan that the new
Lyne Government is “ essentially conservative,”
comes with all the grace of a swollen elephant
from a man who did so very little for the demo-
cratic cause as G. H. R.
The Lyne Ministry consists of eight lay-
men and two lawyers—Wise and Crick. That is
an improvement on various past N.S.W. Minis-
tries. _ The Reid-Want-Gould-Carruthers-Hughes
combination looked too much like blue foolscap.
Broguey-man O'Loghlen, who deeply re-
sented the imputation that he went round with a
no-oonfidence motion in his pocket, arose in Vic.
Assembly to vindicate his character, and concluded
by bringing a paper from his breast pocket and
calmly reading a motion involving the life of the
Govt. In one Vic. session the bould O’Loghlen
moved 19 votes of want of confidence.
Q. Premier Dickson has placed One-man-
one-vote to the forefront in his political pro-
gramme. During the discussion thereupon at
the opening of Q. Parliament, the most surprising
thing was the attitude of certain staunch old
Govt. Tories who said they saw nothing to fear in
it, and were prepared to support it. One of them
actually clamoured for adult suffrage. Truly the
clock moves on.
MELB. MOOCHABOUTS—1, “FLIES.
(Sketched in Little Coll ins- street.)
The expression on the faces of the un-
portfolioed Lyneites, when the new N.S.W.
Premier announced the names of his Ministers in
N.S.W. Assembly, was too painful for descrip-
tion. No poor devil in the dock, with the Judge
passing sentence, ' ever looked so glum. Five
years of fighting, for what? For the other fellows
—not one of whom was half so faithful or so ser-
viceable as the disappointed.
Just before the Referendum, when it was
too late to do anything serious in the way of dis-
proving the idiotic lie, the Brisbane Parochialists
distributed, leaflets about the West Moreton dis-
trict of •Queensland, calling on all the farmers to
vote against Federation on the ground that if it
was carried they would all- have to surrender their
t' tie-deeds and yo to the expense of yetting .new ones
from the Central Govt. And many of them believed
it, too, and rushed to the poll to vote the paroohial
ticket for all they were worth.
Christchurch (M.L.) Trades Council has
ukased that the man it supports at the next elec-
tion must deposit his resignation with the Council
to be used as it thinks fit. The most probable
result is that the Council will have to lend its
support to the worst candidate in the crowd.
Maoriland Opposition is mak-
ing the sale of State lands a leading
feature of its platform, as against
the leasehold system favored by
the Seddon Govt. One of its
favorite arguments is that to
give a man a long lease at a low
rental is virtually making him a
big present, as the value of the
land must go up during his ten-
ure. Of course if you sell
the land to the same
man you make him
a bigger present
as the value of the
land must go up
during his tenure
also, and he has a
much longer ten-
ure, but that
point is kept very
dark. Also, the Russell
party weeps tears of
blood as it draws har-
rowing pictures of the
poor struggling selec-
tor toiling to meet his
rent —this is the same
individual previously
alluded to as the man
who wrongfully got the
big present of the un-
earned increment, and
thereby grew affluent at the State’s expense,
and whose sinful affluence requires to be put down.
And the poor freeholder struggling to meet the
interest on the mortgage is never once mentioned.
Tom Slattery, who proposes to capture
the Boorowa seat in N.S.W. Assembly, vacated
by poet-squatter-warrior Mackay, enjoys a unique
political reputation. Parkes, in ridicule, on one
occasion described him as a political Machiavelli,
and Tom and his friends took the shaft as earnest.
Ever since he has posed as a man with profound
knowledge of human nature, and of the surest
way by secret means of attaining political ends.
Gyinpie (Q.) Troth on the proposal of the
parson-edited Brisbane Telegraph that the North
should be cut adrift because it voted for Federa-
tion :
Let the metropolitan members demand Separation for
Brisbane. They might be disagreeably surprised at the
readiness of Parliament to comply. But there’s not
much hope of them doing anything of the sort. Para-
sites are not generally anxious to sever their connection
with the bodies on which they batten and wax fat.
Westralian wire :
An attempt by Mr. Illingworth to abolish plural voting
was defeated, the Premier pointing out that the time
had not arrived for Westralia to follow the example of
other colonies, and adopt one-man-one-vote.
Even John Forrest wasn’t game to say that
“one man one vote” was not a just principle;
only “the time had not arrived” to do justice.
But the time to do injustice has always arrived —
in the opinion of people like Forrest. .
It is expected that, to be consistent with
their action against the Coal-miners’ Relief Act,
immediately N.S.W. Parliament settles down to
business again M‘Gowen, Haynes, and others
will introduce legislation to do away with hos-
pitals because those institutions only save a por-
tion of the people from death ; and to withdraw
Govt, support from all charitable institutions
because they do not alleviate the distress of all
the indigents in the land.
. Lyne’s new Cornstalk Ministry has to
waste a month of valuable time on the hollow
mockery of being re-elected. At the ’9B election,
Lyne beat his only opponent by nearly 3to 1; and
all his colleagues had large majorities save B. R.
Wise, who got in by five votes. No one dreams
for a moment that any of them will be rejected—
it is a bitterly cold day when a Minister with
public works to give away is rejected; but the
month must be wasted all the same.
Pastor Rentoul, the one-eyed Presby-
terian bigot who leads the Melbourne Non-con-
formist conscience, comes out with a defence of
the Boer Govt, clever and convincing until one
reaches the final paragraph, which protests
against Britain making war upon “our fellow
Protestants.” This is the keynote of the whole
column of argument. When Britain was bully-
ing Holy Roman Catholic Ireland the Rentouls
of the land did their level best to sool on the
British lion.
No man did more for Federation in North
Queensland than one Crabb, a cold-tea apostle.
For many weeks he lectured in and out of Towns-
ville, in street and hall, with much ability and a
thorough mastery of the bill. At Charters
Towers he fairly played with Labor-member
Lesina in a set debate on the question of the hour.
Lesina, by the way, was about the one Q. Labor
anti-billite who worked tooth and nail for his con-
victions. He swore the bill was bad, and de-
nounced it frenziedly, but never in all his speeches
gave one lucid reason for his attitude. But that
was not his fault. The attitude couldn’t be ex-
plained by any sane reasoning.

have been as follows :

MAGDALEN-OR BANANA-SKIN.
DON JUAN, JUNR. (as a prominent member of
W. C. T. U. slips on the pavement): “ ’Ere y’are,
Bill, ’ere’s one of them fallen women as I was
a-tellin’ yer of.”
Theodore Fink was put up in the Yic.
Assembly the other night to express the Age’s
argument that the Eastern Cable co. should be
allowed to start a private office for sending cables,
and compete with the Govt, for business. For the
next half-hour the spectacle of Fink in the soup
was highly interesting. P.M.G. Duffy pointed
out how Australia had been exploited in the past
for the benefit of the cable cos., and Gillies and
Trenwith backed him up. In the end the syndi-
cate’s defender got out and got off, with fragments
of his broken plate adhering to him.
Queensland Govt, papers aver openly
again and again that the Scandinavians, Italians
and others are wanted to take the place of
kanakas on the plantations. Queensland Govt,
agents, however, trot round Europe talking to the
peasantry and telling little stories about vine-clad
cottages, snug little homesteads, every man his
own landlord, and so forth. The small Con-
tinental cultivator who comes to Queensland on
these representations and finds then that he is
expected to do kanaka work on kanaka condi-
tions, is going to get a rude awakening.
Most of the Queensland anti-federalist
journals have accepted their defeat quietly and
gracefully, and avow themselves prepared to take
the referendum verdict in good part. The labour
organs, seeing the stand taken by Q. democrats,
could, of course, scarcely do otherwise. The
bigoted, illiterate Telegraph, however, keeps on
howling and assuring the public it has a card up
its sleeve for the discomfiture of the federal party,
Brisbane is in a fix just now ; it doesn’t know
whether it ought to be more proud of its anti-
national vote or its anti-national newspaper.
Of what value is the pledge of a politician?
Thus Homburg, M.P., a leading Tory Opposi-
tionist in S.A. Assemby, 13th Sept., ’9B, speaking
on the Household Suffrage Bill:
The opinion of a large number of members in the
House was that the vote of the people would be against
the Bill in its present shape. (Mr. Batchelor: “Will
you be prepared to abide by the result of the poll?")
Most emphatically.— Hansard, 1898-9, page 470.
The result of the poll was a majority for House-
hold Suffrage of over 15,000 votes ; but, the other
day, when the second reading was carried, Hom-
burg voted against the Bill /
As the statement by Labor Member Law
that the Fegan Amendment was put into the
hands of five or six members to ensure its safety,
has never been contradicted, it is presumably
correct. If so it was a piece of Fegan.
J. Lionel Fegan first attracted notice in New-
castle as a Wesleyan local preacher—it was this
religious connection that enabled him to secure
nomination on the Labor ticket at the birth of
N.S.W. Labor Party in the’9l election. He threw
over the Solidarity Party for Reid just at the
right moment, and now deserts Reid in time to
secure a portfolio.
Rumors are revived that M.L, is “sick
of Seddonism,” and that the people will “shake
off the yoke ”in December next. It is in turn
insinuated that Seddon is sick of M.L. and will
seek the calm of the Agent-Generalship (whioh
Reeves, it is cabled, is resigning). Truth probably
lies between these extremes. The country shows
signs of “that tired feeling” simply through
having the same Ministry in office for
nine years; so does Dick. And little
wonder, for Seddon has been practically doing
all the work of the Ministry for six months,
while M‘Kenzie has been absent in England,
Thompson tending a sick wife, and Hall-Jones
rebutting “ scandals.”
- Sydney D.T.’s leader on the formation of
the Lyne Ministry was notable for its attitude
re B. R. Wise’s inclusion. The Telegraph loves
Wise as much as Reid does, and it is a bitter pill
to it that his inclusion is the prop of the Ministry,
in whose career the D.T. takes for the present a
friendly interest. The Telegraph is comforted
by the reflection that the Lyne Ministry contains
a “ strong Freetrade element ” —said element con-
sisting of B. R. Wise (whom the paper accused
last year of selling the country to Protection under
the mask of the Federalist Party) and Jno. L.
Fegan, whose tiscalism is of the very mildest
order.
The British Board of Trade presented a
report to the Imperial Parliament in 1849 recom-
mending one tariff for all Australia, however
many divisions it might split into. The Board
suggested that that tariff should, in the first
instance, be fixed by the Imperial Parliament, but
that the various Australian provinces should have
power to meet annually by representatives and
alter or confirm the tariff as they pleased. The
latter is practically what the Federation will now
do; but the Board of Trade at that remote period
pressed to have its recommendations made part of
the then discussed N.S. W. Constitution Act.
Brisbane Telegraph item (6th Sept.) :
The lying Billite spirit will presently say that the heavy
majority against the Convention Bill was in Brisbane
only.
Considering that there is, at time of writing, a
total majority of over 6200 for the Convention
Bill, and that North Queensland, Central Queens-
land, and South Queensland —outside Brisbane —
have all given majorities for it, and only Brisbane
district gave an anti-Federal majority—well, the
chances are that “the lying Billite spirit” wiU
say something of the kind. The average Billite,
unlike the three ex-parsons of Brisbane Tele-
graph, is a little particular about what he says.
The inclusion of W. H. Wood as Ministei
of Justice in the new N.S.W. Cabinet has greatly
angered the old Oppositionists who have long
fought against Parkes and Reid, and have been
passed over in the day of triumph for “a boy of
28.” The disappointed ones first alleged as the
reason of Wood’s appointment that he was Pre-
mier Lyne’s prospective son-in-law; but Lyne
warmly denied this, whereupon another and much
less creditable (not to say less credible) cause was
assigned. S.M. Herald must have got the tip,
for it named Wood as a certain member of the
Cabinet from the first. The why and the where-
fore of the selection is a mystery, for the new
Minister is prominent neither inside nor outside
the House.
A PERSECUTED KING OF THE RING.
The Vic. Freetraders have utterly re-
nounced their former organ, the Argus, and at
the annual Freetrade dinner went out of their
way to shy things at their erstwhile guide, philo-
sopher, and friend. Irvine, M.L.A., who owes
his seat in Parliament to the paper named, ridi-
culed the idea that Freetraders could accept a
compromise of a 20 per cent, maximum duty
to the outside world, as suggested by the
Argus. Following Irvine,- came Max Hirsch,
who burned with Freetrade indignation at
the very thought 'of a compromise, and
dubbed the Argus the “new Protectionist.”
Surely, he continued, it can be said with truth,
“The old Protectionists chastised us with whips,
but the new Protectionists chastise us with scor-
pions.” And the Argus actually gives pro-
minence to these remarks.

Hundreds of people flocked to North Mel-
bourne the other night under the impression that
the district was in flames. As a matter of fact, the
alarm arose from a brilliant illumination at Fitz-
gerald Bros, drapery establishment, laid on to
advertise the opening out of the firm’s latest and
greatest stock of new Spring goods.*
When The Bulletin visited the Art
Furnishing Establishment of Messrs. A. Hall &
Co. (563-7 George-street, Sydney), and inspected
the wares there displayed, it was badly tempted
to rush off and promptly burn down its hereditary
castle in order that it might have the chance of
rebuilding and refurnishing “ from parapet to
basement ” with articles combining in such a high
degree, artistic design, beauty of finish, and solid
durability.*
Boss Booth, jun., of the Salvarmy, still
tries to squeeze the Westralian Govt, into grant-
ing more concessions for the proposed “ over-sea ”
colony at Collie —chief among them authority to
concentrate the improvements on the selections,
and also to have all the latter held in the name of
Booth, sen. But Westralia, after a sad experi-
ence of the big land-grabber, isn’t taking to him
so kindly as it did. By the way, considering the
splendid coal-deposits there are at Collie, the
drum-and-bonnet brigade appears to have shown
its usual hawk-like financial talent in its selection
of the proposed colony.
