Messrs. FRY & DERHAM, Queen-street, or any Member of the Stock Exchange.
THIS Company is being formed for the purpose of acquiring and working a very valuable mining
property situated in the well-known mining centre of Maryborough, consisting of Lease No.
4308. The Lease comprises about one mile of McCallum’s Creek, Crngie, extending for a distance of
over 1000 yards on either bank. The creek itself averages about 30 yards in width. The Vendors are
negotiating for the adjoining property, which will be handed over to the Shareholders at cost.
The early history of these diggings, in which were the Emu, Cockatoo, Gibraltar, Talbot, and
several others, is sufficient inducement to investors to accept the genuineness of the proposition. The
ground has already been proved to contain very rich alluvial deposits, also many thousands of tons of
battery sand, which owing to the crude and primitive appliances used in former days, fully 30 to 40
percent, of the quicksilver and gold escaped into the creek, and there can be no doubt that a very
large portion of this could be easily recovered by a complete up-to-date and inexpensive plant.
The Directors visited the property and took a number of samples at random, and upon being
assayed by Messrs. Jaques Bros, the following result was obtained : 3dwts. 3grs. per ton, and this sand
was obtained at four feet above water level.
The plant to be erected on this property will be capable of treating72o tons per week at (say)only
2dwts., which is putting the return at the very lowest estimate, and would show profits as under :
RESULTS OF TWO MEN who were on the Lease pending flotation, obtained in a few weeks upwards of 201 b.
of quicksilver and amalgam, their appliances being merely long-handled shovels and a tub, some 51b. of same
being retorted produced 6dwt. of gold. This sample can lie seen at the Office of Messrs. Fry and Derham.
E. PRESTON, Esq. 423 b Little Collins-street.
Dear Sir.—l certify that I have treated the small parcel of mercury which you have handed to me weighing
2oz. 9dvvt. 10grs., which produced 3dwt. 15grs. of amalgam, and this yielded 2dwt. 14grs. retorted gold.
Faithfully yours, R. CLARKE WILKS, Assayer.
Reports are herewith appended from Mr. J. F. Jackson, Mining Manager (late Town Councillor of Alexandra),
Mr. J. H. Clark, Mr. W. 11. D. Hopton, and Mr. C. Smith.
Early application for shares is absolutely necessary, as a great number have already been applied for, and
priority of application will be strictly adhered to.
Reports of McCallum’s Creek Gold Recovery Syndicate’s Property.
MR. J. F. JACKSON’S (Mining Manager) REPORT.
The property consists of about 1000 yards in the basin of McCallum’s Creek, which lies at an average of about
10 feet below the banks, and on the sites of the following Diggings which have ceased working—viz., The Cockatoo,
Emu, and others. The bed of the creek has never been disturbed since working ceased. I have been intimately
connected with this property since its inception as a gold bearing proposition, and in my opinion a small, complete,
up-to-date plant is all that is required to prove it a highly remunerative investment.
Mr. J. H. CLARK’S REPORT.
E. PRESTON, Esq.—Dear Sir, —In reply to your letter in re McCallum’s Creek, I beg to report as follows I
have lived in the district of Talbot, Craigie and Majorca for the past 25 years, and am thoroughly aequaimed with
the property known as McCallum’s Creek. There were five batteries, viz., Johnston’s, Wallace & Greenway’s,
Hornby’s, Sear’s, and Malesky’s working, which crushed very rich dirt averaging something like 2ozs., and some as
high as 16ozs. to the load, and having worked there, it is within my experience that a large quantity of gold and
quicksilver (estimated at over 1 ton) escaped into the Creek. Of course the primitive and inefficient appliances in
vogue in the early days are responsible for this loss. There can be no doubt that the great bulk of this would be
easily recoverable by ordinary modern appliances. I consider your lease a very valuable one, possessing singular
natural advantages for dredging purposes.
As regards No. 2 Lease, now applied for, it would, in my opinion, prove a very desirable addition to the property
already acquired, as without any additional expenditure it could be easily and profitably worked. It contains the
bed of a stream known as the Back Creek, and portion of McCallum’s Creek, and adjoins the old workings of the
Talbot, Majorca, Gibraltar, and also other good diggings. The flat ground on the sides of the Back Creek should
prove exceedingly rich owing to the loss of amalgam from the several batteries crushing there, and which should be
easily obtained.
Mr. W. H. D. HOPTON’S REPORT.
In my opinion there can be no doubt that you have a most valuable lease, which need only be known to the
public to receive their consideration and response. The tailings from five batteries which worked on your Lease are
still in large quantities in the Creek and on the banks. From dish trials very satisfactory prospects were obtained.
The fact that thousands of tons of alluvial having been puddled at this Creek compels the belief that a great deal
of fine gold was lost and is still in the Creek. Further, the escape of quicksilver from the batteries, carrying with it
gold in quantities, has been proved to be a valuable asset. I have no doubt that a plant consisting of Centrifugal
Hydraulic Pumps, with Nozzles, Engine and Barge, &c , to work the property would very soon show a handsome
return.
Mr. C. SMITH’S REPORT.
E. PRESTON, Esq.—Dear Sir,—At your request I beg to hand you my report on your lease at MeCallum's
Creek. I worked in that district years ago. There had been five batteries working on that portion or the Creek for
many years, and crushing very rich stone. To my knowledge the owner of Finch’s Reef put through many tons of
stone that went 40ozs. to the ton, and the majority of the stone put through averaged 2 ozs., and it isa weil-known
fact that batteries in those days lost fully one-third of the gold. The No. 2 Lease you refer to, consisting of about
a mile and a quarter above your other lease, I remember when there was a succession of puddling machines working
there, and thousands of loads were puddled that went from 6 to 15 ozs. to the load, and the principal part of the
find gold went into the Creek, the greater portion of which must still be there now, and I consider that should you
acquire the second lease you would have two very valuable properties, that should oonnnand the attention of all
investors.
At Povery Point.
H. J. Samuels, the Sydney music-publisher, can furnish
melanch'oly evidence that, Melba wasn’t, a prophetess in
her own country. Some 14 years ago Samuels aforesaid
speculated in a shilling concert at the old A then aium
Hall, Melb. It was to be a mighty tine concert, for a
“bob’-Mrs. Palmer, Melba (then Nellie Mitchell), Broc-
col'in'i, Christie Fuller, and Frances Saville —but the rain
came down in torrents on the appointed night and only
26 people paid for admission. About the same number
of deadheads were also present at 8.15 p. m., when the
manager announced that the show was “ off, ’ and all
money would be returned at the doors. Whereupon the
audience made a rush for the available silver, and
Samuels presently found that the treasury was several
shillings to the bad, through deadheads collecting a
shilling apiece as they passed out.
Dear Bulletin.- Will you kindly inform us if Federa-
tion will make hats very much dearer ? I hope so. Also
caps suitable for night-wear, say going to parties or
plays. At present fashionable hats are so cheap that it
is a torture to go to any Sydney theatre. The first row
in the dress circle —most of them have large things like
washing-tubs stuck on top of them, and they shut out all
the view from anyone else. Stalls are just the same ; pay
your money and come away quite disgusted, informing
evervone you know “ I have been, but I did not see half
of it, as there was a lady sitting in front of me with
something on her head in the shape of horns.” There
was one woman the other night, in evening dress, with
something on her head—it looked to us like one of those
old-time Chinese hats. One man said it was not a woman,
but something fixed up to take a rise out of the people.
He could see over its shoulders only for those confounded
winkers being where the ears ought to be. Is there any
hope that Federation will make large hats ±lB 10s.
a-piece, and caps with big flappers on them at least £l7
'3s. 6£d. ?— Lemuria.
Musical critic J. F. Runciman, discoursing in the
Saturday Review on modern songs, holds that there are
no English songs worth singing. Of the six com-
posers he mentions as doing the most notable work in
this line, two are knowu in Australia. One is Clutsam,
whose book of songs is, he says, “ entirely German,” not
English in character. Of the other, Marshall Hall,
Runciman says that “ his only very fine songs are not
songs, but scenes.” Clearly there is a chance for a good
“ songsmith ” to distinguish himself.
“ Gallery-Man ” : Agnes Robertson (Mrs. Dion Bouci-
cault) tells in “M.A.P.” of how nervous she and Dion were
about the “Colleen Bawn’s ” first production in K.C.
Dublin on account of the staging for the first time of an
Irish priest. I was myself in the historic gallery of the
Dublin Royal on the first night. There was an enormous
house, and I have never since seen any dress-circle to
equal that one, for size, dress, and beauty that bore
examination with a microscope. The Lord-Lieutenant
(Carlisle) was present, and Boucicault sang a song with a
gag in it for His Ex. :
Oh, if I was Emperor of Roosia to command—
Julius Sayser, or the Lord-Liftenant of the Irish 1
The same day I had dropped in at a Dublin church when
Carlisle happened to be giving away Miss Grattan (grapd-
daughter of Henry) to Captain Esmonde, M.P., father of
Sir Thomas Esmonde, the Home-Ruler, who toured
Australia a few years ago. The celebrated Yelverton
marriage-case was on in Dublin when the “ Colleen
Bawn ” was produced there, and Mrs. B. relates that when
she came on as a winsome colleen, a gallery-boy saluted
her with “ Tu-tu ’’—Major Yelverton’s pet name for The-
resa Longworth.
In the recently-published (Oscar Wilde’s) “ Importance
of Being Earnest” (which is even more amusing to read
than to see on the stage), there is a cynical little passage
about Australia that is worth quoting. Cecily, the
heiress, tells her lover Algernon that her guardian wants
hitn to emigrate and is buying his outfit. “ I certainly
wouldn’t let Jack buy my outfit,” says Algernon ; “ he
has no taste in neckties.” Cecily: “ I don’t think you
will require neckties. Uncle Jack is sending you to Aus-
tralia.” Algernon : “ Australia ! I’d sooner die.” Cecily :
“Well, he said at dinner on Wednesday night that you
would have to choose between this world, the next, and
Australia.” Algernon: “Oh, well 1 The accounts I have
received of Australia and the next world are not particu-
larly encouraging. This world is good enough for me,
cousin Cecily.” Certainly Australia is no place for the
author of “ The Importance of Being Earnest.”
Billy Heffernan’s theatre is one of the memories of Old
Bendigo that will live. It was built in the days when the
digger whose head wasn’t too bad went with a hammer
and whipped out a pound or two of gold from the surface-
reefs and got back to breakfast. The “ show ” alongside
the Shamrock didn’t run continuously ; and when it did,
it consisted mostly of a star—one in tights and with hair
a-hanging down its back preferred. Yet the bill for per-
formers ran into something beyond £lO,OOO a year. Hef-
fernan was partnered in the gilded enterprise by John
Crowley ; the latter, however, kept his eye fixed chiefly
on the pub. next door, and, later on, also ioeally managed
Cobb and Co.’s then big business between Melb. and the
goldfields. More years ago than most people can remem-
ber, the partners divided their gold and went their sepa-
rate ways with several sackfulls. Crowley made af ew
attempts to live an old age of retirement; but they all
failed. Finally, he bought the Bendigo theatre, with its
neighboring block of pub. and shops and offices, and then
marked his retirement from the active management of
that by falling and breaking his thigh. It was too much
for a man a good way past 70 ; and so the goldfields lose
one who knew more about the rollicking side of the cham-
pagne days, and who coined more money out of the rol-
licks, than any of his survivors.
The funniest thing is to watch the darkie juggler of
M’Adoo’s Georgia Minstrels co. gravely balancing two
balls on a stick dla Cinquevalli. They are seemingly so
securely balanced that a vigorous shake is needed to dis-
lodge them, yet the performance isn’t intended for a
joke. An amusing travestie of Cinque’s great feat has
been given in England at times. The humorist, whose
previous tricks have commanded respect, puts the two
balls on the top of a billiard-cue and makes a great show
of carefulness in balancing the cue on his forehead. Then
he cautiously lowers it, accepts the applause of the audi-
ence, and casually holds the cue at an angle with the two
balls still sticking in position, just to show he was taking
a rise out of them.
Circus horses, says Dan Fitzgerald, must have some
“ blood ”in them, and a touch of vice is acceptable. The
vice is educated into mannerisms, which circus-people
call “ playful.” Pony Commodore, for- instance, would
be quite capable of chawing or kicking an incautious
admirer—but only in play.
The fine life-size photo, of Melba which stands in Mar-
chesi’s studio was given to her by her ex-pupil on the
understanding that she will bequeath it to the trustees
of Melbourne Art Gallery. The picture will then be a
souvenir of Victoria’s first famous singer and of the
Frenchwoman who “ made” her. Melba owes much to
Marchesi and precious little to her native city, anyhow.
In these days of Castles boom and senseless Bendigolatry
it seems almost incredible that Mrs. Armstrong, the real
original “ future Melba,” was considered fortunate to
realise £6O by her farewell benefit concert in Melbourne.
Tenor John Fuller, in his semi-amateur days, was in
great demand as a club singer in England. One corpora-
tion had him at many successive annual re-unions, and a
favorite song was one from the forgotten opera of
“ Matilda,” called “ The Crown, the Sceptre, and the
Sway." John got ashamed of it at last and introduced
new stuff, but they wanted the old favorite, so one old
gentleman got up to make the request public. He said,
“ Mr. Fuller, I must beg, on behalf of the company, for
that old song we all like so much— 1 The Clown, the
Spectre, and the Shave.’ ”
“ A sight of Amy Castles’ photo.—full face,” writes
“ Old Pro.” to The Bulletin, “ convinces me that she has
a future —in comic opera. Anyone studying the faces of
this class of artist must have noticed the ponderous jaws
and stubborn, pointed chins which give the face-outline
the appearance of a shovel. From Choctaw-princess May
Yohe ” (she told a London interviewer she was quarter-
bred Choctaw Indian), “to frowning Mabel Love, Holy
Hepner, and All-is-vanity Vane, they have the Dubberke
jaw and chin. The latter missed the ’bus in not going
on the stage, as she is the greatest actress of them all.
In fact, jaw seems a factor as potent in music as in mus-
cle. A certain male singer’s jaw casts so deep a shadow
on his neck that I really thought he had forgotten to
wash it.”
A Vic. paper Las a story of a cheerful thief who vic-
timised a cute mining-man during the progress of a con-
juring entertainment at Bendigo. At a wisely-chosen
moment a slim young man, with a clean collar and a
heavenly smile, slid along the best seats murmuring,
“Will any gentleman kindly lend the professor a gold
watch?" The mining man in question obliged with a
bulky timepiece worth about 60 guineas— well, there you
are I The watch and the young- gentleman with the
heavenly expression passed out by a side door and are
now long-felt wants in the local detective-office.
“Ancient”: The best-drawn character in “Lord
Quex ” is that of the old governor of Umbos, gay old dog
Sir Chichester Frayne. Consider how he takes off the
jaded old bucks you see every da} - in the private bars,
where they imagine they are rending with passion the
bosoms of the painted drink-slingers. The way he bends
forward and smirks, and throws out his chest and leers,
and curls one leg round the other, and narrowly escapes
blowing up with the conceit that he has ’em on a string.
And then, in the morning—when his liver has gone
crook—what a hideous lot they are then ! Yes, Chichester
Frayne, of Umbos, West Africa, is a masterpiece ; or else
Dartrey made him one, which amounts to the same
thing.
Celia Dainpier, the violinist, is the daughter of an
Auckland “ Cheap John "—the child of his old age. Old
Dampier’s reputation as a shrewd, keen trader in any-
thing and everything was not congruous with his phy-
siognomj', refined almost to the point of femininity.
Celia's mother, a black-eyed Irish colleen, won the old
man’s heart whilst acting as a saleswoman amongst his
“wonderful bargains.”

“S.”: After seeing a good deal of the real nigger on
the variety stage, I conclude that he is only a fairly good
imitation of the white man who imitates the real nigger.
D. : Priests are very common in drama, and what
a good old man the dramatic priest always is ! I can’t
remember how many of them I have seen left in lonely
places with nice-looking girls, and not one has attempted
to kiss the girl. No matter how close she gets, his rever-
ence on the stage doesn’t even hug her, even as a lather.
Rev. Freeman Wills, brother of the late W. G. Wills,
has gone one better than parson Walters by writing a
play with no Bible in it. The recent successful stage
adaptation of Dickens’s “ Tale of Two Cities ” is the
work of Freeman Wills, who also paints a little and leads
his own rifle-corps into action at Hyde Park. As the
vicar of a London city parish, he doesn’t get overbur-
dened with religious duties, seemingly.
Incledon, a popular London actor early this century,
was jingoistic—while Napoleon threatened an invasion of
England—and astonished the Drury Lane performers in
“Hamlet,” at rehearsal one morning, with a speech:
“ How long are we going on with this twopenny-halfpenny
nonsense, with the very existence of England threat-
ened?” Then he went off to a review, and gave a little
boy threepence to carry his gun uphill.
The recent bull-and-lion encounter of Roubaix, France,
was not a “ fight” but a slaughter, as Leo wouldn’t come
up for the second round, and was thereupon ignomini-
ously slain by Taurus, who for his part only lost an ear.
The yells of the 14,000 spectators had probably much to
do with the unexpected result, as all the Leo family are
peculiarly susceptible to shouting.
The growth of the drama in the suburbs. “ What is
silver coin a legal tender for?” asked an assistant lately
in a suburban State school. “Please, sir,” said Willie
Woolvvash, “for th’ play at th’ Townorl.”
Robert Buchanan and Harriet Jay, joint authors of
that gloomy melodrama, “ Alone in London ” (or
some such title) have been putting their heads together
again. Their recent results are a French Revolution
story which Mrs. Langtry is staging regardless of expense,
and a new version of “The Heavenly Twins.” Judging
from the solid merit of the melodrama aforesaid, the part-
nership of Buchanan and Jay will produce a fine play
sooner or later.

will be sent post-free from this office on receipt of
price, ss.
LIPTON’S LATEST.
THE SHAMROCK IN NEW YORK.
Sib Thomas Lipton has had a good time in
America with the reporters, every one of whom to
a man —and a woman, too —thirsted to tell the
world all the secrets of the Shamrock’s construc-
tion ; all the inner particulars of the great mer-
chant’s tea trade; all the reasons which inspired
him with a desire to give Killarney to the Irish
people at a cost of <£50,000 to himself, and,
generally speaking, to bring to light everything
he desired to keep dark.
They made him talk whether he liked it or not,
and thus got some fearful and wonderful informa-
tion out of him. But when he had succeeded in
making Miss Gimlet, of the Herald, believe that
he shipped a hundred and seventeen thousand
chests of tea to Mesopotamia every year, and
when Miss Sharpset, of the World, laid before
her editor the full details of a breeding establish-
ment for Bactrian camels which he was supposed
to have located at Chiselhurst, they began to give
him a wide berth. All but Sarah Jane Screwby,
of the Mercury. She vowed to get the better of
him, and she is a very determined woman. Miss
Screwby is a severe lady, prim and angular ; per-
severing and inquisitive; impervious to satire,
and as shrewd as a loan-office manager.! She
fixed him with her cold, grey eye, and thus she
began:—
“Sir, you have grossly imposed upon two of
my colleagues. That is not the way to repay the
kindly interest the great American nation takes in
its visitors. Now, sir, you must not try to impose
upon me, but I will make a bargain with you. I
will tell you, sir, how to win the great yacht-race
you come here to contest if you will tell me truth-
fully what I desire to know about your yacht and
your trade.”
“ Done, madam,” he replied. “ Fire away.”
“ Sir, American ladies do not fire away ; they
goon ! Now, sir, if you want to win the yacht-
race, you supply your sailors, I’m told you have-
sixty, with plenty of Kupper’s Elberfeld Lager,
which is a strengthening, and stimulating, and
exhilarating beverage, perfectly wholesome and
good, made of pure malt and hops, and they will
win that race for you hands down. The British
sailor, sir, loves his beer, and that beer is the best
in the world.”

The cheerfulness with which the average patron
of Randwick Leyerand flat swallows any “information ”
from the paddock, and planks his cash on it, is surprising.
In Randwick Leyer reserve the crowd collects along the
paddock railings, and frequently a “punter” conies
along, and pulls his nose or his ears, smacks his chest,
and otherwise ill-uses himself by way of signalling a tin
to a friend. The caller immediately rushes for the
ring, with the crowd sprinting after him to see w hat lie
books —and that nay has the cash piled on him. On the
first day of rect-nt Spring meeting, writer chased a friend
to ask him if the baby was any better, lost the friend,
and found that the crowd was following him. To yet
rid of them, asked a “ hook ” —“ What price Australian
Star?” which said crowd promptly rushed. But A.S.
won, and for the rest of the day this scribe was followed
altout by a “push,” that gathered round whenever he
put his humble “dollar” on, and followed his lead.

“Gun”: Saw a strong thing at a Sydney
suburban course t’other day. Scratching-time passed;
6to 4 offered first favorite, 7to 1 next; only one in it,
all being right. Half-dozen punters hacked 7to 1 chance
heavily. The consternation among the Ikeys when a
little later they saw the 6 to 4 flier’s number drop out. may
be imagined. There’s a penalty for scratching late, and
there are supposed to he stewards. This writer never
heard of former being inflicted, nor of latter moving in
any way. As between backer and hook it is probably a
fair fight, for Ikey (t he wolf) frequently has a hot “ pot ”
in his hag, and why not punter (the lamb) now and
again? But how’ differently they take their gruel! The
wolf howls loudly and long, but the lamb treats it as a
matter of course—the game is played on him so often.

About the best-bred horse in V.R.C. Derby this
year is the Tasmanian colt The Sirdar, by Bill of Portland
from Martini-Henry’s full sister Wood-nymph (by Musket
—Sylvia). Martini-Henry was a maiden when he won
V.R.C. Derby, and a Tasmanian correspondent writes
that The Sirdar looks good enough to repeat the coup of
his distinguished relative.
» * • •
Although Australian breeders have so far been
unable to raise a colt on Australian soil with a show in
the English Derby, it looks as if the day is not far distant
when, at all events, a young ’un sired by an Australian
horse will have a look-in. Amongst the 299 entries for
the 1901 Derby the sires Sir Mod red, Trenton, Carbine
and Carnage are represented, and such a quartet ought
to raise something pretty good.

A bill is now before Maoriland legislature to
kill the totalisator by the gradual extinction of licenses
for its use. Its author, teetotal Tommy Taylor,
raved to the effect that “ since the tote came
into existence the number of race-meetings have
not doubled or trebled, they have quadrupled.” As
a matter of fact, there were in M.L. in 1879 (the year
before the tote came into existence) 164 race-meetings,
while in 1899, for 390,000 more population, there were
only 150 ! Query : How much “tip” does the demoralis-
ing bookmaker give to the Tommy Taylor crowd that it
should risk its immortal soul telling; such awful yarns in
his interests ? And does the Tommy Taylor crowd «ant
to get rid of the tote, and let the bookie have the Held to
himself again, simply because the tote has no funds to
grease venal apostles with 'I

A recent peculiar incident in connection with
W. A. consultations. A sweep was got up on Meuzies’
Cup, Aug. 31. The only starter, Speculation, had a walk-
over. The first money was 41(100, and the promoter
being puzzled wlmt to do with 2nd, 3rd, and 4th
prizes, as well as amount for starters, appealed to several
sporting authorities. Opinions were divided, but the
best advice begot, in this writer’s opinion, was to hand
the lot over to the drawer of Speculation’s number. Isn’t
this case unique in the sweep business?

* » * *
“Rowley ” Pickering, for more than a quarter
of a century the best-known sporting writer on the
Sydney press (his father before him edited old Sydney
Bell's Life, long ago incorporated with Svdnky Mail)
and one of the best judges of form in A ustralia, lives
luxuriously at his comfortable home near Gosford,
N.S.W., and spends much of his leisure rowing on the
Narrara—Kendall’s Narrara. On last A.J.C. Derby day
he was presiding at a country cricket council and told an
enquirer that he didn’t even know the names of the
horses entered for the Derby !
• » • •
“ Jack ” McGowan, the manliest and decentest
png. that ever broke a nose, made his last Australian ap-
pearance at Victoria Hall (Melbourne) last Saturday
evening, and is now on the briny with Bland lioit’s Co.
Jack has been out of fighting for six months, and the lest
seems to have done him a world of good. On Saturday
he faced “Tot ” Higgins, who offered to bet his weight
was only 9.8, but who looked a stone heavier. McGowan
was three inches shorter and only 9.4, and each man
entered the ring with a record of having gained and lost
one verdict over the other. The fight was a ralding ex-
hibition of science with enough blood and hair to satisfy
the most fastidious. Higgins scored at the outset, then
McGowan went in and landed Higgins’ head
till at the end of the fifth round he resembled
the “ horrible red man ” that correspondent Steevens
describes in “ With Kitchener to Khartoum.” For 15
rounds the little man landed lightning swings on
the other’s face, hut Higgins, with a bunged eye and
cuts on his lips, forehead, cheek and nose, seemed as
eager at die end of the 20th round as when lie opened.
The verdict was unquestionably to M’Gowan, and he had
certainly fought the best fight, of his life. He never gave
a chance, and his clinches to avoid hits were as pretty as
the hits of most men. When the verdict was given
M’Cowan’s chief barraeker, Walter Baker, howled with
elation, and the good-looking little pug. came forward to
make a farewell speech, wherein he eulogised Higgins for
his manly fighting. The crowd then called on Higgins
for a speech, and the beaten man leaned over the ropes
and said—“ The decision wasn’t a fair one ; that’s ail that
I can say." As the house hooted; he turned back,
“ Them as hoots,” he remarked, “can come into the ring
and I'll fight ’em, one after the other.”

Before this appears in print, the money will
probably be up and articles signed for a match (£lOO
a-side) at Yass, between C. Gorrick and C. Massev, for
the pigeon-shooting championship of N. S. Wales.
Massey recently won the title from a big field of cracks
at N.S.W. Gun Club meeting, killing 20 straight off ; but
Gorrick is no duffer, and will probably supple a stiff task
for Massey. The latter, as well as holding tiie'blue ribbon,
has to his name, amongst other feats, the grassing of 66
straight, and 92 out of 100 on the same day. Gorrick
beat Massey once in a 25-birds match—the’former, on
28yds., accounting for 24; and the latter, on 33yds..
for 23.
• * * *
N.S.W. champion pro. swimmer, “Turns”
Cavill got home on Saturday last by the Orizaba after six
months’ absence in England and elsewhere. He told a
Bulletin man there was nothing in the game “down
under.” Anyone labelled “ from Australia” had the
field to himself. Percy Cavill would be champion of the
world if they’d give him a chance, but they won’t;
money-up doesn’t fetch a response. Little Freddy Lane’s
swimming was a revelation. The sport is pool)v‘patron-
ised by the public ; less than 200 saw the half-mile cham-
pionship swum at Southport. ” Turns” describes Jarvis,
England’s best amateur, who broke the 1000yds. world s
record recently, as a thick-set chap of only about sft. sin.,
but over 40in. round the chest. “What sort of man is
Greasley?” was asked by a listener. “Oh,” replied
Cavill, innocently, “only an ordinary-looking fellow,
something after your style.” Collapse of querist.
“Tunis” was in Paris for a while and says Australian
boys could make exhibitions of the French swimming
champions. He vows he'll never quit this little speck on
tiie globe again, and expresses himself as disgusted at
having expended so much time and energy to no purpose.
Swimmer Fred Lane still continues repeating
his best Australian form in a remarkable manner in Eng-
land. Cabled last week that lie shattered the British
“ fastest” for 300 yards at Blackpool, putting up 3mius.
47 4-ssecs.—t l-ssec. slower than his own world’s record
(3mins. 46 3-ssec.), established at Farmer’s Bath (Sydney)
on Feb. 4 of this year. Coupled with the fact
that, in Sydney late last season, Lane made
new figures for the furlong, and swam exactly
the same time when he won the English champion-
ship by 18 yards some weeks ago, it would seem that
there is very little in the notion that Australian athletic
form is injuriously affected by the English climate. Any-
how, the question of water temperature does not enter
into the matter as far as the two performances men-
tioned are concerned, both accomplished in tepid water—
if the writer is not mistaken.

* * * *
After Fitzroy and South's final struggle, the
Melh. football premiership fell to the former. South was
beaten by one solitary point, after a game of the most
willing character. A pouring rain fell all the early pare
of the match, and most, of the tussle was a scramble in
the slush, but the players scrambled in a free, open and
plucky style, that, roused the ban-ackers to hysteria. The
game was played out to the bitter end ; men rolled and
tossed one anol her about with the vigor of a decade ago,
and stoush was dealt out on one or two occasions. The day
finished with Fitzroy 3 goals 9 behinds to South’s 3 goals
8 behinds, and the Southern supporters left the ground
calling the umpire a cow, and swearing that the last goal
was not kicked over the blanky mark. Fitzroy has now
to reply to the challenge of Footscra.v, the premiers of the
much-despised Association. If a meeting takes place,
every real sportsman ought to turn up and barrack for
the boys of the smellful suburb, for the League assumes
immense airs of superiority over the Association, and the
defeat of its elect would do a heap of good in attracting
public interest to the other body. But the Fitzrovs are
a machine-tike crowd, and Footscray, with the assistance
of all its hone-mills anti abattoirs, could scarcely put such
a “strong” combination in the field..

The last nine missionaries despatched from
Melbourne to China include a suburban grocer,
a country bank manager, and a crack footballer—
“Tugger” Hannah, who led Carlton club for several
seasons. After all there must be something in a faith
which can convert a grocer, a bank manager and a
footballer 1 Hannah will not find himself lonely in the
Flowery Land, for among the missionaries already there
is Joyce, the captain of the Fernside club of a few seasons
ago. Joyce is one of the cleverest, and pluckiest plavers
that Victoria ever saw, and though he was chased by
every senior club in Melb. he stuck to the second-rate
Fernsides on account of their Moral Influence. The Fern-
side Foolball Club ivns a wondrous institution, composed
of godly youth, who acted up to t.he tenets of their re-
ligion e'en in the hottest corners of the last quarter.
“ Laugwidge " of all sorts was forbidden by the Fernside
committee on pain of expulsion, and Fernside players
would pause in pursuit of the ball to rebuke an opponent
who said “ blanky.” Other clubs alleged that the Fern-
sides kicked off with prayer, and Imllocked with appro-
priate texts. Rut with the d purl tire of Joyce the death-
knell of Fernside was rune, and to-day its old ground is
occupied by a Paean club, which curses with picturesque
originality' and “deals out stoush ’’ when occasion
requires.

“Sport ” (Gympie) asks the question : “A bets
B that tlie Australians win ihe rubber in the recent test-
matches ; B bets A that the English would. Who wins
the wager?” And the answer is emphatically A. The
spirit of the waeer took the possibility, or rather t lie pro-
bability, of “ draws ” into consideration. A bet B that
the Australians woidd win a majority of the matches that
were not drawn. There was only one undrawn match,
and the Australians won it. It was just as much a win-
ning of the rubber for bet purposes as if they had beaten
the Englishmen every time.

There’s trouble ahead for interprovineial cricket
next, season. Adelaide wants the gate-money of matches
between South Australia and N.S.W. to lie pooled and
afterwards whacked evenly, but N.S.W. doesn’t see it.
S.A. conveniently forgets that during the past decade
her Association might have struck hard times on at least
two occasions but for timely lifts from the N.S.W. Asso-
ciation.

Dear Bulletin. —Paragraphist “ Cricket ” is
flying in the face of history when he asserts that,
“Barring wet weather, or an unusual collapse, no test
match (in England) can be finished in three days.’’ One
of the recent five tests was actually finished ; another
would have been finished, with half a day to spare, but
for the rain, and it was only the slow hatting of the Aus-
tralians that prevented them winning the first test.
In days of yore, when the Australians were weaker in
batting and the All-Englanders st-Oroger in howling,
test matches were almost invariably finished within the
allotted time (weather permitting), though the wicket
may sometimes have been as hard as “Cricket’s ’’ head.
A “collapse’’— i.e., the defeat—of the Australians was
then the usual result. Trott’s 1896 team was the first to
win a test match on a batsman’s wicket in England. The
trouble this time has been (hat both sides beat, the bowl-
ing, as a rule, but a Richardson (at his best) would have
managed to win the game for either side within three
days. After the second game it looked as though Jones
might continue to vanquish the Englishmen. Jones,
howevc-r, seemed to go “ off ” as the English batting came
“ on,” and in the last two tests, anyway, each team mas-
tered the other’s bowling. With Jones doing his deadliest
for the Australians, and a Richardson or even a Peel
doing likewise for All England, a match would rarely be
left drawn on the third day.—F.

Touching the comparatively barbaric state of
sporting show-grounds in England—the present Austra-
lian team complains that a shower-bath in the dressing-
room is the exception, rather than the rule, and the only
alternative is a sponge and a basin. Probably no senior
club in Australia is without a shower-bath in its pavilion.

Admitting that England has half-a-dozen capital
batsmen for every one that Australia can show at present,
the case of Albert Trott proves clearly the weakness of
English cricket. Albert, the ex-Australian, goes to the
country which is still the champion hatting country, and
proves himself to be its best “all-round ” man.

Probably one reason why Victor 'Trnmper has
not done as well in England as he might is that he has
been putting in several hours an evening as correspon-
dent for the management. Applications for free tickets
to the matches and to the Australian rooms on every
ground have been swarming in from all sorts and condi-
tions of people, and to the bulk of these requests
Trumper has had to return a polite refusal—a heavy item
in work to which he should not have been put. A man
cannot put in the night with the pen and play his best
next day.

The trouble between the cycling bodies of Vic.
and N.S.W. promises some interesting developments.
The recent decision of the N.S.W. League to have an
amateur section was as the fat in the fire, and the N.S.W.
Cyclists Union, afraid of being undermined, called a con-
ference of amateur associations at which it was decided
that those organisations alone should control amateurism,
thus leaving the League and its amateur-scheme out in
the cold. The Vic. League made things worse by inti-
mating that it purposed recognising the Cyclists’ Union
as controlling N.S.W. amateur cycling ; whereupon the
N.S.W. League charged Vic. professionals with a breach
of the reciprocity agreement„and claimed the £lOO for-
feit provided for bv said compact. During the slang
whanging the N.S.W. C.U. charged the N.S.W. League
with having boasted, when first formed, that it intended
to wipe out the amateurs altogether, whereupon the
League retorted that 12 months back, when the League
was supposed to lie moribund, the amateurs altered their
constituuon so as to embrace professionalism and thus
swallow the League; a little dodge which failed in-
glorious]}’.

N.S.W. cyclist B j n Goodson was not ns success-
ful as anticipated in World’s Cycling events at Montreal,
Canada, last month. lie got away with the five-mile
amateur handicap by half-a-whecl from the Scotch crack
(Caldow), was unplaced for Columbus trophy (open to
winners of all amateur races on programme), finished
second in his heats of the one-mile and half-mile races,
and was third in semi-final and final of the latter. In the
10(» kilometres world championship Gondson finished 2nd,
four miles behind Nelson, and got third position with
Caldow (Scotland) in the tandem pursuit race (five miles)
less than 16 secs, away from the winner. The Canadian
press generally thought the Australian should have done
better, and most followers of the game in these parts will
agree with the opinion.

The late Torn Sparkes’ first big fight was with
“ liungaree” (Gorrick) at B itany in ’46, when Tom was
so much of a youth that, as the pugs, shaped up, yells
arose of “Take the boy home!” They changed their
tune a little later. The “boy” got through over 60
savage rounds, and though the referee eventually decided
against Sparkes on an alleged foul, Gorrick himself
admitted afterwards that the decision was wrong.
Another big fight was Tom’s 94 fierce rounds with King
at White Gum Hollow (N.S.W.), Sparkes being worsted.
“Old Timer ” : Re reeently-dead Tom Sparkes.
His fnrt.c for many years pist, was arranging cocking-
mains on George's River (N.S. \V.), in which lie was an
adept, especially as r gar.led the adjustment of spurs. In
tliis operation the slightest turn of the steel-pricks one
way or the otln r from the proper direction will result in
injury to the bird wearing them. Tom, alter fixing
the spurs, used to look along them much as a billiard-

of regular occurience every few months and took the

“ C. .T.” : Lured by the glowing accounts of fear-
some snaps in “ Sporting NHions” I went down to the
Gjlden Gate Club in fear and trembling, expecting to see
something about lb times more terrible than ever hap-
pened in a Roman arena. The door of the dread place
was surrounded by a crowd of spitting, straw-hatted,
tired-looking young bucks. On the step a fair young
man earnestly abjured everybody to get a good seat
before the crush came. Prices, Is., 2s. and 3s. The en-
trance is through a long, narrow pas-age in which flaring
gas-lights maintain the idea that something important is
waiting at the otller end. Arrived at the other end,
there was a feeling of relief when a polite young man
asked to see my ticket, arid sought out the most com-
fortable seat in the division, and hoped I would enjoy
the show. The G.G.A.C. is a ramshackle, tumble-down
sort of barn with a gable roof of corrugated iron, in
which are four sky lights. Eight gas-lights struggled to
p’erce the rollingcloudsof tobacco-smoke. Two musicians
d s oursed mu-icon piano and \iolin. In tlieeentre is the
‘ ring,” a square space surrounded by two strong ropes
stretched from four corner posts. The seals immediately
around it are the stalls —two rows of forms without hacks
or cushions. On one side, the southern, is the d.c., a
raised platform about 3ft. high, furnished with old-
fashioned kitchen chairs, the ones with the backs adorned
with a red apple and two nondescript leaves. The wall
behind those chairs is dadoed with lurid chintz and
decorated with portraits of pugs in war-paint, and a
picture of a bygone scrap. The pit occupies all the rest of
the building and consists of tiers of plain boards that rise
to the very roof. Suddenly two apparitions, clad only in
bathing trunks, dirty socks and tennis shoes got into the
ring and sal down in their corners. Then came a
peremptory order to put out all pipes, cigarettes and
“stinkers” (presum ably alleged cigars). The appari-
tions rose up and danced round each oilier twirling their
hands. After an exhausting' exhibition of this, the Surrey-
Tlls Slaughterer stamped his right foot ferociously and the
Darlington Digger (led round the ring three times in a
stale of abject terror. Then the gong sounded and they
sank down in their corners and hung iheir paws over the
ropes, while henchmen waved towels, and two dirty dogs
blew mouthfuls of water over them. It, reminded me of
the Chinese cooks making iced cakes. The gong sounded
again, and they bobbed round some more and twirled
their hands in a manner that was awful to see, till sud-
denly, without warning, the Digger stamped his left foot
savagely and the Slaughterer tied round the ring 2£
times in a state of intense dread. The excitement became
terrific, and the audience howled itself hoarse while the
Slaughterer got in his leg-work. Then the gong again.
More towel work —and it was work, the towel men earn
their bit. The pugs, faced each other more cautiously
next tune, and the house held its breath, and the
Slaughterer walked round twirling his hands fiercely.
The Digger looked pale, ceased to twirl his fists,
and contented himself by stamping both feet when-
ever the Slaughterer came too near. Suddenly the
Digger sprang in and smote the Slaughterer right
on the boxing-gloves. It was a serious blow,
and the house gasped. Then the Slaughterer
rushed in close and gave the Digger a considerable bash.
Gong! The towels waved. The two combatants
were honorably spat upon by their seconds, and
the next round was due. But there was delay, consulta-
tion, hesitation, and then it was announced that the
Slaughterer had thrown up the sponge—knocked out by
the i wirling of his hands. There were no ears ripped off,
no sprinkling of sand to soak tip the gore, and all eternity
seemed to roll between the (Jolisamm and the Golden
Gate. There are more gore and brains to lie seen in
any ordinary street scrap any day. Several other alleged
contests were on the bill, but I had seen enough.

Some people denounce the “off -theory” as the
reason why test-matches are left unfinished al the end of
three days, and cricket in general is a slower business
than when the first object of bowling was to hit the
stumps. On the other hand, the theorists maintain that
howling which is calculated to miss the wicket is essen-
tial to file occasional success of a direct attack. The
modern batsman must he taken by surprise if he is to
be taken at all, as far as his slumps are concerned,
or he must he lured by strange devices into giving
the field a chance. Probably the expert and the inexpert
are equally foolish in Iheir contentions. Long before the
advent of “ off theory ” there was an outcry in England
for an increase in the height and width of wickets. Bats-
men were said to he healing the howlers. Then the
Australian Elevens put new life info the game fora time.
Batsmen ceased to have too much the best of it. Spof-
forth, Boyle and George Palmer were already waning
lights, and the hat was going very strong again before
the “off theory” came into full force. It has remained
in force. As to whether it was really more effective at the
outset than it is nowadays nobody can say for certain,
inasmuch as nobody knows whether the new batsmen are
superior to the fellows of 15 years ago and would have
been capable of punishing the old bowlers more severely
than they wallop Trumble, llearne and co. One thing is
certain—the arrival of the “off theory” made no per-
ceptible difference to W. G. Grace’s average. Tub
Bt i.lktix has no profound faith in any howling but the
very fast stuff, which no batsmen can “ place” carefully,
and which mostly scatters the bails if it gets past his
hat. The Richardson-Jones theory defies criticism when
put into practice by a man of Richardson-Jones ability.
The only possible better theories have yet to be dis-
covered by some Cinquevalli of the future.

Mr. Horatio Bottouiley’s new scheme is the
Aesociated Consols, to take over the Associated Southern,
K.W. Associated, and Auxiliary Associated, the total
capital of which amounted to over a million. Whenever
writer enquired about any of these mines on the field,
the usual reply was—“ Not worth a rap.” Boltomle.v has
been for the last two years booming them in his joint-
stock circulars, with the result that scrip came down to
2s. 6d. each. Now the game is to put all these “ cats ”in
a bag, with a capital of £'300,000, and make a fresh start.

London Ckitic, after quoting extracts from a
glowing report by the manager of the Auxiliary Asso-
ciated about the richness of the lodes, remarks that
“ such a report will certainly come as a surprise to Kal-

leases were lately closed down, and it is well-known that
Bottoinley originally bought them very cheaply indeed.
The Great Boulder returns are to be published
monthly in future instead of fortnightly as heretofore,
bringing it into line with the surrounding mines. The
latest is that rich gold has been pierced by the bore at
900 ft. Three weeks ago it was given out that a similar
strike had been made at 800 ft. Jt is certainly a good
sign, but cannot be taken as absolute proof of either rich-
ness or permanency. Anyhow a bore only proves a few
inches of ground—and one of the easiest things in the
world to “salt” is a bore.

An Australian-in-China to The Bulletin
China is turning out a dismal mining failure, except per-
haps Mongolia, where there is a little gold. All tlie coal
measures are proving small and very faulty. As a rule
the seams are on edge, while the reputed rich lodes of
copper and silver have not. yet been discovered. In March
last gold was found at Wei-llai-Wei. Four Australians
went to the god-forsaken spot, and, locating a likely-look-
ing granite-quartz formation, applied to the Admiral for
mining l ights. They were told to produce certificates of
good character, and after obtaining these documents the
Admiral said lie would allow no one to work gold near the
Port, as the discovery of a payable lead would attract bad
characters to the place. Bill Adams, late of Broken Hill
and Coolgardie, has given up the Pritchard Morgan syn-
dicate, and i now mining-expert extraordinary to the
King of Corea. Hoover, late manager of the Sons of
Gwalia (W.A.), and Wilson, of Bewick, Morcimr, and Co.,
are exploring in Mongolia for British people. George
Kingswell, late of Coolgardie, has bought Shanghai Daily
Pbksk, and celebrated his first, week's occupation by
receiving four writs for libel.
The Amoor River, which separates the Chinese
from the Russian Empire, contains in its bed an incal-
culable amount of gold. An Australian, who recently
went miles up the huge stream, estimates that
190,009 Coolies live by hand-dredging. The Russians,
however, will allow no Englishman to work a dredge, and
so the country is absolutely closed.
* • * »
Westralian Govt, is pretty well bled in the
shape of subsidies to medicine-shops (sometimes thinly
disguised as hospitals) and medical funds. It pays even
where wealthy companies—as on the goldfields and at the
timber-mills—might be expected to part a trifle. At latest,
the Collie-Wallsend miners had got a promise of Govt. £
for £ for their fund ; and so the co. is still further relieved
—it doesn’t contribute a cent.
It is customary for Q. Govt, (writes a miner to
Tim Bullktin) to send geologists to examine those
mineral districts which are thinly populated by the miner
on his “ own.” Then, after months, or perhaps years, is
published a report, which the man trying to "make a
living on the spot will be lucky to see at all, and even
then the technicalities in which it abounds make it nearly
useless to him. Also the g.g.’s, although quite willing to
accept information about outcrops, dykes, lodes, &c.,
rarely, in return, even so much as name the rocks from
which they take their specimens. They should be in-
structed to give a short lecture in each camp, describing
the leading geological features of the neighbourhood in
plain language. The time would be regained in the
future, through the miners being more competent to
supply necessary data.
* * * *
Broken Hill miners believe nothing more firmly
than that bad accidents run in threes : and scores of cases
in.support arc remembered. No serious mishap occurs
but bosses and men alike look out for the others—and
therein, probably, lies the explanation. Miners are often
blamed by cocksure newspaper correspondents and others
for heedlessness : it is a commoner experience that they
get into trouble when they become Hdgetty. In some
parts of the world (for the creed is pretty general among
miners) work is either temporarily suspended after a bad
accident or scores of men take leave. Another explana-
tion, however, is that climatic changes (and they are
very sudden and-very severe at Broken Hill) have most
to do with falls of earth, which are responsible for most
accidents underground.

were much admired. The gentleman is full of cleverness
and cheek.

Maggie Stirling, who went ’Ome more for the benefit
of her health and to pick up new songs and “ wrinkles,”
than with the idea of making money by the trip, seems to
have been encouraged to stay awhile. She sang at the
Duke of Westminster’s and other high-toned houses dur-

arranged to stay with Lady Darrell in September and go

In a Tasmanian mining township theatre, the other
nigtit, a local identity with vine-leaves in his hair rose in
the middle of the performance and passed various more
or less witty remarks on the play and the players.
The audience smiled and the management sw ore softly.
A policeman was appealed to. “More than 1 dare do,”
he replied. “If I shifted the old chap I’d lie the most
unpopular man in the force. Everybody knows him.
Lave him alone.” The ancient persisted in his beery
interjections. The doorkeeper suddenly remembered a
time-honored plan. Elbowing his way to the interrupter
he whispered smilingly, “ Comeanavadrink ! ” The old
chap slowly turned a fishy eye upon the inviter and said,
as lie hauled a whisky bottle from his shirt, “No (hie)
fear; have one (hit:) with me ! ” So he stayed and inter-
jected and the hobby looked the other way. The affluent
old inhabitant can do pretty well as lie pleases in Tassy.
An ex-telegraph man writes:—“At Her Majesty's
(Sydney) the telegraphing in ‘ Secret Service ’ was a sight
for the gods. When the instrument clangs the receiving
clerk immediately starts to write ata frantic rate, whereas
he should first let Hie man who is calling know that he is
in attendance. Apparently the sender rushes to his key
and starts his message to headquarters without troubling
if there is anybody in attendance to take it down, and
does nor know if lie is clicking to the winds or to an
operator till the receiver suddenly stops him and by a
thump of his key signifies that lie has had enough. The
proper procedure is as follows : —Sender calls headquarters
with its telegraphic call, which may be HQ ; lie simply
bangs away HQ, HQ. IIQ, I IQ, HQ, till the mail at head-
quarters replies ‘ HQ lieie,’ and in war-time a special pass-
word acknowledgment. Then lie sends liis message,
and when it is finished gives the signal, ‘ end of message,’
and tlie receiver (the man at HQ) acknowledges tile
receipt by giving the number of the message. The process
as done at Her Majesty’s is like a man ringing his tele-
phone -bell and immediately starting to talk without
knowing if the party he wants to speak to is at the tele-
phone or down the street watching a dog-fight. Also,
the operators at Her Majesty’s read by sound, and in the
days of Jeff Davis they read by tape alone. Sound-reading
has not been in vogue more than twenty years or so.
The actual telegraphing seemed to lie a mere jumble of
dots and dashes—only heard one real signal given, that
of the repeat when tlie spy, Thorne, broke off in the
middle of 1 1 is message. It was genuine.

Dear Bulletin. —Be J. C. L. Fitzpatrick,
M.P’s much-reprinted joke about tlie N.S.W.
“Liquidarity” party. Jt is a first-rate joke. I
always thought so. I thought so when I first sent
it in to you in ’95, and I was confirmed in my
opinion when you printed not only that, but
several variants of it of mine, subsequently in ’9B
and dune, ’99. I am even more confirmed than
ever in that belief now that, it has come back to
its original home, your columns, after many days,
m the shape of a quotation from Hansard. It
supports my theory that if a smart par or two
could be successfully lifted from The Bulletin
into a Parliamentary speech, it would make a
Parliamentary reputation. Please turn the lime-
light, off J. C. L. Fitzpatrick and put the same on
me.— Locust.
Premier Reid’s final press interview was
reserved for the S.M. Herald reporter. He con-
sidered himself at that point, lie said, absolved
from dealing impartially with the various sections
of tlie press. Up till then G.11.R. had distributed
Cabinet and Ministerial morsels without regard
to editorial friend or foe. Which recalls that
during Dibbs’Premiership, the turbulent-tempered
one fell out with Sydney D.T., and was accessible
only to the Herald reporter. One day the I).T.
inm slipped into the Chief Secretary’s presence
unknown to the usually-vigilant usher, and found
Dibbs, short-sighted at best, enveloped in clouds
of tobacco smoke. “Take this down as fast as
you like before the other fellows (Ministers, pre-
sumably) come in ” —and with averted head
Darnellicago ” fired off a column or two of im-
portant copy. Then—“ Now, I think you’ve pot
enough. Don’t bother me for a week.” The
reporter escaped hurriedly unrecognised. Just as
the Cabinet gathered, the Herald man was
announced. “ Tell him lie's got all lie’ll get
to-day. r i here’s nothing for him. Thunder ! how
much more docs he want?” Next morning—ye
gods! ° J
Ex-Premier Hugh Nelson, of Queensland,
in a recent interview :
“ The House has a right to take whatever course it
deems best, ami to judge for itself whether Die referen-
dum indicates the real consensus of opinion of tlie people
of Queensland,”
M hich is presumably Hugh’s way of dropping a
hint of possible shady doings to come. Yet, when
the House asked the people to express their
opinion on the Federation Bill, and they expressed
it by a very solid majority, the House has no right
to start judging whether the opinion which the
people have expressed is the people’s opinion or
not. Hugh Nelson might as well say that the
House lias a right to judge for itself whether
Hugh Nelson is Hugh Nelson or not—with the
natural inference that it has the right to decide,
if it likes, that Hugh Nelson is not Hugh Nelson.
The Great Open-Window Question.
A medical man writes to The Bulletin :
The protest of semi-stifled railway travellers is at last
beginning to make itself heard above the rattle of depart-
mental wheels in many land&and continental legislation
is moving to a sanitary end in this respect. The ventila-
tion of railway carriages will make a point for considera-
tion by a Federal Parliament. Linder present conditions,
the average carriage, especially on country lines, is a
breeding-ground for many varieties of serious disease, and,
apart from all question of convenience, the menace to the
public health is a serious one. A normal adult lakes, on
an average, 17 breaths per minute, using 30'5 cubic
inches air on each occasion. By “using” is meant
that he withdraws from it a portion of its oxygen, and
adds carbonic acid, water, and certain foul organic sub-
stances. Such air may he re-breathed, hut acts injuri-
ously if not replaced after a short period. For health
the average adult requires 3000 cubic feet of air per hour
— i.c., the complete changing of the air of a receptacle of
1000 cubic feet three times per hour. This change will
not produce an appreciable draught, hut any number of
changes over five per hour under these conditions will do
so. Under English law, a minimum of -240 cubic feet of
space is allowed per adult head in common lodging-
houses, and 300 in workhouses —an altogether inade
quote amount. The railway authorities go a little further.
Taking a flagrant example, many country lines in
N.S. W. and Victoria are supplied with a class of fully
compartmented carriage, each division of which will
seat 8 persons when full. The first-class divisions each
contain about 315 cubic feet of space, the second class
230, giving an average of 39 and 35 cubic feet per head
respectively in a full carriage. For ventilation, six win-
dows may he opened—two in some varieties of carriage-
yielding in the former case 18 sq. feet of draught area.
With the door open a surface of about 20ft. is given.
Now, four adults will theoretically breathe over the en-
tire stock of air in such a carriage with the windows
closed, in about 35 min., but air is not such an easily-
managed medium as most persons suppose. Asa matter
of fact they will re-breathe one portion of the air several
times and will vitiate the remainder with the products of
respiration. On country lines, the intervals between
stations are often lengthy, and in half-an-hour a pois li-
ons atmosphere can he produced which is eminently
calculated for the convenience of the bacterial legacies of
former voyagers. To obtain the normal 3000 cubic feet
per hour for each individual it would be necessarj to
renew the air nearly 80 times per hour-and a renewal of
even ten times per hour in a space of 300 nq. feel produces
a violent draught! The window-area would effect this,
hut the resulting gale with a train travelling at an average
rate of speed would do nearly as much damage as the
slow suffocation.
An extreme instance has perhaps been selected, but it
affords a good object-lesson.- The hogie-car seating 00
people gives some 06 cubic feet per head, and a ven-
tilating area of about 101 sq. feet. On suburban and main
lines the compartment-carriages are somewhat larger and
possess overbead-ventilaLors, but even they are altogether
inadequately fitted for proper ventilation. The winter
traveller has in all of them a choice of two evils. By
closing floors and windows he speedily creates a poisonous
atmosphere, loaded with injurious matter. By opening
a window he admits a strong draught to flow over his
chest and face while the rest of his body remains in
the stratum of moist, organically-laden air, with occa-
sional interludes of freezing gusts when the floor is
opened. By allowing for a carriage full of excursionists,
on a winter night, the picture can be colored to a proper
hue of horror.
A German scientist recently experimented on guinea-
pigs by injecting a solution of the dust from railway car-
riages. In every case the animal died with symptoms of
tubercle or acute septicaemia. The warm, moisture-laden
atmosphere, filled with the foul organic products of
respiration, with loose skin-cells, and the other bye-pro-
ducts of human combustion, makes an admirable culture-
ground for all forms of bacteria. Tubercle spores flourish
in it, the coccus of pneumonia needs no better environ-
ment, and the sporules of many skin-diseases luxuriate.
Dried pus-cells (capable of producing tonsilitis), fungi
There is no need to continue the list.
Several inventions hare been brought forward to re-
move the evil, hut the authorities are. indolent. With
the forward rush of the train and the engine furnace
always at hand, a system of air-currents, warmed in
winter and cooler! in summer, should he easily avail-
able, and although the full amount required might be
difficult to supply with comfort, present conditions
would be much alleviated. Of the dust - collecting
qualities of the average carriage, and the absolute lack of
cleanliness in those who are sarcastically termed “car-
riage-cleaners,” it is unnecessary to speak. At present
the long-distance traveller will do best by sitting with
his hack to the engine, opening two opposite windows,
or a window and a ventilator, to produce a through
draught, and by making an outcry on every possible
occasion. For the suburban victim, a bicycle or the tram
will give a better chance of seeing Federation accom-
plished.
Within the last 10 years the North German
Lloyd S.S. Co. has built 51 steamers, and has now
building 14, all twin-screw boats of heavy ton-
nage ; one being a 14,000-ton vessel, intended to
beat, with a speed, the Kaiser Wilhelm
der Grosse’s ocean-average record of 22i. The
capital of the co.—lo years ago, .£1,500,000 —is
now £5,600,000.
Big John Forrest’s Redistribution of Seats
Bill promises considerable changes in W.A. The
North, which used to have seven members for its
huge wastes and its struggling droves of cattle, is
reduced to four. Perth and its surroundings are
increased from four members to seven. Kalgoorlie
and the surrounding goldfields are to have ten
members instead of six, and there are other re-
arrangements. There is considerable smash
among the two-men-and-a-dog constituencies
which used to return the sound old ’Gropers to
Parliament —though Forrest has alleviated the
smash as much as possible. Also the electoral
qualification has been made a trifle easier. Alto-
gether the Six Families are likely to be much
smaller tin gods in the first Parliament elected
under the new law than in any ’Groper Legislature
up to date. But when that Parliament will be
elected is something of a mystery. There are no
signs of red-hot haste about John.
A Brisbane correspondent:—
Re Barton's mobbing in Brisbane, the other day, none
of the newspapers refer to the spirit Toby displayed in
the row. I was close to him, and noticed that he could
use his fists to some purpose. No funk, though much
ire.
An ex-Brisbaneite writes mournfully
liome from Paraguay re his troubles in Lane’s
Paradise. It seems the Brisbaneite got on splen-
didly until he was seized with the mad ambition
of raising a fat pig—inasmuch as he and his family
were suffering severely from want of meat. No
objection was raised (so deponent avers) so long as
the pig was small and lean, but as soon as the
porker waxed fat and substantial on private care
and nourishment, the Communistic Dictator
stepped in and claimed the swine as communal
property. Result—a quarrel and expulsion from
Cosine of two genial ex-Queenslanders. The
trouble with Lane is his ideals. He’d sacrifice
his mother to his ideal, but he hasn't any respect
for other folk’s ideals. “Another ground of
variance arose over the thrift question. One or two
of the settlers, in place of consuming all the g- ods
to which they were entitled, preferred to allow a
certain pro|x>rtion of their earnings to accumulate
in credit-dollars at the store. After a while, an
outcry was raised that these individuals were
becoming capitalists by virtue of their credit, and
a demand was made that their hoards should be
given up for the common welfare. Refusal and
ructions ensued. . . . An abundance of coarse
food was obtainable, but clothing was sadly
deficient. Difficulties and embarrassment beset
the pilgrims on every side, for Lane’s trip to the
old country in search of proselytes turned out
disastrously. Of the 45 individuals he imported
not one remains at the settlement, and it appears
the stock was pledged to provide funds for the
passage-money. . . . In conclusion, the Brisbaneite
wants to come back to Queensland, and the Govt,
is going to help him.” ... A sad and ludicrous
business !

“Constance Clyde”: “Invercargill is
about to start a Federation League.” Writer for
her sins was once buried three days in that dread-
ful out-of-the-world M.L. city, whose port, the
Bluff, possesses, according to Christie Murray,
the most southern lamp-post in the world.
Wherein Christie Murray was wrong ; at least I
am credibly informed that dreary Punto Arenas,
in Magellan’s Straits, has lampposts. Inver-
cargill is a large, unpopulated city, with streets so
flat and so empty that a woman can see her hus-
band going into a pub. three miles off, but like-
wise so broad that he can think out 1000 ex-
planations for his disappearance into that pub.
while crossing from one kerb-stone to another. If
even such a dead and alive city of the plains can
muster up enough enthusiasm for a Federation
League, then the cause in Maoriland is already
won.

Prell’s Building's, 60-70 Queen Street, Melbourne, May 31, 1899.
Dear Mr. Palmer, —I wrote you May sth a testimonial (entirely unsolicited) relative to the beneficial results your famed medicine
Yitadatio had accomplished in regard to a complaint to which I have been a martyr for years, viz. : Sciatic Rheumatism. I wrote the
letter in a guarded manner, being somewhat of a pessimist and knowing the incredulous
doubts that would arise. I find that lam or have—so to speak—“ awoke to find myself
famous.” “Is it genuine, Norton ? ” “ Could not cure you; impossible.” “An advertis-
ing dodge,” &c., &e., are the remarks I hear daily. Well, to those who know the agony I
have battled with—the suffering, the insomnia resulting from the same—my present re-
lief and entire freedom from pain must (from the fact that I have not taken any other
remedy) point to Vitadatio only. To preclude the slightest lingering doubts in the
minds of those who “ wish to scoff but prefer to pray,” I enclose you herewith a Statutory
Declaration, and may say “ An honest tale speeds best being plainly told.”
I am, very faithfully yours,

Prell’s Buildings, 60-70 Queen Street, Melbourne. May sth, 1899. Mr. S. A. Palmer, Esq., 45, 47 Bourke Street. Dear Sir. —I have
been hesitating for the last six months relative to the actual merits of your famous medicine, Vitadatio, and although I am anxious at all
times to recommend any sterling recipe for complaints similar to that from which I have suffered for the past ten years—namely, Sciatic
Rheumatism—l, knowing that my opinion and recommendation would carry weight amongst a wide circle of acquaintances, felt I must
actually satisfy myself beyond the slightest doubt before expressing an opinion good, bad, or indifferent. I have during my long career
travelled pretty well all over the world, tried many prescriptions, and acted upon medical advice from gentlemen of repute and standing.
I must say now conscientiously that although I have not been at all consistent regarding diet, etc., during the time I have taken Vitadatio,
still I have derived greater benefit from the same than from any other medicine. Each attack of Sciatic Rheumatism gradually lessens in its
intensity, and without doubt is rapidly being eradicated from my system. I think it iny duty to give you this testimonial and fervently hope
that those who have suffered like myself will be equally successful in obtaining relief. With good wishes for the future.
Obediently yours, FRED B. NORTON.

Westralia t’other day nearly passed a Dog
Act providing that when any man found a strange
dogkilling hissheep he could shootthe animal, after
advertising his fatal intention in three consecutive
issues of the local paper (published, perhaps, 100
milesaway) and once in the Government Gazette
at Perth. The Bill had got through the Assembly
and was nearly through the Council, when a
thoughtful member remarked that all this process
might easily take six weeks, and the dog might be
gone before then.
The way of the cold-tea martyr is indeed
hard. One was addressing a big crowd in an
Auckland (M.L.) street and pouring wrath and
contempt on the beastly publicans, when the man
in blue requested him to move on. Burning for
martyrdom he refused, and was run in and fined
Is. and costs. He declined to pay and announced
his intention of going to gaol, and being a great,
widely-advertised saint suffering for the “caws,”
but just as he was starting for there the Brewers’
Association paid the fine and exes, and spoiled
the martyrdom. Next time the temperance man
attempted to address the crowd he was greeted
with requests to speak kindly of the trade that
had kept him out of gaol. The beer-maker is a
low animal who deprives the poor saint of his God-
given right to pose as a martyr.
