Cabled that the Bryanites in New York
have been cheering Aguinaldo, the leader of the
Fil ipi nos, as “a true hero.” It needs a lively
imagination to find anything heroic about a man
who took a big bribe from the Spaniards to
abandon the revolutionary cause, then allied him-
self with the ’Murkans, then abandoned them,
and set up for himself.
Arthur Adams’s book, “ Maoriland and
Other Verses,” to be published by The Bulletin
on Sept. 28, will definitely place him among the
first flight of local poets. The book contains, in
addition to those published in The Bulletin,
many charming pieces not previously printed ;
and is issued in dainty style. Brice 55., post-free
from Bulletin office.
W. A. Governor Jerry Smith was
“ courted ” the other day for the value of refresh-
ments supplied for an At Home of Lady Smith’s
during the Coolgardie Exhibition opening. Plain-
tiff put the figure at £4O, Sir “Jerry ” at only a
modest fiver, and the. “ Binch ” came in third with
«£lt> 10s. and costs.
Members of Vic. Winegrowers’ Conference
in Melb. at show-time told of wandering anxiously
from pub. to pub. in quest of a glass of good
native wine, and being served with vinegar, raw
spirits and saccharine, and other abominations, at
sixpence a dose. “All we can say to the credit
of the -publicans is that -the doses were—small,”
said one member. The Govt, is asked to “ take
prompt steps,” but politicians only do that on
pay-day, or when asked to step out and encourage
the whisky industry.
The Bulletin receives from N.S.W. Govt,
another large, officially printed paper which sets
forth that the weary Ur. Ross, M.L.A., has been
isking this question in the Assembly
Dr. Ross asked the Minister of Public Instruction-
(1) Will he ascertain from the Govt. Astronomer if
he can account on any scientific grounds for the reason
of the present remarkable anil unprecedented wet, un-
settled weather aiid rainfall from which Sydney and
coastal districts have been suffering during the last few
months; and why the drought in some parts of the in-
ter.or is still a source of trouble to settlers and pas-
toralists ?
(2.) Can he furnish any reasons, meteorological or
otherwise, for the existence of such precarious arid phe-
nomenal weather ?
“Wet, unsettled weather and rainfall,” to say
nothing of the humid moist liquid damp fluid that
often accompanies them, seem to get on to
old Ur. Ross's corns. And the price of the docu-
ment which sets forth the above question is gravely
quoted as being 3d.
B. II.: Wise is the creature _of electoral
caprice, and never quite knows when.he will be
left out by the voters. South Sydney returned
him in 'B7, rejected him in ’B9, and returned him
in '9l ; and Flinders returned him in ’94 and
rejected him in ’95. In ’B9 West Macquarie would
not have him. In the Federal delegates’ election
he was tenth—the last—man on the poll, and for
several hours it was a toss-up whether John See
or he would lie included in the elected. The look
on the faee.s of \\ ise and See as they watched the
figures at the U. I’, office at 11 p.in. was a study
to be remembered. At the July, ’9B, election the
result of Ash field s vote was delayed till midnight,
when Wise won by five votes only. B. R.W., it
was rumored, was to be opposed at Ash field by
F. E. Winehcombe, a popular business man of
no political experience, but the latter says lie is
content to wait for the dissolution, which he
prophesies will come three or four months
hence. As the new N.S.W. Attorney-General
showed splendidly in the Federal fight, he, if on
that ground alone, deserves to be re-elected—quite
apart from the fact that no Minister should be
opposed on taking office, save under very excep-
tional circumstances.
A certain police-sergeant at Prahran (Vic-
toria) is apparently a prompt Napoleonic bobby
who deserves well of his country. In his district
was a friendless sick woman for whom it was
desired to find hospital accommodation. A police-
man was sent to inquire if there was room for
her, and the hospital authorities cordially replied
that there wasn t. Then, in the ordinary course
of things, the sergeant should have reported the
matter, and the town clerk and the Mayor and
the health authorities should have communicated
with each other about it till tile patient died. But
the Brahran bobby simply took the sufferer
in a cab to the hospital, carried her in
there,, planted her down in the most comfortable
attitude be could manage, and walked off. Then
the hospital, as it couldn’t decently drop her on
the pavement, put her to bed in high dudgeon.
The board afterwards demanded a written explana-
tion regarding the outrage, and the sergeant gave
it at great length. He explained what he thought
the woman was suffering from, how she was
carried to the cab, how she was propped up in
the cab, the number of the cab, the general appear-
ance of the horse, the instructions given to the
driver about going slow and not bumping, the
route taken to the hospital—in fact, being re-
quested to explain his conduct, he took the request
literally, and explained it. And now the hospital
board is angrier at the explanation than it was at
the original outrage.
At Townsville (Q.) recently John Rees
committed the dark crime of falling asleep on the
beach. While he was sleeping the brilliant, plain-
clothes policeman bore down on him. Some pro-
perty had been stolen, and certain footmarks, pre-
sumably those of the thief, had been found round
the place where the property had been before it
went away, and the policeman was looking for
somebody whose boots fitted the marks. He tried
the sleeping man at random, and his boots fitted
—at least they fitted near enough. Considering
that about half the adult male population wears
No. 7 ready-irufde boots, and that footmarks are
generally rather indefinite, it is probable that the
boots of 500 other men in Townsville would also
have fitted these marks. Consequently John
Rees was run in. Nothing was known against
him, and none of the lost property was
found on him, but his boots and the darkly-sus-
picious circumstance that he had fallen asleep on
the beach were against him, so he was remanded
oft and on for two weeks. Every time he came up
ho told the bench that he had been mining at
Alligator Creek on the dateof the alleged robbery,
and offered, if allowed out in custody of that
intelligent and painstaking officer, Constable
O’Booligan, to find men in Townsville who could
prove bis alibi, but the bench only shook its
large wooden head and remanded Kees some more.
It wouldn’t even make any serious inquiry itself
into his alibi ; it was too busy trying to fit him
violently into the crime, and (indirectly) to put
down tin* inmky offence of sleeping on the beach.
And at the end of two weeks it found, to its great
surprise, that Rees had really been miles away
when the theft took place—by which time the
offender bad got a long start and the footmarks
hid perished through effluxion of time. Then it
had bitter thoughts about giving Rees three
months for sleeping on the beach, but as certain
lifficulties were in the way that brutal and hard-
ened criminal was let off.
Alleged that there prevails in Romo an
impression that the Pope intends to establish to
represent him in Australia a high Italian digni-
tary, such as is already so placed in America.
Something about the early Reid. The re-
cent N.S. VV. Premier first tumbled into the arena of
public life with a pungent pamphlet attacking
Henry Parkes over the Panama route mail-con-
tract. This drew the attention of Sydney’s leading
mercantile men towards Parkes’ ruddy adversary,
and Thomas Sutcliffe Mort was certain that a
young man of great political potentialities had
appeared on the scene. Parkes never forgave and
never forgot the pamphleteer, although he twice
offered him a portfolio. In iBG9, when he was
thought to be the real leader of the rising young
men of Sydney, Reid and VV. C. Windeyer, then
in Parliament, challenged attention; together
with G. R. Dibbs they founded the Australian
Patriotic Association, which celebrated its first
big appearance in public by a picnic at Clontarf.
On that occasion, Windeyer responded to “ The
Land we Live in.” Reid responded for “The
Association,” and echoed Windeyer’s patriotic,
not to say Jingoistic, utterances. Dibbs proposed
“ The Press,” and complimented N.S. Wales and
Sydney on possessing two such rising men as
Windeyer and Reid. And how the latter loved
each other a quarter of a century afterwards !
Also, how fondly Reid and Dibbs still cling to
each other 1
“ Hell hath no fury like a mummer
scorned ! ” List to the tale of woe of one James
Carter-Edwards, a hyphenated retainer of that
turbulent tragedian, Wilson Barrett. Barrett,
Carter-Edwards, Lewis Scott, Nugent Robertson,
and others, had a great controversy, and said a
lot of nasty things and jeered at each other a
while ago in Sydney D.T., and The Bulletin
stepped in and jeered at them all impartially.
VV hereupon Carter-hyphen-Edwards now sends us
from England the following terrific outburst. The
fun of it is that The Bulletin rather overlooked
Carter-hyphen-Edwards in the controversy—in
fact, considered him merely as one likely to get
trodden on in the crush, and said next to nothing
about him. Ho was only referred to in one line as
“Carter, mind the hyphen-Edwards, too.’’ And
hasn t it just roused him ! Hear him bellow !
Limelight, please, and slow music for Mr. Carter-
hyphen-Edwards :
To think of it! We might have called him some-
thing worth talking about, and here we have gone
and passed over this foeman worthy of our steel
with a pitiful jeer about his hyphen. Wait till
the gentleman comes back ! We will have a few
real insults saved up for him.
Vic. miners will be able to make out a strong
case in support of their plea to be put on the
same footing as seamen with respect to old-
age pensions—on the same footing, or some-
thing like it. Mining is subject to extraordinary
fluctuations, and so miners have to shift their
ground often—from dull Vic. fields to NVestralia,
Broken Hill, the Tas. West Coast, or elsewhere.
And then scores of men, played-out by working
in thedeepor badly-ventilatedgroundof Bendigo
or Ball arat, go for a spell to other fields, just as
men passed from Broken Hill to Vic. to have a
spell out of the lead, and then went back again.
Also, many N.S. W. mines are more Vic.-owned
than N.S. W.-owned. Not one Broken Hill
mine has its business-centre in N.S. \V., and
not a first or second-rate Westralian mine has
its head office in the province. This is a con-
dition which distinguishes mining from every
other industry. But though special provisions
like those suggested by the miners will remove
some hardships, nothing short of an Australian
pension scheme will be quite satisfactory.
Workmen have had to be Australian for many
years past; there are scores in every trade who
would have starved if they had baulked at
boundary lines.
Item from the speech at opening of Queens-
land Parliament:—
O n ? °f the results directly following upon the adoption
in this colony of the Commonwealth Bill will necessarily
be an alteration of our electoral law. By the Act refer-
ring the question to the electors it was prescribed that
no elector should vote more than once at the poll, not-
withstanding that his name appeared on more than one
electoral roll, and in the draft constitution itself it is
pro\ ided that, in the choosing of senators and of members
of lhe House of Representatives, each elector shall vote
only once. My advisers consider that it would be anoma-
lous that, in pronouncing upon matters of local concern,
the existing system of plural voting should obtain, while
on wider questions of national import electors should be
restricted to a single vote, and they propose accordingly
to submit to you a Bill assimilating in this respect the
franchise in Queensland to that of the Commonwealth.
Advantage will he taken of the opportunity to remedy
certain inconsistencies in our Elections Act, and to alford
increased facilities for every qualified person to register
his name as an elector.
Yet the anti-Federal Ananias party in Queensland
swore to the bitter end that there was no One-
man-one-vote in the Commonwealth Bill. Federa-
tion has already led to Victoria getting One-man-
one-vote at Assembly elections, and it is almost
certain that it will confer the same blessing on
Queensland.
AN ADDRESS.
To be spoken by Wilson Barrett, in sincere admiration
of the Fot-oaliaiit, and behind-a-hedge lies and slanders
published by the Banders of the Sewer Press.
Since last we met “ full thirt3 r times hath ” —no. that’s
musty.
But, yet, earth's axle, here, has creaked a little rusty;
Or did my ears mislead? And does this croak, or
creak i n g,
Come front the souls of those whose “moral crease” is
leaking?
Or from those God-souled scribes who put—with valor
shoddy—
A bullet in your reputation—not your body?
Who—like the vulture, when ill hunger’s flight he’s
preyintr—
Leaves all that’s sweet, and swoops where foulness lies
degating !
hat shall I say? llow tilt against these muck-daubed
lancers ?
There are no lower depths to match them with our
answers !
I can t do better, p’raps, than herein make confession
Of all our sins which goad these scribes to wroth ex-
pression.
First, then, your praise we won, my gentle dames and
masters ;
You gave us full success, they prophesied disasters !
False prophets ever grizzle, when they fail at cursing!
They cannot lead by Light, but Terror and coercing.
You see, we’re English born ! to them that’s wormwood
bitter !
They re like to none —sole microbes of a pest-born litter !
Their jeers deny us brain and Art, but prate of ravings !
Yet mark their envious groanings, born of brain of
shavings !
Be judge, good friends, what crimes they say we have
committed !
They pounce upon a “ hyphen,” as if ’twere unfitted
lor eyes polite; and, 10, they’d send poor Carter-
Ed wards
Forgive the dash .'—to chains, or into sulphur bed-wards !
And then, oh, Scott! I fear, alas ! their stings have
killed him !
Or rendered prone his brain ! or with deep shame have
filled him.
Blest he, if with these “ village mongrels” he had herded,
And, with their gall-dipped pen, more spleenful lies had
worded.
Oods! how these Austral scourges snarl their calling
gorge full! e
Of poison pin-pricks Littleness they have a force full
But these they had withheld, and’ fain they would have
bless'd us,
If whelped like them—we in their currish mind had
dress'd us.
Or if—with brain deseased, imbibing “Bass” or “ Wat-
ney ”
We, with this canine pack, had been dumped down at
Bot'ny !
Or “like a sucking dove” they would have roared their
spite out,
If we had bowed our flesh for them to tear and bite out.
Cur A.N.R.—whose tail they decked with string and
kettle—
These kindred mongrels sent a-yelping, tuned with metal
So tinny, cracked, and yet so viciously discordant,
They jointly licked their snarling jaws in sweet reward
on't.
We dared-oh, gods ! yes, DARED-to guard us from the
smiter;
And scoffed the bloated heads of these square-iialo'd
writers !
Then, cruel brutes, their onslaught big on little Hodces,
Wiped him from earth, save just some blots of microbe
splodges !
Indeed, what Reputation have these scribes not stricken ?
What honest work not cursed? While noxious growths
they quicken.
They say I have decried your Art! abused your country !
Much worse than lying, this —’tis cowardly affront’ry !
To pull down Art they climb—" Excelsior ” and “Allez!”
Their praise no higher mounts than the fat legs of the
ballet !
Speak WORTH !—from Australasia’s furthest capes, to
Bight of it—
Have you been fostered by their aid, or in despite of it ?
“ But, stay ! ” they cry, “ your rhymes are foul retalia-
tion !
Our slanders are but Seraph’s sonys, or sweet damnation.
We steal a cow, a herd !—no crime the world alleges—
But you ! llow dare you even peep across the hedges?"
But this apart, good friends, and well I know you’re
many,
Remember this, we’re absent! Strangers I and should any
Of these envenomed pens repeat their foul mud-slinging,
Or stab us in the dark, and send more lies broad flinging,
Think only that we love .you, with good faith, and truly !
Believe we bl ess and honor yourgreat country, throughly!
That not one word, one thought, of mine, or of the
others,
But w hat recalls you as our friends, our sisters, brothers !
They'll proffer still their feast of gall ; kill names with
slanders !
They’ll still ban all of Good, and bless all filthy Panders !
But when they’ve done their WORST—a task which their
soul gladdens—
This Halm's for us— and this their jealous passion mad-
dens !
We woo your hone of Right, and not in vain we’ll woo it ;
And though they spit their spleen, we’ll thrive and still
live through it !
JAMES CARTER-EDWARDS.

Something is After You.
Far away, in the Jungle of Central India, a
village lies asleep. Only a solitary human figure
can be seen, following the winding jungle path in
the moonlight. It is early night, and the native
who comes has been delayed on his way home.
But what is that dark shadow that crosses the
path behind him? Quite oblivious, the man
inarches on. He looks neither to right rtor left
nor behind him, where crouches that dark shadow.
A few yards more and he will be out of the jungle.
A few minutes more, and he thinks he will be
sleeping in the bosom of his family . But see-, the
Shadow moves ! With two noiseless bounds like
a giant cat the tigress is upon him. One. despair-
ing scream and all is quiet. Bitten through the
head, the victim is snatched up and carried by the
grim man-eater to her lair in the hills. Meanwhile
the village slumbers peacefully on.
Was it the man’s fault? Yes, I think it was.
Had he not lingered, he would not have been
caught. The tigress would not have attacked him
in broad daylight. But he was no worse I think
than those in this country who are to-day doing
the very same thing. Thousands and thousands
of English men and women haveashadow in their
path. It is after them. The time will come, if
they do not take heed, when they will feel, like
Mrs Lydia Golding, “as if something had over-
taken” them. Here is a letter from her in which
she tells her experience :
I “All my life 1 had been a strong healthy woman,
and up to the autumn of 1891 1 never ailed any-
thing. At this time I began to feel weak, weary,
and tired, and as if someth in;/ had overtaken me. I
had a foul taste in the mouth, my tongue being
furred, and a sour fluid would rise into my mouth.
I had no appetite to speak of, and the little food I
took gave me no strength. After eating I had a
heavy weight and pain across my chest, and a
gnawing feeling in my stomach. I belched up a
deal of frothy fluid, and in the night I awoke with
a suffocating feeling. Cold clammy sweats broke
over me, and what with loss of appetite, and not
being able to sleep at night I soon got so weak
that / found it hard work to net about.
“On December, 23, 1892(0ne Sunday morning),
whilst preparing breakfast for my husband, I was
suddenly seized with paralysis, which affected the
whole of my right side. I had no use of my hand
or leg on that side, and a numbness took me on
both left and rioht sides. My husband got me to
bed, and fetched a doctor from NorthHeet, who gave
me medicines. After this I lost my strength
rapidly, and what I suffered I cannot tell.
“I could not rest night or day and I was in and
out of bed every now and again. I got little sleep,
only dozing off for a short time and then starting
up. I was afraid to be left alone, and often in the
night I have been so nervous and frightened I
could scarcely bear it. I took medicines of all
kinds, but was little better for anything until one
day in August, 1893, my husband read in the paper
“Weekly People, ” of Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup, and got me a bottle from Perry and Son,
Chemists, High Street, Gravesend. After taking
it a short time, I began to gain strength, and got
stronger, and stronger until I was free from the
effects of the seizure. I now keep in good health.
You can publish this statement if you wish. ”
Yours truly, (Signed) Mrs. Lydia Golding, 12,
Carter’s Road, Perry Street, Gravesend, May 14th.
1890.
What was this shadow that had crossed Mrs.
Golding’s path? What was it that “overtook”
her and laid her helpless and suffering upon a bed
of sickness? Surely you can recognise the mon-
ster that sprang upon her as the dreaded disorder
Dyspepsia, which attacks you when you are most
defenceless and weakest. Paralysis; yes, Mrs.
Golding had it, and could feel it, but' what she
could -not feel was what it was that had so acted
upon her nerves and muscles as to cause them to
become so helpless. Nor could she, I suppose,
understand hoiv Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup
cured her so quickly, though we know now that it
was because the dyspepsia was got rid of.
But, as the native was never heard of more so
there are some who cannot get out of the clutches
of Dyspepsia, even with such help as Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup, if the help comes too
late. Hence the best way of all, say I, is to keep
out of the jungle after dark ;and if you must go
walk quickly.

The Case of
MR. THOMAS MATTHEWS.
(by a special.)
(For the “ Bur wood Courier.”)
“Is Mr. Matthews at home?” enquired
the newspaper man, as the door opened to
Ins knock.
“ Who wants me ? ” replied a cheery voice,
as the gentleman himself stepped forward,
and politely enquired our errand.
“ Mr. Matthews, it may here be said, is a
well known resident of Enfield, on the
Burwood Road, and the owner of the finest
herd of milch cows in the district.
“I havo heard, Mr. Matthews,” said the
reporter, “from one of your relations, that
you are a great enthusiast in the matter of
Clements Tonic, and are continually sounding
its praises ; will you kindly tell me how you
came to form that opinion ?”
“Practical experience” answered Mr.
Mattiiews ; “proof in my own person, verified
time after time during these last few years.
It accomplished what nothing else even
attempted to do in my own case, so I simply
speak as I find.”
“Will you kindly give mo the details of
your sickness, Mr. Matthews?”
‘ It is seven or eight years ago that I first
noticed that something was radically wrong
with me. I did not understand what it was,
nor can I assign any reason for the bad state
of health into which I gradually drifted.”
“ What were your symptoms ? ”
“ I was taken with sudden fits of giddiness,
my brain seemed to whirl round and round,
everything I saw' with my eyes appeared to
swim and turn about. It was as though I
had been running round and round in a ring,
as children will at play till they become so
topsy-turvey that they cannot stand up.
This was how I felt, I could not stand up.
I had to clutch hold of the first thing handy
to prevent my falling, and then go and lie
down a while till the attack passed off. ”
“ Were these attacks very frequent, Mr.
Matthews ? ”
“ Yes : in the morning, particularly when
I stooped to lace my boots while dressing,
then my brain would co nmence to swim and
sway, and I would have to lie down to save
myself from falling down.”
“ Did you have any other disorder? ”
“ I began to find that certain foods did not
agree with me. I had to restrict myself in
drinking tea, for instance. But with all these
precautions, I found the action of thedigestive
organs was still deranged. After meals I had
a severe pain in the pit of the stomach and an
accumulation of wind there; this wind used to
belch up, then my pains would ease. Often and
often I would vomit up my food, and sometimes
in the middle of a meal the giddy sickness
would come over me and I was compelled to
get up and leave the table.”
“I was always more liable to attack on
Sundays, and had to be very careful in my
diet. No doubt this arose from my resting on
that day from active exercise, which, as we
all know, assists digestion.”
“ Did you have other troubles?”
“ When my giddiness came on there were
pains in my forehead and temples and I had
very frequently a sharp pain between the
shoulder blades. These were all the actual
pains I suffered ; but far worse was the
mental de, ression and sickness of spirits that
oppressed me at that time.”
“ Tell me about that, Mr. Matthews?”
“ I got into low spirits, dismal thought®
haunted me, I was always foreboding ill
things or impossible calamities. I fancied
something dreadful was going to happen
to the* Business, my family, or myself,
that never happened or was likely to happen.
My giddy fits made me so uneasy, not
knowing their cause, that I was sure I wa®
going out of my mind, and that they ivere
the premonitory symptoms. This further
tended to accentuate my gloom of mind.
All the world appeared overcast to me. I
did not care for anything or take interest
in what was going on around me. 1 was
in that hopeless mental state that I did not
care if 1 lived or died. This was in my
waking moments ; but when 1 tried to sleep
I got no relief; my slumber was troubled by
nightmare, and horrible dreams. At one time
I would imagine I was falling over a
precipiee into unseen depths below ; or that
bolted and in deadly peril of an imminent
I was on the back of a horse that had
smash into some obstacle, and so on, my
dreams were endless variations of terrible
dangers. And, finally, when my fright in
sleep got to the last point it would wake me
up in a trembling sweat, feverish and
unrested. ”
“ Did you consult a medical man, Mr.
Matthews ? ”
“ Yes, I went to see a doctor and told
him my symptoms, and he said I suffered
from indigestion and he ordered me some
medicine, which I took without benefit; but
I may say from what I had heard 1 did not
persevere with his treatment.”
“ What was that you heard, Mr. Mat-
thews.”
“Friends told me that I should find a
certain cure for my sufferings in Clements
Tonic —that it was a wonderful remedy.”
“ And you followed their advice ?”
“ Yes, I purchased a bottle at once and it
had such a marvellous curative effect that I
could not have believed possible had I not
actually experienced the benefit. Like
magic, all my distressing pains and mental
gloom disappeared. My digestion was re-
stored to its natural condition, I no longer
had the pain in the pit of the stomach after
eating, the belching wind, or the pain
between the shoulder blades. My fits of
giddiness ceased and the weight of
imaginary trouble was lifted from my mind.
I slept without evil dreams and my spirits
regained their natural buoyancy and
cheerfulness.”
“ And you give all the credit of this
transformation to Clements Tonic solely ? ”
“Oh, I have no doubt of it, fori have
proved it limes and times again. 1 always
keep a bottle of Clements Tonic in the
house, and at the least sign of a return of
my old complaint, 1 take a dose or two and
it never fails to cure my tioubles right off.”
Have youany objections, Mr. Matthews, to
this interview being published in the press ?”
asked the reporter.
“ Not in the least. I shall be only too
pleased if my experience should meet the
eye of some one in my own unfortunate
condition of years gone by; it might be the
means of guiding them to the same reb'p
that I have obtained.”

Her Coup-de-Theatre.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They had been engaged for a year and a half, but
lately she had notioed that his love was beginning
to 0001. He never talked in the old, rapturous
way of “some day,” and their daily meetings had
dwindled down to weekly, without even letters
between.
The last time she saw him she had noticed that
her laughter and smart speeches had jarred on
him, and he had spoken in praise of a woman of
their acquaintance with a soft, cooing voice.
“Purring,” the girl called it.
Bhe expected him to-night, and had made up
her mind for battle, and victory. She clad her-
self in her woman’s armour—a dress he liked the
best, a clinging white cashmere that left her throat
and shoulders bare. Her soft round arms and
neck were her strongest weapons, and she meant
to use them.
She was sitting in a low chair when he came,
and he noticed how the yellow cushion behind her
head suited her eyes, her soft brown eyes.
She rose as he came towards her and held out
her hands with a glad gesture.
“ I knew you would come to-night, for I have
been thinking of you all day.”
He sat down by the fire, bub she did nob go
back to her chair. She stood behind him, her
fingers playing in his hair in a tender, motherly
way. She did not speak for some minutes, but he
could feel her heart against his ear as she leant
over him. It made it very hard for him to say
what he had come to tell her. He wished he had
written, or waited till to-morrow. Why had she
worn that frock to-night? He had not seen it for
months. And why was she so soft and sweet to-
night, when she had been so different lately ? ”
“Dear,” her voice was a caress, “ why did you
stay away so long ? I have been so lonely.” Her
bare arm came soft and warm round his neck, and
her cheek was laid on his hair.
He could never tell her like this. He must do
it at once.
He got up quickly and walked across the room
and back again. She stood watching him with a
hurt, wondering look.
“What is the matter, Jim? Something is
troubling you. Have you anything to tell me ? ”
“Yes, I have. Won’t you sit down? I can’t
talk while you are standing there.’’
She sat down in her chair by the fire, but he
stood against the mantelshelf. It gave him
courage to stand thus looking down on her, and
clumsily, in his man’s way, he told her what, he
had come to say.
It was just what she had expected. They had
both made a mistake. . . . They were not suited
to each other. . . . He had met another girl, a
little, fair-haired thing who was just the sorb of
gentle woman he wanted for a wife.
“You are too clever for me, Joy, you know.
You would soon grow tired of a big, stupid fellow
like me. You want a man who can talk poetry
and metaphysics and stuff like that. And 1 want
a wife who cares for home things more than
books. You must see that we have both been
mistaken, don’t you Joy ? ”
She sat listening, with her hands clasped before
her, her eyes never leaving his face for one second.
When ho finished speaking', she made just one
little ohoking sound, and hid her face against the
ohair.
She did not speak at all, and he stood looking
down at her, wishing she would not sit like that
but would say something. He had expected
angry, cutting things, but instead there was
silence, and that bent head. What a pretty head
it was 1 The firelight shone on the bright brown
hair, and played in the deep ripples above her
ear. Those ripples were never made by tongs or
curling-pins, and neither was that little curl that
lay on her neck, and tried to hide the tiny mole.
He remembered that mole so well. She had
always grumbled at it, but he had kissed it and
called it her beauty-spot. How long ago that
was ! It was soon after they were engaged ; the
night he had given her that emerald ring. He
glanced at her hand hanging limp by her side.
Yes, there it was in the same place on her little
finger—she would not have it on the third. Her
hand would look strange without it, for it was the
only one she wore. It was a dear hand. Too
thin for actual beauty, but soft and well-shaped ;
and, after all, he did not like fat hands. Hers
were firm and cool, and so comforting when one’s
head ached. Ilithel’s hands were such baby
things, soft and pink and kissable, but he
liked a woman to have firm, strong
hands best. Joy’s were strong. He remem-
bered how she had held his dog the day the
poor little brute broke its leg. She had cried so
hard afterwards, too. He wondered if she were
crying now. Her head was still hidden. Would
she never look round ? He couldn’t stand it much
longer. He wanted to comfort her. That little
moje seemed to invite him to kiss it. He—
She lifted her head and looked at him. Her
eyes were wet, but they gleamed with a depth of
love he had never seen there before. All the
sparkle had gone, out of them, but something
sweeter had come in. She rose and came to where
he was standing.
“ Perhaps you are right, dear! lam too hard
and worldly for you.” Hard !—with that look in
her eyes ! “ She will make the soft, yielding wife
you want, and I know you will be happy. We
W u\ Say £°°d-byo, and you must go away for a
while, and then I shall get used to it—some day,”
She was fidgetting with some violets in a bowl
beside her, and he could see she had something
more to say.
Suddenly she lifted her face to him and held
out her hands.
“Jim, Jim ! kiss me just once before you go !
You will be hers soon, but now you are mine still.
I love you ; oh, my dear, I love you ! ”
Her head was thrown back. Her red lips were
parted in eagerness, and her eyes glowed with
passion. She had never looked so beautiful before.
For one second he looked at her, and then ....
one white arm was round his neck, her head was
bowed on his shoulder, and she had conquered.
* • • *
When he said good-bye, two hours later, it was
settled that the wedding should be in a month.
‘I nearly made a big mistake,” he said ; “which
only proves that I was right when I said you
were too sweet and clever for such an ass.”
Her only answer then was a kiss, but when the
door closed behind him she smiled at herself in
the glass.
“Yes, he was right! I am far too clever for
him. It was a fine piece of acting, ma chbre I” —
with a little bow to her image in the glass—“and
it brought down the house ! ”
FAYRE.
The Philosopher.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was sitting on a stump with the usual smile of
contentment making drains in his face to carry
off the perspiration. I remarked that it was hot.
He grinned and said, “ Nice warm weather, ain’t
it?” A fly fell from his forehead frizzled, and
the reflection from a vagrant tin dish struck me as
if from the hand of God. I threw myself under
the speckled shade of a ragged butt-oak and eyed
the Philosopher. His smile took on a twist of
superiority as he watched me scrape the dust and
sweat off my face, and blow vapor at 98 on the
end of my nose to save it from liquefaction.
“ How the blazes do you manage it ? Are you
guaranteed against heat-stroke ? ” I asked.
“Naw, I’m just enjoyin’it—l enjoys every-
thing.”
A red bull-ant crossed my line of vision bound
Philosopher-wards. The set of his body denoted
warfare. Disgusted with the curse of Life-under-
the-circumstances, he had dug up the hatchet.
“ You enjoy everything ? How about frost ? ”
“ Beautiful, sir—beautiful! Pine, bracing air
on the cold winter mornings—glorious when the
sun rises and clears the mists of night away! ”
I agreed.
“ How, confess ! ” I said, “ when something un-
expected happens—say, bump your head on a
ridge-pole, stub your toe on the one stump in a
ten-mile stretch of three-chain road—do you never
curse ? ”
“ Never, sir—never! I laugh instead.”
Certainly there was method in this lunacy, but
I suspected hypocrisy. I awaited the progress of
the bull-ant. He had stopped half-way up to the
sock-ledge overhanging the brown blucher, and
had assumed an interrogatory attitude as of a
soul questioning the Unknown on the threshold.
“ And where—where do you find the element
of humor in the business ? ”
The Philosopher stretched out both legs, assum-
ing a half-lying, half-sitting position. The
trouser-leg moved downwards. The bull-ant was
ushered into the Unknown.
“ I look at it, sir, as I think God looks at it.
Don’t you think, when He sees you dancin’ round
like a damned sperrit just because of yer own
carelessness, He must laugh ? ”
The view was new.
“ Do you ever pray ? ”
“ Never. Think you He’d stop His machinery
because a fly had got under the belting ? ”
“ You look on the firm of Heaven and Co. as a
crowd of practical jokers ? ”
“ That’s about it.”
“Laughing at Humanity’s pain.”
“ Yes, the pain is there—there’s more of it than
anything else in the world. And what’s it for ?
It amuses somebody ! ”
“You suspect God ? ”
“ He’s the cause—object not stated.”
I was killing time. I judged it was up to the
ant by this time to adduce his little argument.
“ Yours is a cheerful philosophy, my friend,” I
said, rising and sweeping the dead leaves and dust
off my pants.
“ Yes ” (he rose). “I’m always happy—always
contented. Nothing ever ruffles me ! —nothing
can disturb—oh, hell 1 Dash and blank and
blast 1 What the ”
I threw myself on the dust again and laughed.
The day had grown cooler, and I fancied I could
hear the laughter of the hosts. A crushed bull-
ant and a crumpled Philosopher 1 I understood
God.
Vio.
L. M. D.
The Arbitrator,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The cutter lay anchored under the lee of a reel
Johnson, lying down smoking in the little cabin,
sprang suddenly to his feet and on deck. “ Yot
black hound!” he cried, “I’ll teach you to fool
alonga that fella, Kitty,” and drawing his re-
volver he at Moonlight. He missed, thej
clinched, and, struggling, fell overboard. Rising
to the surface, a momentary pause for breath
then a fin, a swish, a gurgling cry, and a crimson
stain in the water. Moonlight, climbing on board
again, said, “ Now, Kitty, you b’elonga me ! ”

PASCOE.
It is on record that G. H. Reid once pub-
lished a volume of alleged poems, but The Bulle-
tin has never been able to secure a copy. Here,
however, is a set of verses from the pen of the
portly prophet of Yes-No
AN EVENTFUL DAY IN THE HISTORY OF
NEW SOUTH WALES.
19th April, 1770.
Along the virgin coast primeval silence reigns,
Save when the stubborn rock the dashing wave disdains
While wrapped in sloth secure the native savage lies,
Content to live and die beneath his native skies.
Twas naught to him that strife immense, on sea and
land,
Blazed in the north from prairie waste to coral strand ;
That in the East a Clive had more than stood his ground;
That in the West a Wolfe had grave and glory found ;
Or that a Walpole’s tact, and freedom from display,
Had nerved the British race for Pitt’s majestic day !
Nor did he dream that any such advancing power
Gould cast its fatal shadow o’er his humble dow’r.
Alas ! the might that thundered on those distant seas
Gould even penetrate such unknown seas as these!
For, as his looks roam round the unfrequented verge
VYhere Heav’n appears to rest upon the lonely surge
And which for cycles past his simple sires had scanned
And saw no danger rise to vex their native land—
He starts! affrighted, and beholds a monstrous thino-
''bring* 1 knows ’ 00 joy ’ or friends > or peace can
What mad’ning fears possess his soul as next he sees.
The horror grows with each auspicious breeze !
Ah ! could he realise the fate that nears the shore,
How would his barbarous heart the awful day deplore 1
How different are the joys which fire the noble Cook,
How thankful in his heart, how happy does he look 1
The land ahead ”is proudly watched bv all on board.
Who ve won more lasting fame than any of the sword ;
The course they steered was all unknown, the stars un
known ;
On her broad beam their lusty barque was often thrown.
But, fearless all, and true as steel, they never swerved
From faith in.the great British sailor whom they served.
As up the coast the staunch Endeavour bravely stands
Each day confirms the growing’ promise of these lands ;
And when their country’s flag's unfurl'd upon the strand
Eaeh valiant heart beats high amorist the patriot band.
February 29, 1879.
G. H. REID.

Who Says So?
Tt matters a lot whose word you have for a statement, doesn’t it?
The story about a man being cured of some trouble away up in Queensland or down in
Victoria may be so, but you’ve no way of proving it.
But when we tell you about Sydney people—
People who live right here, and whose statement you can prove in an hour any day—
Well, when we tell you about these people being cured of kidney troubles of every sort
by that little Conqueror of Kidney ills,
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills,
You haven’t much excuse for not believing it, have you ?
And that’s the proof we give you. f ydney proof. The word of your neighbors.
They’ve suffered—They’ve taken Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills—and now they’re glad of
the opportunity to tell others about it.
We select three from the hundreds of Sydney statements at hand :
Mr. Charles Alexander, 143 Riley-street, Woollooinooloo, says:—
“ While working I ani obliged to lift heavy articles, and this, I believe,
affected my kidneys, as about two years ago I began to feel severe pains in
my back. Many widely-advertised medicines were tried without gaining
any advantage. Indeed, I seemed to be getting worse, when an attack of
influenza left my kidneys in such a state that I could scarcely bear up
against the pains. The doctor to whom I went treated me for Bright’s
Disease, and his treatment did me a certain amount of good, but I still had
a racking pain across the kidneys and up between the shoulders. I could
not turn in bed and my baok felt as if someone were trying to cut out the
backbone. At last I was wholly confined to my bed, when a friend came
to see me and remarked that Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills had relieved
him wonderfully. So some were obtained for me at Newman’s Pharmacy,
King Street, and I set about taking them. They gradually improved me
and when I had used them all was entirely well again. The Pills have also
cured one of my children who was afflicted with urinary trouble.”
The above is what Mr. Alexander said one year ago. He repeats his
testimony to-day, and says :—“ Since I gave this testimonial twelve
months ago I have had no reason to change my opinion. I still believe
that they are the best pills ever brought into Australia. I have tried
medicines which have been extensively advertised, and also drank stuff
prescribed by doctors, but I would never fall back on any of them, as none
of them gave me satisfaction for my money, but Doan’s Backache Kidney
Pills were cheap at ten times the price. Some of the kidney medicines I
have taken were, like swallowing knives to out away the disease, but Doan’s
Backache Kidney Pills were mild and thoroughly effective.” Mr.
Alexander works at the Lending Library in Queen Victoria Markets.
Mr. Martin Nicholas, whose city address is No. 16, Elizabeth-street,
says:—“A year ago I gave my testimony as to the wonderful benefits
derived from Doan’s Backache Kidney Fills. I had been a great sufferer
from kidney trouble for a long time. My back ached so terribly at night
that 1 could scarcely sleep, and was so sore that I could hardly turn over or
get out of bed. The disease, or whatever it was, was getting a firmer hold
on me, and at one time I was laid up in bed for three whole weeks. The
doctor advised an operation, but I would not consent to this. I finally got
out again, but I was a constant sufferer. At times the pain in my back
was so severe that I could hardly stand up. I had tried doctors and
numerous remedies, but not until I had taken Doan’s Backache Kidney
Pills did I get relief. I purchased these pills at Newman’s Pharmacy in
King-street. I continued the treatment until I was completely cured. It
is nearly a year ago since I discontinued using Doan’s Backache Kidney
Pills, but I have had no return of my old complaint, and this convinces me
more firmly than ever that these pills are a true specific for kidney troubles
and as such I will always recommend them to my friends.”
Mr. J. Frodd, China and Glass Riveter, 99 Crown-street, says “I
suffered from the effects of kidney complaint for three years. The small
of my back ached very severely, and when I had occasion to stoop down I
could not straighten myself without great pain. I was also afflicted with
various urinary troubles. lam glad to say thatDoan’sßackacheKidney Pills,
which I obtained at Newman’s Pharmacy, King-street, have completely
oured me, and I can honestly recommend them to any person suffering
from kidney complaint.”
DOAN’S BACKACHE KIDNEY PILLS may be had of all Chemists and Storekeepers throughout the Colonies, price 2s. 9d. per box—six boxes, 15s. 3d.—or
will be posted on receipt of price by the proprietors, Foster McClellan Co., 76 Pitt-st., Sydney; or from the following branches, 292 Flinders-st., Melbourne ;54 King

Derelict.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I am the foolish artist,
Far-fallen from the height
Where winged-gifts up-bore me,
When life was young with light.
I am the foolish artist
(Drugged brain and rolling gait).
Men choke desire within them
And profit by my fate.
They stay me in the by-ways
(High morals make them bold),
I seem to drink their sermons;
I drink instead their gold.
But such as seal their purses,
The word-free, miser tribe,
I flay with whips of satire,
And stab with goodly jibe.
Along the hard, black pavement,
When lost to woe and weal,
The Loved-of-God lie dreaming—
I, God-abandoned, reel.
At times, when sleep eludes them,
They hear my footsteps roll—
“ What ails the foolish artist
That he drowns thus his soul! ”
“ What ails the foolish artist ? ”
The good wife turns to weep ;
“ Thank God, I’m not as he is ”
The good man falls asleep.
I knocked at the gate of Glory,
And straight it opened wide :
A whisper wooed me earthward,
E’er I had stood inside.
I knocked at the Heart of Woman,
Knocked with me boor and lout,
“ Come open, O Heart of Woman,
An artist stands without.”
But chill on my soul fell answer,
As though a cold wind blew—
“Go hence, you foolish artist,
Here is no room for you.”
Since then the foolish artist,
Long lost to dreams divine,
Hath ta’en the way of folly
And bowed his knee to wine.
Lights out! and the clock is striking :
Lights out! and out o’ luck :
Last stroke !—and the green tide gurgles ;
Lights out! . . . And the Hour has struck.
RODERIC QUIN.
A Brewer’s Story.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Ah!” said the bald-headed man, with a half-
dead smile, “ never run a brewery ! I had been
in the shipping trade, the newspaper business,
drapery, mining, the law, the stage, the church
but I found that I was a perfect baby when,
in my innocmce, I undertook to run a brewery.
Look how thin lam ! Yet they say that beer’s a
food and that when a man’s drinking he wants no
tucker!
“I began my management a youngish man and
a happy one. Every morning at nine I started
out in a buggy with both eyes keen for detail, and
a resolve set to win or die. And I ended ! Look
at my bald head !—and at my coats hanging
round the wall there ! —all smelling of patchouli
thrown over me by barmaids. Of course the
tied-houses accounted for much of my hair :
in my city 95 per cent, of the houses were
tied. But then that’s so the world over, and we
knew it when we started. King Beer in his
palace chuckled at our temerity—so tremendous
was the handicap against us. We worked, and
worked, and worked ; mined and countermined ;
lied and counter-lied. Ah ! the foxes. Nearly
every publican is a fox of foxes.
“ But the worst trouble—at any rate, to a
youngish manager, as I was, engaged to be mar-
ried —is the giving of presents. You see, the
publicans wouldn’t sell our liquor unless we regu-
larly made presents to their wives, and daughters,
and barmaids. It’s the way of the liquor-trade
everywhere. One big brewery in this city gave
away ,£20,000 last year in presents alone. And
that’s how I met my Waterloo.
“ My girl and her mother came to my office one
morning when I was out ; and waited. Both were
up-to-date, as women are nowadays and always
have been, and consequently when the ’phone
rang-up my girl answered it for me. The clerk
was away, and she did it out of kindness, as the
homely phrase is. ‘ Boss,’ came the thick voice
of a publican who did 15 years one time for
attempted murder, * a pair of fowls for my missus
on Sunday ! And, boss, can you get me a-bar-
maid—no matter how hot, so long as she don’t
steal ? ’ My girl noted down the request with
serious misgivings. Her mother, being absorbed
in a woman-suffrage treatise, did not notice, and,
therefore, did not question.
“ Ring ! went the ’phone again, and my girl
jumped up quickly, fearing another strange mes-
sage, and fearing equally that her mother might
get hold first. ‘Air ye there! blayguard?’
shrieked Mrs. O’Grady. ‘ Oi’m puttin’ me Sun-
day dress on to come down an’ tell yer board of
directhors about yer conduct! Ye’ll ’ave a showin’-
up, take my word for it.’ The vindictive scream
of Mrs. O’Grady pierced my girl to the core, and
she sat down near the ’phone and tried to reason
with her fears as she waited for the next discovery.
It came soon enough. ‘lt’s Lucy,’ laughed a
frisky voice; ‘What about the gloves, dear?’
* You won’t get them,’ my girl answered sharply.
Her mother looked up and as sharply asked,
‘What is it?’ ‘Nothing,’ answered the girl,
and tried her best to suppress the suspicion that
would rise against me. But the practised eye of
the match-maker perceived trouble, and from that
on she took hold of one receiver, and noted down
the memoranda in this form :
Flossie wants tan gloves : you know the size.
Susie wants a sable : you can put it on.
Mary wants a bracelet: you can put it on.
Emily wants perfume : wall-flower: ducky.
Nea wants a brooch : and three kisses : one on each cheek
and the third where you like.
Becky wants hair combs : nice ones.
Jenny wants silver hair-brush : love.
Polly wants silver-handled parasol—for you and me.
Mrs. O’Smyth wants dress-ring.
Jennv wants opera-cloak—£4 one will do.
Mrs. Dobbs wants tailor-made gown.
Mrs. Dooley wants petticoat— laoe one—has stopped it
out of account.
My girl’s mother wrote down this list with the
satisfied relish with which old ladies discover
anything against men. And at intervals she pro-
nounced me ‘a brute, as all men are.’ My girl
did not answer; her heart was checked and sad,
and longing for my return. When I did arrive
my intended mother-in-law tackled me hard.
‘ls this ’ —pointing scornfully at the list —‘ is this
the Shah of Persia’s harem ? ’
“ I told the truth about it. Explained that the
traveller, in going round that morning, had made
all sorts of promises in the hope of pushing trade.
My girl’s mother pierced me with a glance of keenest
irony. ‘Oh ! ’ she said, ‘ indeed ! Of course it is
the traveller. A man always blames somebody
else. A man never pleads guilty.’ And, turning
instantly to her daughter, the match-maker in her
became merged in the woman-suffragist, and she
held forth in hysterical tones, ‘M.y dear Mab,
forewarned is forearmed ! Unless you can become
the only wife, never marry. It is iniquitous, but
it is true. Marriage means that you, unfortun-
ately, will be one of a number. And, unlike
those of the number who have their liberty and the
right to speak their mind, you will be the poor
wretch under a man’s great, cruel thumb. If his
Susie or his Flossie or his Becky or his Polly give
him offence he cannot harm them—he can only
refuse presents. But as for the poor one whom he
has the legal right to call his wife—he can starve
her, beat her, keep her in rags. And what can
she do ? Weep her heart out, poor girl, or else
sue for separation or divorce. Which means pub-
licly exposing her misery to the mockery of a
callous world. The men, being-, all brutes, will
sympathise with him, while the women will laugh
and say, * The booby ! I knew she couldn’t hold
his love. Ah, my dear, a woman never realises
how down-trodden her sex is until she marries !
She is utterly dependent on one man, then. And
after years of ill-treatment at his hands, then, if
she does not feed and flatter the brute, he will
take all her pin-money and pay a cook with it.’
“ ‘O’ill ’av ’is heart!’ shrieked Mrs. o‘Grady
as that 2751b5. of Irish hot fired into the room.
‘ Now ! ’ she yelled, shaking her big red fist in
my face, ‘ it’s between ye an’ me. O’ill ’av six
pairs o’ silk stockin’s, open-wurrked, out o’ ye, or
by the saints uv heaven it’s to h—l wi’ ye an’ yer
board of directhors, an’ yer beer goes inty the
gutther ! ’
“Next moment I was alone with Mrs. O’Grady
to battle it out. I did that. But never was I
able to reconcile my girl to me. I tell myself it’s
for the best, but, all the same, I’m a bald-headed,
miserable brewer.”
S. A. MILLS.
Two Little Women,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“CtAd! that was a near thing the other night,
Little Woman ! How did you manage to explain
it?”
“ Yes, wasn’t it 1 He was positive he saw youi
arm around my waist—threatened divorce, shoot-
ing, and all sorts of things. However, even my
conscienoe didn’t make a fool of me. I declared,
in my most tragic manner—you know you often
tell me I ought to have been an actress —that if he
insulted me by even thinking me capable of such
things, I would refuse to live under the same roof
with him, and save him the trouble of turning me
out.”
“ And did that bring him round? ”
“Oh, yes! that and a few tears, and some
kisses. The kisses were the hardest part of all to
me.”
The man had sufficient grace to murmur
mentally, “ Poor chap ! ” Aloud, he said, “ Well,
you are a marvellous Little Woman ! ”
And their laughter was drowned in many kisses.
* * * *
In course of time the man married. He should
have known better, for he was no longer young.
Besides, he made the mistake which few men of
his sort make, and married a galloping girl. At
first, the possibility of his wife preferring others
never entered his head. But, in due time, the
übiquitous “ other chap ” appeared on the scene,
in the simple fashion peculiar to other chap--.
Then someone sounded a warning-note, and the
man understood.
Mad with rage, he confronted his young wife,
and hurled the ugly accusation. Trembling, but
game, she stood bravely to her guns, and worked
them to such purpose that soon the enemy’s fire
ceased. She had almost added one more triumph
to the countless brilliant achievements of woman
in this direction, when, betwixt kisses and tears,
she cried, “Oh, Jack! How could you think
such things of me? If you ever do so again, I
shall refuse to live.with ”
But here the tide of battle set against her, for,
from some remote chamber of memory, the man
heard a voice asking, “And did that bring him
round?” And a softer, brighter one, “Yes . . .
the kisses were the hardest part! ” And the echo
that had lived all these years woke the demon
within the man anew. Which was hard luck for
a plucky Little Woman.
Q.
